and improving ever since. The book is stated to

Errors of type and misprints abound. On p.
r f re ad first; p. xi., for Somoa read
Sanwa, and for Reponsibilities read Responsibili-
rl«d pom' nea r , ead near ’ P- 18, for Phillips’
read Phillips; p. 12, for La Perouse read La
Perouse; p. 26, for begun read began; p. 39, for

k spelied both Vlamingh (pp. 7, 10) and Vlaming
The book is illustrated by several maps in the
rext, and by some score beyond the text, litho-
graphed in colour. Of these latter, many are ill
conceived and ill plotted. Some skill is required
in drawuig and printing a map for reproduction
so that the relative importance of geographical
names shall be preserved, and the map shall not
oe obscure ; and this skill has been wanting to the
book under consideration. There is no list of
maps in the table of contents. The map which
faces the title is apparently designed to represent
Australia at the present day; but as there is no
explanatory title, and indeed no title at all, this
opnnon may be erroneous. There is a note
of reference to explorers’ tracks, but the tracks
are barely decipherable-which is possibly
rather an advantage than otherwise, seeing that
they are incomplete and inaccurate. The only
record of exploration in Westralia, for example, is
given by a track which represents a hitherto-
unknown journey made by Leichhardt from
Albany to the Swan river. It does not appear
that Australia has ever been traversed from east
to west. No railways are shown ; no overland
telegraph-line. Port Jackson i 3 named, but not
Port Phillip. Wollongong and Bowen are shown,
but not Toowoomba and Ballarat. In other ways
this map has curious interest for geographers.
Facing p. 1 hj a map of New South Wales, show-
ing railway lines. The trunk lines N. and S.
apparently end at Tenterfield and Albury respec-
tively. There is no railway communication
between Tamworth and Manilla, Byron Bay and
Lismore or Murwillumbah, Culcairn and Corowa,
Parkes and Forbes. The Clarence and Macleay
rivers are named, but not the Richmond. The
Clarence River branches in several new directions,
possibly following the coach-tracks. In other
particulars this map will repay careful study.
A map facing p. 72, and apparently designed to
show the extent of early coastal exploration of
Australia, has no title.
The maps of other States, and the map of
Maoriland, offer material for comment similar to
that offered by the map of N.S. Wales. In some
respects the most remarkable map in the book is
that of Queensland, here reduced in fac-simile
of line.
Observe that Cairns is omitted, and the fail-
ways from Cooktown to Laura, and Cairns to
luareeba, are joined in one remarkable line—“bv
authrjrity ” of N.S.W. Education Dept. ! Note
that the extension of the railway from Charleville
to Ounnamulla has been tacked on to the Central
hne, and goes south from Longreach, ending
somewhere near Adavale ! ! -Note all the other
points for comment !!!
Were the book otherwise perfect, it would be
condemned mechanically. It is printed on
glossy paper from light-face type ; and only bold-
tace type, and paper without glaring reflections,
should be used for school-books. And it is badly
printed. In the copy under notice the formes
have rarely • received sufficient ink, and the
impression is so unsatisfactory that many letters
do not print clearly {e.g., p. 238). As the book
stands, it is a menace to one’s eyesight ; and
the eyesight of our children is very premous. To

FLORENTINE PAINTING OF
THE FOURTEENTH
CENTURY
mHE art of the Trecento is overshadowed by the genius of
otto - , He had discovered a style so perfecUy moulded
to the ideas of his time and the genius of the Italian people,
that for nearly a hundred years artists were engrossed the
work of applying that style to the recital of all the legends of
Christum mythology. Thus it comes about that the art of this
po-iod has the effect of an anti-climax. It is tob like Giotto’s
art in its aim not to be instantly compared with that, and too
unhke m its accomplishment not to suffer by the comparison.
Adhmng strenuously to the high and heroic notion of art
which Giotto had established, it left no room for the .mf.Hin.
o slighter talents, for the indulgence of trivial but genuine
interests. With the comparative lowering of the imaginative
arms Of art m the fifteenth century, such minor painters
attained to their complete development; and we often pass by
a G.ottesque fresco in which the principles of the grandTtyle
are stuchously upheld, m order to admire the genial trifling of
some fifteenth century cassone painter who scarcely regarded
himself as more than a craftsman, but who had the good
fortune to be able to work unreservedly, on the level of hia
own temperament |lt was not always thus. When Italian art
" Studled more for Ch-stinn sentiment than for •>,
here is (refJuced) page of the Education
Dept. s _ h,story,” printed from light-face type,
upon bright-surfaced paper :
HISTORY OP AUSTHAMA.
ci *P- Id The Gold Era. [i Kl
remained triumphantly in possession of the fielt). Having-
thus taken a decisive step, they proceeded to organise them-
selves as a military force under chosen leaders, and, having
arms, erected a stockade and hoistod a flag representing the
Republic of \ ictoria, which was duly proclaimed within the
new entrenchment. The,force of soldiers on the field was
small, consisting of only about 200 men, while the insurgents
were numerous, although badly armed and without disci-
pline. The remainder of the regiment (the fl!)th) had been
hastily dispatched from Melbourne to reinforce tho troops
already on tho Beene, and the diggers, never dreaming of
their approach, betrayed the carelessness of undiaciplinod
men.
The officer in command of the troops at Ballarat.eager to
distinguish himself before the arrival of his superiors, seized
The Eureka the opportunity thus offered, and at day-
Stockade. break on the morning of Sunday, the 3rd of
December, surprised the “Eureka Stockade,” which, after
a short struggle, he took by storm. A good many insurgents
were cither killed or wouuded, and 125 taken prisoners.
Amongst the latter was Mr. Peter Lalor, who had boen the
most prominent of the leaders. Having, been accidentally
concealed by the falling timber of the stockade, Mr. Lalor
escaped to the ranges, where he obtained concealment.and
I medical attendance. A reward, of £5OO offered
capture hv the Government produced no result; and tho
ringleader of the Ballarat insurrection lived to preside for
many years as Speaker over the Victorian Assembly, al-
though with the loss of an arm to recall this etching event
in hia career
The essentials of comparison are maintained :
and it cannot be questioned which page is the
easier to read without fatiguing the.eyes. N.S.W
Education Dept-, in its hurry to issue its “ His-
tory, appears not to havq, considered the vital
point of legibility.
We might presume it is the foundation of the
Commonwealth that has brought the Education
Dept, to bed before due time. The foundation of
the Commonwealth sets indeed a convenient
period to early Australian history. This is the
fitting time to survey the record of tin? past, and
to place m the hands of Australian school-children
a, book which relates how the diverse paths of the
Australian colonies reached the high-road of
National Union. But, singularly enough, the
Education Dept, s history gives no such relation
It never reaches its natural close. It is published
m September, 1901, and it leaves the “History of
the Australian Colonies ” floundering in the dis-
-ISQS 6 °4 1899 J Th u e - refer to 1897 and
1898. The stage which the Federation movement
has reached is told on p. 188—thus:
of T ! BQa r , eq ?L 3ifce , pr ° visi u on /or the submission of the Bill
of 1899 to the electors having been made, in June it was
accepted by New South Wales, and shortly after by Vic-
toria, South Australia, Tasmania, and Queensland. The
Parliaments of these colonies have since sent addresses to
the Queen, pray mg that the Constitution may be passed
into law by the Imperial Parliament. p
That is as far as the native caution of N.S.W
Education Dept, will permit it to affirm, so
the reference to its reckless activity must be
taken relatively only. The Department’s warm
espousal of the tradition that Queen Anne
is dead cannot be attributed to mechanical
reasons, for there are half-a-dozen printing firms
m Sydney that would undertake to complete 5000
copies of the History ” m three months and the
resources of N.S.W. Govt. Printing Office4re
piobably greater than any two of those firms can
command. No - the Department’s ignorance of
events since 1899 is attributable purely to its
native caution. It distrusts the stability of
these new-fangled constitutions. So that the
Departmental miscarriage of history cannot
after all, be ascribed to any natural desire
to commemorate the foundation of the Common-
wealth ; and the unseemly haste in publication
remains entirely to be deplored.
It is doubtless the Department’s mad dream
that a school hi story-of 350 pages can be prepared
for issue m only eleven years that is responsible
a f , • ie a PP e arance of a historical monstrosity
U times even a sheep is dangerous; and when
Kea-lape grows revolutionary, anything is pos-

it is plain that too severe a strain has been placed
jy°“ t" 6 . compiler—“ Joseph Finney, B.A.
I raser Scholar), Lecturer, Public Training Col-
lege; Master of the Practising School, Fort-
street, Sydney.” In justice to Mr. Finney the
Department should have hastened slowly, mind-
ful that the greatest zeal is only creditable when
rightly directed. Caught in the furious whirlwind
of Departmental energy, Mr. Finney evidently has
been hurried along atapace which made proper con-
sideration of his work impossible. So the History
is not only feeble and inadequate as a whole ; but
it contains many pages written in awkward and
plebeian sty'e, many blunders of syntax,
and not a ew gross misprints. The task
°u * ood sh ° rt histor y of Australia that
.shall be at once correct and inspiring,—a river of
full and lucid statement that is never lost in the
desert of facts and dates, —is by no means an easy
one. Yet it is a task not beyond the powers of a
practised writer gifted with a clear head, a little
imagination, and the capacity of taking pains
The authorities are easily accessible, and easily to
be mastered. One would think that almost any
head-teacher employed by N.S.W. Education
Dept, should be able to survey the data and write
an accurate and adequate sketch of a history. To
write the History it is plain that one should
command an English style always elevated, clear,
any strong. By the insane haste of the Education
Uept. Mr. Finney has been spurred into a style
which is often no style at all—so mean, obscure,
and cumbersome does it become. The first 150
pages, or so, of the History are satisfactory, and
often good; but when the Department’s pace
begins to tell the language flounders all over the
course. Even the commended pages are some-
times faulty. Thus, on p. 43, we read of Capt.
King that
he was intelligent in his objects and persevering and
patient in their attainment.
Capt. King s intelligence could not be in objects
beyond it; it might be in his aims or in his choice
or objects. He could not be patient in the
attainment of objects, since it is meant that, his
patience preceded attainment—that he was patient
in the pursuit or prosecution of his objects, or
persevering to their attainment. ( Their is not
admirable). It is a pity that Departmental hurry
did not permit of relative words being intelligent
in their antecedents and persevering and patient
m their attainment : throughout the book
(e.g., p. 75; p. 122, 1. 10; p. 143, 1. 20; p. 174,
1. 7 ; p. ISO, 1. 22; et al.) the relative’s pursuit of
the antecedent would disappoint Capt. King.
And even when the antecedent is caught one does
not always get pleasure from the attainment.

Sufficient has been written to show how utterly
disgraceful is the book which N.S.W. Education
Dept, has authoritatively endorsed. The quota-
tions given are not exceptional; but typical.
ii n ij l ! £ oes without saying that no book
+ °Ji r 6 placed in the hands of children for
study which cannot bear the severest criticism in
pomtof accuracy of statement and of style, since
the children naturally follow the models which are
placed before them.
The History ” closes with an extraordinary
chapter entitled “ Citizenship—its Rights and
Responsibilities,” which begins by a reference to
the nine Australasian Colonies,” and proceeds to
discuss the laws of Hew South Wales as if they
had force in Australia and Maoriland. This
chapter is by no means faultless in statement and
expression ; but the limit, of criticism has been
reached. '
There is no, index to the “ History.”
Plainly, N.S.W. Department of Education has
been too rash. Its authoritative book will not be
fit for use in Australian schools until another
dozen years have elapsed. The copies of the issue
should be withdrawn from sale and burnt—with
the exception of one only, which should be pre-
served in the Departmental museum for the warn-
ing of impetuous officialdom, and for the wonder
of more enlightened times.

X.O. : Thus a Sydney artist who really sup
ports himself and family by his art: “Never
mind about the State fostering Art. Let it govern
the country so that the country will grow othe

V : ihe insignificance- of Henslowe’s Annua
is explained by the fact that many of the con
tnbutions are gift horses, and the editor has not
looked at their mouths. The editor, by the way,
is ati ingenuous English person who remarks in i
preliminary notice to contributors—
I do not wish to discourage would-be writers, who must
remember that in writing, as in alj else, success is only
attained by long- practice. With regard to artists, many
of the drawings submitted, showed much promise, yet
were not good enough for reproduction in the Annual.
Then you turn the with interest to find
that the editor’s own literary contributions are
uncommonly feeble, and that his artistic contri-
butions are feebler and imitated. His drawing
on p. 19 looks like a tracing of Mackennal’s
“ Circe,” ridiculously altered. On p. 53 there is
an impudent plagiary of the figure of Whistler
from p. xm of Phil May’s Sketch Book; and on
p, 31 appears an imitation of the figure of Oscar
Wilde, in the same drawing by Phi] May. Good-
natured tolerance of such a “magazine” is unfair
to all penmen and pencilmen who try to do
original work.
The Athenceum of Aug, 17 “notices ” Becke and
Lawson in three columns wherein disparagement is
varied by the old familiar references to Gordon,
Clarke, and Stevenson. The writer lays down the
law with all the superior superficiality of an Eng-
lishman who is thoroughly ignorant of what he is
talking about. Becke’s taste is corrected, and
Lawson’s language ; and some things are said that
are true—some things that were true once, or
true partly—and some things that are false. The
Athenceum is doubtless able to set Australian
authors right in relation to literature ; but when
it undertakes to set them right in relation to Aus-
tralia it ought to employ a reviewer who has some
better basis than the faded memory of chance
acquaintanceship. '
The remarkable correspondent, and the others—
Maskee : Shallow...Yesab ” asks where S. H. Sime’s
drawings are being printed now ? Outlaw : Basis
not broad enough for inference, obtainable books being
limited in class and number J.L. : Oan’t agree; L.
Mack’s verse touches us in the manner of those Turner
sunsets which J.B. dispraises. Turner was a kind of
spirit of mad God moving upon the face of the paints,
but out from his incoherent whirlwind comes at times
such a dazzling gleam of Power that (once seen) you bow
of course to his godlihead. De minor. : there is a similar
light behind Miss Mack’s tourbillon of language. Pray for
eyes M.L.: “George Bernard Barton” should have
been “George Burnett Barton.”

WARNER’S SAFE CURE
The remarkable power possessed by Warner’s Safe Cure in over -
coming all diseases of both the liver and kidneys is conceded by
scientists and specialists throughout the world.
WARNER’S SAFE CURE
Restoring to health and activity the kidneys and liver, necessarily
cures all disorders arising from the failure of those important organs
to cleanse the blood of urea, uric acid, and other poisonous impurities.
indigestion and Dyspepsia
Are cured by Warner’s Sa'e Cure, for the simple reason that good
digestion is assured when the liver and kidneys are acting, normally.
The digestive organs are then supplied with pure blood, which is in a
condition to absorb nutriment freely.
Biliousness and Headache
are cured by Warner’s Safe Cure. When the liver and kidneys are
properly performing their duties there is no such thing as biliousness,
no such thing as headache. All bile formed is duly delivered to the
intestines, and act as nature’s catharthic.
Rheumatism, Sciatica,
Lumbago, and Gout,
Are cured by Warner’s Safe Cure. These disorders all arise from
the presence in the blood of urinary and biliary poisons, due to the
defective action of the kidneys and liver. As soon as the kidneys and
liver are restored to health these poisons are expelled from the system
in a natural manner, and these diseases are consequently cured.
Neuralgia
is cured by Warner’s Safe Cure. Neuralgia is caused by poverty of
blood, and the excruciating pain felt is but the demand of the nerves
for richer blood. A healthy action of the kidneys and liver ensures
rich blood.
Anaemia and Debility
are cured by Warner’s Safe Cure. This medicine, causing as it does,
the liver and kidneys to act perfectly, every particle of food eaten goes
to replace nature’s waste and build up the constitution. A proper
and generous diet is all that is then necessary to make the weak strong
and the pale face flush with the bloom of health.
Blood Disorders
Are cured by Warner’s Safe Cure. Pure blood is incompatible with
boils, skin eruptions, etc. Warner’s Safe Cure, by inducing a perfect
action of the kidneys and liver, causes all impurities to be extracted
from the blood and to be expelled from the body.
Gravel and kindred Troubles
Are cured by Warner’s Safe Cure. The specific action of the medicine
upon the kidneys and other organs restores them to health ; the
accumulated acids are dissolved, and the troubles cease.
Bright’s Disease
Is cured by Warner’s Safe Cure, This remedy is the only medicine
known to science which will cure this dread disease.

of the world that it is now easy for anyone to make inquiry amongst personal

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Deserted Diggings: Maoriland.
The golden light is waning,
Falling through
The sombre mountain gap
On the Mother Forest rolling
Out of view :
The sleepy shadows gather
Low on her spacious lap.
The long grey flats are silent,
Lone and dun ;
About lie ruined huts,
Like dead men’s bones a-bleaching
In the sun;
Here runs an ancient truckway
Hid in its grass-grown ruts.
It leads into a tunnel
In the hill,
And glow-worms gleam aloof
Where once men’s voices echoed ;
All is still:
Like tears that follow laughter,
Drips water from the roof.
Near-by a rotten windlass,
Half o’ergrown,
Lies ambushed in the flax ;
A weka whistles shrilly
From a lone
Log, in some faded summer
Felled by the ringing axe.
A dam is over yonder,
Lying low
Behind its gravel banks
Piled up by human labour
Long ago ;
The gauge is leaning over
Its water-sodden planks.
Like lepers, sad, forsaken
Of their kind,
The pine-trees’ naked trunks
Arise, —and from the stagnant
Swamp behind,
Bereft and bare of branches,
Reach up their withered stumps.
O’ergrown with matted bushes
Is the race,
Close by a ruined flume,
Where black pipes hissed the water
On the “face,”
And swart and sweating sluicers
Drenched in the flying spume.
Here lies a rusty shovel:
All about
Old boxes, rotten planks,
Just as the owners left them—
“ Duffered out! ”
Oft have the blows of hammers
Rung on these rock-built banks.
Once, down this silent gully
Laughter woke
The echoes of the gorge,
And from these ruined chimneys
Issued smoke;
And loud the bellows panted
On yonder broken forge.
Oft round the camps at even
Tales were told,
And songs of many lands
Sung by the fire of rata;
Miners old
Joined in the “ lang syne ” chorus
And gripped each other’s hands.
Where are ye now, old comrades ?
Past alarms,
Past lust of gold or gilt /
The sinews of a nation
In your arms,
Out of your strength and folly
A Nation ye have built!
M.L.
LOLA.
Mummer’s Sick.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
'Ave yer fed them ’orses, Percy ?
Martha ! Jus’ go in ter “Mum.”
(Now then, Standon’t cry, my sonny !
Pupper’s goin’ ter give yer some.)
Perce ! you go an’ put the saddle
On the yaller mare ; be quick !
Ride across to Andy Hogan’s :
Tell yer auntie, Mummer’s sick ;
Leave the panel down. ’Ere, Stanley 1
Let the dawg alone, my son !
Come along and play with Pupper,
An’ ’e’ll let yer see ’is gun.
Is the kettle boilin’, Martha ?
Take yer Mummer in some tea*
Then you go an’ get yer supper.
No, yer needn’t wait fer me.
Pupper put ’is boy to bye-bye ?
Daddy undress little Stan ?
See if we can git ’is boots off 1
That’s ’is daddy’s little man !
Lay down, Patch ! (I’m cornin’, Mummer!)
Martha, fetch a bit more wood.
(All right, Mummer !) Good night, Stanley !
Go to bye-bye and be good !
’Ere’s a bite o’ somethin’, Mummer !
There now ! let me raise yer ’ead I
W’at was it the doctor told me ?
W’y, ’e said —’e said —’e said—— !
Oh, my Gawd t ’e couldn’t mean it /
Bess ! you ain’t agoin’ ter die ?
Bess, my darlin’ . . .
All right, Stannie!
Pupper’s cornin’! Don’t you cry.
S.A.
NIL.


A British Tommy, writing home from the
Ladyhrand distriot of S. Africa During the
last week we have killed several thousand sheep,
leaving them near farms to rot, to make every-
thing as uncomfortable as possible for the enemy.
By such severe means are tne Boers to be reasoned
into common-sense.” “ Reasoned ” is good.
The annual report of Factory and Shops
Inspector M‘Lay (Brisbane) was laid before
Queensland Parliament lately, the first report
under the new Act of 1900. Average wages for
Beveral trades were shown: Bread-makers, £119s.
Bid. and hours from 48 to 70 per week against
Victoria’s £2 8s. for 48 hours ; boot trade, males,
£1 7s. 64d.; females, 12s., against Victoria’s £1
14s. 5d. for males, and 14s. 7d. for females, &c.,
&c. The report concludes thus: —
The possibilities of future developments underlying
these figures open up a subject which is not within the
scope of this report to discuss, but it would appear cer-
tain that important changes are impending requiring
earnest scrutiny. Victoria will presumably very reluc-
tantly relinquish her minimum-wage. South Australia
has already adopted regulations for the appointment of
wages boards, and N.S.W. seems to be moving in the
same direction. Will Queensland be forced to follow
suit, or will Victoria have to recede from her present
standard ? It is, I think, obvious that, after the intro-
duction of inter-State Freetrade, the two scales cannot
continue to exist as they do now. If the statement of
Victoria’s Factory Department is to_ be accepted in full,
no one seems to be conscious of having greatly suffered
by having to pay more for the articles they require.

Parramatta is excited over a rumored pro-
posal to erect a big consumptives’ hospital at
Rydalmere. It can’t be too strongly insisted that
a retreat for the tuberculous should have at least
1000 acres of grounds, and be five miles from
everywhere.
"V errnont is one of the Prohibition States
of the U.S., and it is habitually held up to
admiration by the Cold Tea brigade as an earthly
Paradise, where all blessings are and whisky isn’t.
But, according to the Lancet, since Vermont
gave up liquor it has taken to opium instead, and
the quantity consumed is so large that it would
provide every adult with one ordinary dose daily.
There are plenty of non-users, of course, but then
the hardened cases who take the equivalent of
many ordinary doses per day compensate for them
and keep up the average. The opium habit has
grown up in a night; the teetotal brigade had
barely got back from chasing the drink fiend over
the State border, and admired itself in the glass
as the saviour and regenerator of its country,
before it found the new fiend in full possession.
It is a sorrowful fact that almost every country
which doesn’t take alcohol takes opium instead.
A country may pull through without much
alcohol, but if it takes none it is pretty safe to bet
that it takes something a great deal worse.
Vermont, as a whole, doesn’t mind swearing off
its old rye whisky now. Its opium-wreck rises up,
clawing the air, to assure the world that whisky is
a superfluous habit, and that it is all a mistake to
say that liquor is essential to anybody’s happiness.
“ Demon ” to The Bulletin :—
The strength of the detective force of the city of Mel-
bourne and suburbs up to Healesville, Dandenong, Epping
and Diamond Creek, is 18 men. Population in that area
nearly 500,000. There are 27 racecourses in and around
Melb. Two detectives have daily to be present at race
meetings where spielers are taking down spielers, thieves
robbing thieves. Flemington and Caulfield meetings
sometimes demand all the detectives available. Then,
again, it is a detective’s duty to watch gaming-houses,
suppress bookmaking and Chinese lotteries. A “ d.”
must report himself at the office in Russell-st. either
personally or by ’phone at 2 p.m. daily. Say, one is
making inquiry at Oakleigli. He is just getting on the
scent. Looks at his watch and finds it nearly 2 p.m.
Rushes then franticallj- to find a telephone, and tumbles
across one. Has to tell the shopkeeper or business man
he is a detective. From Russell-st. comes the inquiry
“ What are you doing there?” “Attending to Blanky’s
case,- fairly on the scent.” Answer from the Inspector—
“ Got something more important for you, come down at
once.” Now it so happens the owner of that tele-
phone is often friendly with the person the inquiries
are being made about and the cat is let out of the hag.
This is what the heads of the dept, calls detective work ! !
How can the men do their duty when hampered thus ?
How can they track criminals while they have to attend
race-meetings in the day and theatres at night ? How-
can 18 men keep under surveillance hundreds of clever,
unscrupulous criminals? The thing is impossible, and the
men have given it up in despair, and only do their duty
in a perfunctory way. The criminals know the routine
of the detectives, and they work smart schemes on the
strength of the circumstance. Rotten with tyranny and
parsimony, steeped in ignorance of the very rudiments of
how to detect crime, the Melbourne detective force —no,
farce —stands a disgrace and a monument to the incom-
petence of those responsible for it.

ANCHOR FENCE
THE ANCHOR PENCE has befen pronounced by the leading fence experts as the strongest fence on the market. It is made with plain
galvanised wire, and cannot injure any kind of live stock—nor can the live stock injure it or displace any of the wires owing to the use of the
Anchor Clamp. The Anchor Clamp securely fastens the horizontal and cross wires, thus consolidating the strength so that the whole fence
resists an attack on any part of it. As the strength of a chain is gauged by the strength of its weakest link, so a fence is only as strong as its
weakest part—the Anchor Fence is equally strong throughout, and being constructed wholly of large wires is much stronger than a fence in
which small wires are used.

expressly reserved by the publishers.

whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in length,
or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters, or Newspaper clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that we
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient

will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-

for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 5, 1901.
The N.S.W. Budget.
It is a specially cold day in New South
Wales when, after the appearance of the
Treasuier’s financial statement, the Sydney
dailies do not issue a leading article apiece
about the “ gratifying progress of this great
country under a sound and judicious policy
of expansion.” So long as there is no strong
party issue involved—and party politics are
practically dead in the N.S.W. Legislature
since the fiscal question was removed to a
higher place—almost any kind of financial
statement is accepted comfortably as a proof
that “we” are a great and progressive
people and the salt of the earth gener-
ally. On this point the unanimity of the
press is vast and wonderful, and it is
a unanimity which saves much trouble,
and is pleasing to the public vanity.
It is also remarkably like the mental attitude
of the American of many years ago as carica-
tured by Dickens in “ Martin Chuzzlewit ”
—the alleged American who, knowing him-
self to be the admiration of the universe,
put his feet up on a high place and expec-
torated at Creation generally.
* * * *
In any country which troubled much about
its future solvency, Treasurer Wapdell's
financial statement would have roused a
general execration sufficient to blow that
painfully ordinary gentleman off his official
pedestal. Last year closed with a huge
deficiency of about £150,000 —the fourteenth
deficit which N.S.W. has had in 21 years.
This was despite the fact that £1,241,917 of
the proceeds of land sales and £126,000 of
other people’s money that will have to be
refunded—the rents derived from Sydney
properties resumed but not paid for—was
treated as revenue, and that £081,786 of
ordinary, unproductive expenditure was
paid out of loans. These are the Treasurer’s
own figures, so presumably he has reckoned
everything as productive expenditure which
bore even the slightest resemblance that
way. The £961,786 went iii bridges, dred-
ging work, schools, museums, colleges,
gaols, court-houses, lockups, fortifications,
and all manner of sundries, including that
good old item, the “reserve stock of
ammunition.” Almost every year the “re-
serve stock of ammunition ” is bought out of
loans ; then it is shot away (it has to be, for
ammunition doesn’t keep in a reliable state
for ever), and a fresh “reserve stock” is
bought out of the loan funds, and it is also
shot away when it ceases to be “ reserve,”
and a further lot, similarly purchased, takes
its place. The amount of loan money paid
away last year to cover ordinary expendi-
ture was the largest that even N.S.W.
has ever got through in 12 months.
And Treasurer Waddell says that that
old, enduring spectre, the Accumulated
Deficit, has now been reduced to £2,785,000.
Considering that Coghlan, on page 794 of
his “ Seven Colonies,” estimated the deficit
a year ago as being down to £2,027,584, this
looks rather like an increase. It is certainly
a slight-—a very slight— apparent reduction
on the figures for 1894, but even that reduc-
tion is only apparent. The Cash Basis
swindle has arrived since 1894, and now
there is the great mass of unpaid accounts
to be reckoned with as well as the visible
deficit.
• * * f
For the new financial year Treasurer
Waddell reckons that—after handing over
the postal and telegraph revenue and a part
of the customs and excise receipts to the
Commonwealth, and with the abolition
of inter-State customs close at hand—
he will collect £11,004,076—0ver £200,000
more than the State ever collected when
it had its postal and telegraph and
all its tariff revenue for its own exclu-
sive use. And he expects to swell up
the expenditure to £10,980,660, which is
more than N.S.W. ever expended, even
when £750,000 a year of postal and tele-
graphic expenditure, now paid by the Com-
monwealth, was a charge on the State
Treasury. This leaves a poor little prospec-
tive surplus of £23,416. The N.S.W.
Treasurer assumes that he will receive
£200,000 more from the railways than he did
last year. And he takes it for granted that,
despite the abolition of inter-State duties, and
despite the fact that he can only reckon upon
receiving three-fourths of what remains of
the tariff revenue after the inter-State duties
are abolished (the Federal Government being
entitled to the other fourth) he will get
£2,200,000 from this source, which is more
than N.S.W. has received for many years
from the whole of its customs and excise,
with border duties included. In other words
he expects to get back £1 12s. 3d. per
inhabitant, and neither the Victorian nor
S.A. tariff, with the inter-State duties
abolished and a portion of the revenue re-
tained by the Federal Government, would
yield anything dimly resembling that. In
addition to this he proposes to repeal, or
suspend, Lyne’s Act (which never really
came in force), whereby the State is pledged
to make an extra annual paymentin reduction
of the old deficit; but as that Act was probably
never meant to come into force this is only
what might be expected. He also abandons
the promise to put the bankrupt Civil
Service Superannuation Fund on a
sound footing; the fund is simply
to be permitted to exhaust itself, and
then, about two years hence, it is under-
stood that the pensions will become a charge
on the revenue. Nothing is to be done in
the meantime towards meeting the liability.
And the old process of using loan money as
ordinary revenue is to go on in the same old
way.
* • * *
The Cornstalk man of finance also served
jup again the old, comforting lie about how
the N.S.W. railways were returning a large
profit. The same lie has been served up
every year for a long and weary time, and
year the Auditor-General goes through
the accounts and shows a heavy loss. Up to
June, 1900, which is as far as the Auditor-
General’s published figures go, the accumu-
lated loss was £7,983,311, and for the
year ended June, 1900, there was a deficit of
£138,317 —the worst since 1896. The
Treasurer reckons that this unpayable rail-
way system, which cost about £42,000,000
to build, would sell for £54,000,000 to a
foreign shindykit, and is of opinion that this
alleged fact fully justifies N.S. W. in having
a public debt, which stood at nearly
£71,000,000 in June last, and is now over
£75,000,000. As the foreign shindykit
usually wants from 5 to 10 per cent, for its
money, instead of 2| or 3 per cent., the
probabilities are eminently unfavorable.
The same authority calculates that the un-
sold public lands are worth £75,000,000.
This may be so, but it is difficult to see what
it has to do with the case. It is impossible
to sell all N.S.W. bodily to a sliindykit in
order to pay off the public debt. And
N.S.W. lives on this land—over £1,200,000
of its income is derived from land sales.
If there is still £75,000,000 worth to be sold
before this source of revenue gives out and
the State is left on its uppers, the country
has at least the satisfaction of knowing that
it is getting through this asset as fast as can
reasonably be expected.
* * * *
So the view which the See Government’s
Treasurer takes as regards the future is
that New South Wales will just about pull
through—
(1) If it uses about £900,000 a year of loan money as
ordinary revenue, in the same way as heretofore.
(2; If it goes on raising about £2,100,000 a year land
revenue as at present, and uses it all for current expenses.
(3) If it keeps on borrowing in all about £3,000,000 a
year, as at present, to keep the boom afloat.
(4) If it leaves the question of making good the Civil
Service Superannuation Fund shortage to some future
day.
(5) If it repeals or suspends the law requiring an extra
£250,000 a year to be paid off the old deficit.
(6) If there is a considerable railway boom.
(7) And if the Federal Parliament imposes a heavier
tariff than that now existing in Victoria, and a far heavier
one than that now existing in South Australia.
If all these things happen it is reckoned that
N.S.W. will just get through and have a
margin of £23,000. A great change has
come over the spirit of the financial dream
within the last year or two. The passing of
the Old Age Pension Act, without any
corresponding provision for finding the
money, was partly responsible for this. The
great increase in the railway deficit in 1900,
and in the annual interest bill (which is now
£2,542,000) is also partly responsible.
Nothing is heard now of the gentlemen
who used to write wildly to the papers
proposing that the huge surplus that
was to accrue to N.S.W. under the
Federal tariff should be used to reduce rail-
way fares and freights by 50 per cent. The
Foreign-trade push still talks of a moderate
revenue tariff, but N.S.W., under its
Foreign-trade policy, has now got to a point
where, unless there is a great increase of
direct taxation, it requires a larger tariff
than either Victoria or South Australia—
possibly a larger one than Tasmania—to
enable it to keep up even a bad semblance
of paying its way. When allowance is made
for the loss of duties on inter-State imports,
and for the fact that heavy duties will
materially reduce foreign imports, and,
further, for the fact that a part of the
Customs revenue will necessarily be retained
by the Commonwealth Government, it will
require an all-round 20 per cent, or, pos-
sibly, 25 per cent, tariff to realise the
N.S.W. Treasurer’s estimates. And even
they are based on the assumption that about
£900,000 a year of loans will still be used
for revenue purposes ; if that practice was
abolished a 30 per cent, tariff would hardly
fill the gap. There are some interesting pro-
blems ahead in N.S.W. But the See Govern-
ment is in a unique position which almost
places it beyond criticism. It is a personally
Protectionist Ministry, which inherited and
maintained a Foreign-trade policy. The
importing press has to allege that N.S. W. is
gloriously prosperous because a Foreign-
trade policy is in force, and the Protec-
tionist press feels bound to believe that the
State is in a state of unparalleled prosperity
because it couldn’t be otherwise with the
old Protectionist hacks in office. So the
Waddell Budget has been received with an
enthusiasm which that shocking document
has done nothing to deserve.
The Bread of Death.
N.S. Wales has a Public Health Act, and a
Public Health Board, which is supposed to have
full power to protect the people against unwhole-
some food, and preventible infection. How
thoroughly this Board has carried out its duties
the records of the baking trade during the past
three years will show. Bread is the pivotal point
of our food supply, its use is so universal that in the
language it is the type-word for nutriment. Any-
thing tending to taint the bread of the community
is as dangerous to life, therefore, as the infection
of the wells and springs which supply water. Yet
during its existence N.S.W. Board of Health has
taken not a single step towards the protection of
the public from dirty bread, and the State’s
main article of diet has been, in many cases,
produced under conditions of revolting filth, and
of sweating, dangerous not only to the workers in
the trade but to the whole public. The direct
result of ill-ventilated, sewer-infected, filth-
begrimed bakehouses and over-long hours of
labor, was that the journeymen bakers, as a class,
were ravaged by consumption, and the public was
infected with heaven-alone-knows-what amount
of poison with its daily bread of death. *
* • * «
There is no reason why, with proper precautions,
the baker’s trade should not be a moderately
healthy one. The time of labor is at night, and
the bakehouse is necessarily hot and, to an extent,
steamy. These conditions are unavoidable. But
with short hours of labor, with provision of a
proper interval for a midnight meal, and
with scientific ventilation and perfect cleanli-
ness in the bakehouse, a trade could be
made endurable which was, a year or two ago,
absolutely deathly, and which, in many cases,
is still just within the bounds of human tolera-
tion. During the past few months the Sydney
trades-union of journeymen bakers has, by a
thorough organisation of the workers and by wise
co-operation with the employers, put an end to
many of the conditions which made the baking
trade a menace to those engaged in it and to the
public generally. The conditions before, in many
instances, were simply loathsome. In underground
bakeries, ill-ventilated, ill-drained, infested with
vermin, contaminated by sewer-gas or the reek
from lavatories, workmen were employed from
9 to 10 and even 12 hours of the night; some fall-
ing victims to consumption addea a new poison
to the already deadly atmosphere.
• * * *
The prevalence of consumption and other chest
diseases among bakers has long been a familiar
fact to medical men. Phthisis, pneumonia, sciatica,
rheumatism are the chief banes of the journeyman
baker, all four telling of unhealthy conditions of
labor, the two former more particularly so. In
connection with the bakers’ trades-union there is
a friendly society with a “burial benefit.” Of
the three last deaths recorded, two were from
phthisis and one from pneumonia. As the condi-
tions of labor have, within the past three months,
been considerably ameliorated, it may be expected
that the ravages of phthisis will be, to some extent,
checked, but there has been established a
dangerous nucleus of infection to the community
which will exist for years to come if remedial
measures are not taken.
* * * *
The work done by the Bakers’ Society, in
conjunction with the Master Bakers’ Union, during
the past two months may be summarised:
Sweating has been put down, and eight hours
established as the duration of a night’s work;
unwholesome and insanitary bakeries have been
put in repair or are now being attended to ; the
lack of accommodation in many bakeries (leading
to dirty methods of work) has been or is being
remedied; underselling (“ cheap ” bread was the
cause of most of the sweating) has been stopped.
There still exist a few sweating-shops—under a
dozen all told—in the metropolitan area, but the
great majority now work under the agreement
come to by the master bakers and their workmen*
* _♦ • *
But what the to note is this: The
fairly sanitary conditions in the trade now
existing have been won by the efforts of the work*
men almost solely. Whilst some employers have
always sought to observe wholesome condi-
tions, many others had to be forced at the
point of the strike manifesto to clean, and
drain, and ventilate their shops, and to allow
to their men hours of labor which make it
just possible for them to keep clean and healthy.
The workmen, assisted by the fair employers, have
been the reformers. They have had absolutely no
assistance from the Public Health Board, or from
all the Health and Factory legislation, of which
so much was expected. And it is a very grave
question whether the burden of observing healthy
conditions of labor in this vital trade should be
allowed to remain upon one little trades union,
which may, or may not, be equal to the task in
the future. It is also open to serious argument
whether eight-hours’ night-work in a hot, steam-
ing atmosphere, without any proper interval for a
meal, is not too much.
• * * *
Finally, the existence of the consumptive work-
men in the trade is a problem of great moment.
It is, naturally enough, not the desire of those
connected with the workmen’s organisation to
disclose the medical history of members But
what facts can be gleaned show that phthisis is
extraordinarily rife amongst bakers, and every
phthisical workman is a source of grave danger
to the community. The responsibility for the
illness of these men rests upon the Government
of the State, the Government which, with ample
powers, in esse and in posse, to prevent it, allowed
these unfortunate fellows to be stifled, and
sweated, and poisoned in deadly bake-houses. If
the burden of the comfortable maintenance of
these men were taken over by the State, as the
price of their abandoning their trade, the bargain
would be' strictly equitable, and certainly a good
one for the community. It seems, indeed, one of
the necessary steps in the fight against phthisis.
But what is still more vitally important is that
the conditions which lead to the ravages of lung
diseases among bakers should be effectually and
permanently remedied, by strict Government
regulation and supervision of the conditions and
hours of labor. The baking of bread would prob-
ably kill tubercle germs existing in the dough
when put into the oven—though a few might pos-
sibly survive in the centre of a lightly-cooked loaf.
It is the subsequent handling of the bread, and its
dusting with unbaked flour, which constitute the
chief danger when a bakehouse is infected with
consumption. At present, undoubtedly, the staff
of life is helping many on to the road to death.
More White Australia.
Last week the Federal Government informed the
House , of Representatives, in the plainest possible
on behalf of the British Government, has inti-
mated that any exclusion measure which alludes
definitely to the color or birthplace of the ex-
°luded nigger will be vetoed by the British
Government. 1 his is the language of Joseph :
c In th w fi ! st d l }? recently-vetoed Queensland
Sugar Works Bill is here alluded to) embodies a disquali-
fication based on a place of origin that is practically adis-
tinction of race and color. Any attempt to impose dis-
qualifications on the base of such distinctions, besides
being offensive to a friendly Power, is contrary to the
& eneral conceptions of equality which have been the
g }“ ding pnnciple of Bntish rule throughout the Em-
Disqualification by educational tests such as are
embodied in the immigration laws of the various colonies
is not a measure to which the Government of Japan, or
any other Government, can take exception on behalf of
ks objects, and if the particular tests in these laws are
not regarded as sufficiently stringent, THERE IS NO
reason WHY MORE STRINGENT AND EFFECTIVE
ONES OF A SIMILAR CHARACTER SHOULD NOT
BE ADOPTED.
The explanation is reasonably plain, and wonder-
fuiiy like Chamberlain. The Judas of Bir-
mingham has been the boss liar of British politics
for many years, and though he is a very unc-ori-
yincing liar, still he tells his tale with an
assurance that carries weight. The British
Government goes almost unanimously on the
principle of denying its colored subjects all self-
governing rights whatever—it draws the biggest
color-line in the world and shuts out nine-tenths
of the empire from all share in the empire’s
management. But, all the same, it maintains the
silly and childish hypocrisy of being an empire
where black men and white men are equal—
and it doesn’t mind if they are made unequal
surreptitiously, so long as a sham appearance
of equality is maintained. And, according to
its new policy of humble subserviency to Japan,
it desires that the Jap., though not a member
of the empire, shall not be kept out openly,
but doesn’t in the least object if the same result is
arrived at sideways, in an accidental and apolo-
getic sort of fashion. Chamberlain’s instructions
are not to walk straight at the purpose desired,
but to get there on all-fours without attracting
attention,
But Chamberlain is reasonably explicit in
stating that the educational test may be worked
for all it is worth, and if it is worked for all it is
worth then it will keep out anybody and every-
body that the Federal Government desires to
exclude. In this respect, undoubtedly, the best
test is one by which the immigrant may be called
upon to show his ability to write out a dictated
passage in “some European language”— not the
one which requires him to show his ability to
write a passage in English. For the clause which
makes “ some European language ” the test does
not specify that the immigrant can choose his
own language. It is a clause under which the
anglicised Jap. might be called upon to show a
good knowledge of Spanish at a moment’s notice,
or a specially objectionable-looking Greek, or
Turk, or Pole might be examined in an erudite
and complicated Scandinavian author. But either
clause, if applied with discrimination and enthu-
siasm, would close out everybody that Australia
seriously desired to exclude,
* * * •
And the question for Australia to consider is
this: Is there any real reason why Australia
should go out of its way to make Joseph
Chamberlain and co. less crooked than they
naturally are ? The educational test is a hypocrisy,
but it isn’t our hypocrisy Apparently we can get
that kind of nigger exclusion at once. The straight
and honest kind may be obtained after a year’s
haggling, or ten years’ haggling, or it may not be
obtained at all so long as the Imperial connection
lasts. If Australia demands straight-out exclusion


and will take no other kind, and the British
Government will only assent to the education
test, then either the nigger must stream in un-
checked, or else this country must “cut the
Eainter” and set up for itself. Possibly it would
ave to fight the British empire for its indepen-
dence ; almost certainly it would have to fight the
arrogant brown man of Japan on the immigration
question. And the Australian people have shown
no general willingness to fight over the mere form
of exclusion. And little draper M‘Millvn, who
has just taken an Imperial title, and who talked
so glibly about cutting loose from the Empire over
the matter, would be recalled to England by his boss
softgoods firm when the trouble began and trade
was stopped, so that his services to whoop in the
front rank of dreadful war would not be available.
And the Barton Government would almost cer-
tainly lose its billet over the matter, and be suc-
ceeded by George Reid, who has already crawled
down over the same question.
* • * •
The great trouble is that Australia has already
accepted the educational test, and found it to
work well if honestly—that is to say, dishonestly
—applied, and the fact that it is a mere subter-
fuge hasn’t troubled this country in the least.
When George Reid, who is so honest and valiant
when he wants to get another man’s billet and
salary, and so abject when he is afraid of losing
his own job and salary—allowed his own straight-
out Nigger Exclusion Act to be vetoed in 1896,
and didn’t even make a protest, but agreed to
accept the educational test in a humble and con-
trite spirit, he set up a precedent which it is now
difficult to get rid of. And the Labor party (in-
cluding Mr. Watson, M.H.R., who now demands
that the Asiatic shall be shut out in the
most definite fashion) stood by and let
things go. That George Reid should froth
wildly because the Federal Government
does as he did is merely what might be expected
of that unscrupulous billet-hunter and insatiable
Yes-No. What the Barton Government has to
consider is that Australia wants any efficient
kind of exclusion that it can get at once;
that the educational test can be made
efficient; and that, after Australia’s peaceful
acceptance of the educational test at George
Reid’s instance, there doesn’t seem to be
enough abstract conscience in the country to make
a great fight over the mere form of the thing.
Also, it has to consider the fact that the men who
are demanding straight-out exclusion are, in many
cases, nigger-labor politicians, who only desire to
get a clause inserted in the Bill which will ensure
its rejection. And, lastly, Australia can fight for
direct exclusion (and be determined to insist when
necessary—even by force of arms—on direct
exclusion) after it has got its educational test just
as well as now, and The Bulletin hopes to see it
do so. Then it will have the blessed advantage of
knowing that the Piebald One is not streaming in
all the time the long fight is going on.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Bishop and His Brougham.
The Bulletin has received the following polite
letter from the Bishop of London :
Fulham Palace, London,
Aug. 20, 1901.
Sir, —An interesting account has been forwarded to me
from many correspondents, cut out from your paper,
purporting to be the description of a meeting held in
London in the cause of Public Morality.
I rearret to say that you, or your London correspon-
dent, have been the victim of a hoax. A skit appeared
in some serio-comic, paper in London, which was appa-
rently meant as a joke, but the paper was so little known
that we decided to take no notice of it.
It is a different thing now that it has been sent to what
I understand is a widely-read journal in Australia as a
serious account of the meeting, and I shall be obliged if
you will insert this denial of the truth of the whole
account.
As the Daily Chronicle said on Monday : “ It is hardly
credible that any intelligent human being should believe
what was printed then of the story, but if there be any
such, we have the Bishop’s authority for stating that it is
a pure fabrication, to say nothing of the evidence of our
reporter, who was present at the meeting.”
Youri fWVull'’,
A. F. London.
The Bulletin is quite satisfied with the Bishop’s
word without any corroboration from the Daily
Chronicle reporter, or from the argument as to
human intelligence. At this end of the world
there is no opportunity of studying the O.E.
Bishop in his solid silver state (a colonial bishop is
notoriously only electro-plate), and we were, there-
fore, misled into accepting as truth what was
only intended as a too ingenious satire on a
Bishop’s brougham. Our readers will now under-
stand that the statements (B. July 6) headed
“ The Bishop and the Prostitute ” —which
reached us as a newspaper-cutting purporting to
circumstantially report a London meeting—are
not facts, and sympathise with our regret at any
annoyance to which Dr. Ingram has been sub-
jected. One moral of the incident is that no
paper is so little known that it can be ignored
with impunity.

The Slaughter of the Children.
Of 107,425 white refugees detained in cencentration-
camps during the month of August, 1878 died, including
510 out of 22,036 children, in the Orange River Colony.—
Cablegram last week.
This much is certain —that so long as the enemy know
that their wives and children will be supported in, to
them, unaccustomed luxury and idleness, at the expense
of the British taxpayer, just so long will S’Africa be
harassed by roving bands of swiftly-moving, ever-escap-
ing marauders.—Sydney Tetegrapii’s S'African corres-
pondent.
I was very much surprised with the Boer farms, for,
from newspaper accounts I had read of the people, I
thought they were an ignorant, dirty race, but the sight
of their homes totally dispelled that idea. Almost with-
out exception, they were scrupulously clean and neat,
and in many houses there was either a piano or organ,
and books were to be found in every place.— Australian
contingenter’s letter ; a fair sample of many dozens that
have appeared in the Australian press.
The August mortality in Kitchener’s murderous
concentration camps, according to the cabled in-
telligence, was at the rate of about 210 per 1000
per annum. Among the children, the death-rate
was 275 per 1000 per annum ! The prisoners are
almost wholly women and children, and Kitchener
certainly makes war on them much more success-
fully than he does on grown-up men. The death-
rate is eighteen times the death-rate of New South
Wales, and twenty times the death-rate of Maori-
land. And this is among non-covnbatants.
It is a death-rate which will practically
extirpate the women and children of the
Boer race inside five years. The prisoners
in these camps are mainly the people whose
homes have been burnt by Kitchener —the
homes which our combustible contingenters
agree in describing as, in the main, clean
and picturesque, well furnished, with books,
pictures, musical instruments, and other evi-
dences of a high civilisation. The women and
children were dragged from these burnt homes to
herd under canvas in densely-crowded, leaky tents,
to sleep in many cases on the ground, to dispense
with the ordinary decencies of life, and to perish
of neglect and disease at a rate which beats the
record of the Black Death —commonly regarded
as the most awful plague which fell on the world
in historical times. During the worst two years
of the Black Death it is reckoned that the total
death-rate went up to 167 per 1000 per annum ;
Kitchener’s prisons leave even that result hull-
down on the far horizon. They beat the death-rate
of London in the worst cholera year ; in fact, they
show a five times worse record than London did
in one of its great cholera visitations. And this
is not an isolated record ; the results have been
much the same, month by month, ever since cer-
tain members of the House of Commons dragged
from the reluctant Salisbury Government in-
formation as to the rate at which the Kitchener
system was killing off the women and children of
the Boer territories. Also, it is from these camps
that the Red Cross nurses were excluded a few
months ago, on the ground that their services
were superfluous, and that things were perfectly
satisfactory ! And yet —consider the humor, or
the brutish callousness, or the hoglike ignorance
of it —the allegedly civilised Sydney Telegraph
describes these perishing mothers and infants as
“being supported in unaccustomed luxury and
idleness,” and is indignant because they don’t
suffer even worse horrors in order to coerce their
male relatives into surrendering. It will be
many years before the moral tone of the
British nation recovers from the degradation
of Kitchener’s campaign. He has dragged
it down to the level of the Middle Ages,
and made the war a war against non-combatants,
and a general destruction of private property, and
he has patterned after Weyler, the Spanish
butcher of Cuba, who invented the horrors of the
concentration camps—though whether Weyler
killed off the mothers and infants as fast as
Kitchener is a matter of doujit. And it will be
long before Australia recovers from the moral
degradation of aiding in this work, and its press
from the unspeakable foulness of being
Kitchener’s apologist.

Another Benevolent Shindykit.
The vultures still hover over that rich carcase —
New Guinea. The latest scheme of Papuan
exploitation—somewhat vague as to details, but
eminently lucid as to the project—was put before'
Premier Barton, the other day, by Theodore F.
Bevan, F.R.G.S. Mr. Bevan has just returned
from the home of boodle—Hingland— and said
he represented a shindykit, which, if the scheme
could be engineered, proposed to call itself
the New Guinea Chartered Industrial and;
Trading Corporation, Limited. The N.G.C.I.
and T.C. Ltd. proposed to “interest itself”
in the whole of British New Guinea, “ the
Commonwealth retaining the government and
continuing the present contributions towards
the cost of administration ; all else would be
made over to the Chartered Co., who would
find all money required for the gradual deve-
lopment and re Cement of the country.”
The amazin r gene wity of the: e shindykiters
is only p i aliekd by their astounding im-
pudence. Mr. Bevan went on in a large and
enthusiastic way. The country was a land of
boundless resources and inexhaustible wealth ;
rich gold runs down from lofty mountains into
rivers which are natural sluice-boxes ; India-
rubber, copra, pearls, timber, &c., constitute
further sources of untold wealth. The syndicate
is willing to tackle all this wealth gratis, and
let Australia pay the cost of governing the
country. It proposes to run steamers on the
rivers, electrical railways, mines, &c. ; har-
bors are to be built, townships created,
and so forth. The Chartered Co. doesn’t
say anything about the present native inhabi-
tants of the country; probably they will be
induced to become amenable to the influences of
civilisation, thereafter to be employed (at a
nominal wage) in boosting-up the syndicate’s divi-
dends : and if they refuse to become so amenable
they will most likely be eliminated, or dispersed,
or concentrated, or whatever happens to be the
word in vogue at the moment. Anyhow, they
don’t seem to count. The Corporation is pre-
pared, so Mr. Bevan says, to lay out at least
£10,000,000 in New Guinea within the next five to
10 years provided the Commonwealth makes it
worth while. Bevan, being merely a sort of
advance agent, couldn’t go into particu-
lars, but he thought the co. would be' satis-
fied with a lease (presumably including all
mineral, agricultural, water, and other rights)
for from 50 to 100 years of such portions of the
land as may become available from time to time.
In a word, this awful syndicate wants the practi-
cal ownership of nearly all New Guinea for a
whole century, without the cost or trouble of
governing it, absolutely for nothing ! As a speci-
men of the modest, retiring promoter, this is
simply stupendous. New Guinea is beginning to
have experiences. The last syndicate (Somers
Vine and co.) that struck that blessed isle was
admittedly “on the make”; this one professes
itself an association of benevolent philanthropists
animated solely by a desire to improve a valuable
Australian asset. The Bulletin hopes this is
the case—but it has its doubts.
An Over-Governed Community.
A good old Sydney Tory, A. W. Pearse, some
little time ago got this off his trunk in Sydney
Telegraph
AN OYER-GOVERNED COMMUNITY.
We have eight Governors, 16 Houses of Parliament,
seven Agents-General, 72 Ministers, and 700 members of
Upper and Lower Houses. Also a vast body of Govern-
ment officials, and all this to manage a population less
than that of London. Is there any wonder that our debt
is fearful to contemplate, and that our taxation is in-
creasing by leaps and bounds? What other result could
we expect? We are told that we have such wonderful
assets in our railways and other reproductive works which
have cost hundreds of millions of pounds. So we have,
if population was being attracted to our shores, but it is
not; in fact, thousands of our young men are leaving us
for South Africa, because they can see no future here,
and our population is not increasing as it should do.
Vast areas of land in New South Wales, Queensland,
and South Australia have been abandoned owing to idiotic
land legislation, and private enterprise is being blocked
rather than encouraged by legislation introduced, not for
the good of the country, but as a means of keeping in
office. What will be the result in the end?
What will be the end ? Or “ How Will It End ? ”
aa Isaiah Cohen said in his novel. Therefore,
let Mr. Pearse join with the Labor party and The
Bulletin in advocating the abolition of five of
the Agents-General, six State Governors (a cheap,
locally-appointed Lieut.-Governor, as in Canada,
is enough for any subordinate State), and six
Legislative Councils (one House of Parlia-
ment, as proved by the Canadian example,
is enough for any State). And let him holler
for breaking up the big estates which
should be available for all these young men to
settle upon as a desirable form of private enter-
prise. As for other private enterprise, let us,
with Pearse’s assistance, give it a big, vigorous
boost by Protection, under which the United
States has become the great workshop of the
world, and let Pearse cease wasting his time over
bi-metallism —which isn’t a productive industry.
Also, let him try to acquire a rooted objection to
the Tory crowd which ran N. S. W. all on its own
until the Labor party appeared in ’9l, and which,
between 1881 and 1891, added £36,000,000 to the
N.S.W. public debt. Things are bad enough
now, but if the Labor party hadn’t appeared in
’9l and somewhat checked this fearful shovelling
out of loan money, N.S.W. would owe ninety
millions by now.

The Colored Capitalist.
The Hambledon (Q.) Cane Planters’ Association
has asked the Queensland Govt, not to indent
Kanakas to Chinese growers. But it is pointed
out that no power exists to prevent the colored
man elbowing out the planter as well as the
planter’s white workmen. In the fate of his boss
—pushed to the wall by a monster of his own crea-
tion—the disinherited white worker may find, for
a while, some melancholy satisfaction, But the
Philp Govt, is certain to discover means of relief
for the capitalist confronted by cheap Chinese com-
petition. In the pearl-shelling industry, organised
capitalism, having driven out white labor with
cheap colored divers, found itself the victim
of tlie black and yellow capitalist’s competition.
At once Parliament interfered to prohibit the
person of color from owning a pearl-shelling boat.
All the pathos about how the Christian faith
makes no distinction between the white man and
the colored specimen at once dried up. The rich
man, who is so shocked at the selfishness of the
poor man in wanting to shut out cheap Asiatic
competition, found the Asiatic competing with
himself, and promptly forbade him doing it by
Act of Parliament. Then, having forcibly sup-
pressed Asiatic competition as directed against
his own personal interests, he went right on being
shocked at the poor man’s selfishness in just the
same old way. Now he wants to shut out the
Asiatic from being a boss planter, because in that
capacity he will interfere with the Fat Man’s
interests, and at the same time his attitude of
Christian surprise because the white worker also
objects to the Asiatic interfering with his interests
is in as good repair as ever.

A Dutch Legislator,
Arthur Griffith, M.L.A., to The Bulletin
As typical of the methods by which the S’African mine-
owners’ press has from the first deliberately misled the
British people with a view to getting them to believe in
an alleged Africander conspiracy, the following is in-
teresting. In the Oape Times of 25th Oct., ’99, appeared
this paragraph
“Mr. M. J. du Plessis, M.L.A. for Cradock, addresses
a letter to his constituents through the medium of the
local Dutch paper, in which he tells them : ‘ The Trans-
vaal people are our kith and kin, and it is only natural
that we should sympathise with them. There is no
honest man, no matter of what nationality, who will not
admire us for doing that.’ ”
Mr. du Plessis is a prominent member of the
Bond party in the Assembly, and this paragraph
is calculated to fasten on him a charge of stimulat-
ing co-operation with the Republican forces. Now
compare this sentence with a literal translation of
the entire letter which Mr. du Plessis addressed
to his constituents, and which may be taken as a
fair example of the tone generally adopted by the
Dutch members: —
“ Oradock, October 14, 1899.
“ To my Fellow-Citizens, —
“ Beloved fellow-citizens, the times in which we live
are serious, and as I shall not have the opportunity of
meeting you at present to address you, and to inform
you of what is in my mind, I use the columns of the
Afrikander, as the matter brooks no delay. The in-
habitants of the Transvaal and the Free State are one in
blood with us, and it is impossible for us not to sympa-
thise with them. There is, moreover, no right-thinking
person of whatever nationality who will not approve this
sentiment. However, I desire to say to all my fellow-
citizens, ‘ Men, sympathise and feel with and for our
relatives and friends as much as you please, but do not
forget that we are subjects of her Majesty, our revered
Queen Victoria, and lei us not commit any excess to mar
the last years of her reign. Let us stay still and leave
the issue which is so momentous for the whole of South
Africa in the hands of Him who judgeth justly, and who
will make all things end in the best way.
“ Your fellow-citizen,
M. J. du Plessis.”

The Flight of Capital.
Apropos of that alleged person who said he was
going to start a Bovril or Beef-tea or something
factory here with British capital, but was so
shocked at our awful Labor legislation that he
couldn’t conscientiously recommend his people to
do it, the Canterbury Times (Christchurch,
M.L.) makes these remarks :
Some wealthy London corporations seem easily
frightened. We remember that a few years ago, before
the Labor tribunals in this country had quite settled
down to their career of usefulness, the Government and
the Liberal party were roundly accused of driving capital
out of the country. A standing dish at all meetings of
employers and of Chambers of Commerce was the sad
story of a wealthy English syndicate whose representa-
tive, having come to Maoriland with the object of
starting a new factory, employing thousands of hands,
was frightened away by our Labor legislation. The
trimmings were varied, but the meat was always the
same. Sometimes the man’s name was Smith, some-
times Jones ; sometimes it was a chevril fac-
tory, sometimes a wooden-nutmeg factory ; but
the man always went away without investing his
money in the country. It seems now that Smith had
been in Sydney, where he confided to a member of the
Chamber of Commerce that he had been all round the
colonies to make arrangements for the manufacture of
raw products on behaif of a wealthy London corporation,
but that, owing to class legislation, he could not recom-
mend his principals to invest money here. He was going
to the Argentine, he said, to carry out his project there.
It is a pathetic picture of the absolute failure of British
colonies, and we really do not understand why so many
people persist in carrying on their bankrupt industries,
losing money day by day as though they were enjoying
themselves. We recollect now that in the Maoriland stories
Smith sometimes went to the Argentine to found his indus-
tries, but we fear that the tick, or lumpy jaw, or some-
thing of the kind, must have frightened him there, for the
industries were never started. The reason for Smith’s
appearance in Sydney is not far to seek. N. S. Wales is
seriously considering the question of introducing legisla-
tion on the lines of our Industrial Conciliation and
Arbitration Act, and the employers are resorting to the
same methods of fighting the reform as were adopted in
Maoriland five or six years ago. But even if we accept
the Smith story as absolute truth, what bearing has it on
the justice or injustice of the legislation, which, after all,
is the question at issue? We are better, in the human
sense, without an industry which cannot afford to pay
a living wage to the workers and to grant
them proper conditions of labor. And from the
financial point of view we cannot believe that
labor legislation constitutes a more serious tax upon
industries than do the strikes of older countries.
Here in Maoriland we learnt that lesson nearly a decade
ago, and whatever changes may be made in our legislation,
it is certain that neither employers nor workmen would
consent to any alteration that would throw us back to the
conditions existing prior to 1891. Mr. Wise, whose sym-
pathies are not altogether with the workers, has been
courteous enough to defend Maoriland against the attacks
that are being made upon her in N.S.W., adopting very
much the attitude of Judge Backhouse. He does not
accept the Maoriland experiment as final, but he admits
that our experience does not justify the gloomy fore-
bodings of gentlemen like the Smith of the story. It has
shown, he says, the folly of those who told them that
capital would leave the country if industrial disputes
were settled by other than the “ barbarous and wasteful
methods of strikes and lock-outs.” That much is granted
now by all sections of the community, and we have little
doubt that Australia will have the good sense to follow
our example in the near future.
Peacock s vacillation over the Convention Bill
has already had the effect of further stiffening the
sufficiently-inflexible back of Victoria’s House of
48 Rich Landlords. The stolid four dozen, being
invited to pass the Old Age Pensions Further
Provision Bill—validating payments made since
last June—calmly put it on one side till they got
an assurance from the Kookaburra that 7s. a
week should be the future maximum. The spine-
less laugher cordially agreed to the 7s. limit, as
far as he was able to commit the Assembly;
and the brutally selfish four dozen promptly
showed what they thought of him by
throwing his Exported Products Bill into the
gutter. There is little hope for Victoria till
that State finds or grows a Premier who
knows how to fight, and has a cause worth fight-
ing for. Meanwhile, every fresh insolence on the
part of the Landlords brings nearer the time when
the Victorian members will sool the Federal
Legislature on to them. The Federal Parliament
should take up the Exported Products question,
which, as a commercial matter, comes within its
range ; it can easily do so by levying an export-
duty on produce that is not of sufficiently good
quality to deserve the official stamp. And it
should levy graduated land and income taxes and
absentee tax as Federal imposts, whereby it
might make the Landlords sorry that they had not
been more reasonable in their day of blatant
pride, and grieved that they had provoked outside
interference.

The other day Customs Minister Charley
Kingston arose in all his W.C.T.U. wrath and
prosecuted the publishing firm of Geo. Robert-
son and Co. for selling Balzac’s “ Droll Stories ”
and Paul de Kocic’s “ Monsieur Dupont,” on the
ground that they were indecent works. Prof.
Morris was called to uphold the opinion of the
department, and P.M. Panton fined the firm
£lOO, reducing it to £25 after a lot of trouble.
Balzac, especially, possesses a reputation
which, rightly or wrongly, leads the average
man to regard all his books as standard
literature. If every book-firm is supposed to be
answerable for the decency of every line of its
books, not one quarter of the literature written
prior to ttie present century, and including
Chaucer, Shakespeare and the Bible itself, will be
available. The suppression of these quasi-indecent
works may be advisable in the interests of
morality, but Religious Tract Societies which deal
in Bibles should certainly be instructed to cease
the pernicious practice on pain of being haled
before a police court. And, during the
prosecution, Librarian Armstrong admitted
that there was a copy of “Droll Stories”
in the Melb. Public Library—in French, it is true,
but available for all-comers. The prosecution of
Mr. Armstrong, and the fining of the Library
trustees, must logically follow Kingston’s action.
Mr. Coldham, who appeared for the defence,
elicited from Prof. Morris that he had included
“ St. Agnes Eve,” by Keats, in a book for school-
boys, but saw nothing indecent about it (notwith-
standing that its motive is of a young man looking
through a keyhole at a girl undressing herself).
The friskiness of Kingston’s youth used to be
described as reprehensible by pious Adelaideans.
The morality of his old age threatens to be
calamitous.'

In Victoria the Govt, is doing what a private
member has undertaken in N.S.W. Solicitor-
General Wynne has introduced a bill to amend
the law respecting money-lenders. It includes
every variety of usurer excepting licensed pawn-
brokers, societies registered under the Friendly
Societies or the Building Societies Acts, or bona
fide banks. No specific rate of interest is fixed, or
named as excessive, but very wide powers are
given to the courts to open up and review all kinds
of money-lending transactions, and to reduce
charges considered to be “ harsh and unconscion-
able.” The Bill provides that the shent per shent
gentleman must register in his own name and ad-
dress. The penalty for non-registration is a fine not
exceeding £lOO, and, on a second conviction, im-
prisonment for not more than three months. If
the offender be a body corporate, that body is liable
to a fine not exceeding £5OO. Any money-lender
who makes any false or misleading statement,
representation, or promise, whereby he fraudu-
lently" induces, or attempts to induce anyone to
borrow money) is liable to imprisonment up to two
years or to a fine not exceeding £5OO. Every honest
man in Vic. Parliament ought to do his best to
rush this Bill through—with a big and important
amendment. The weak point is that of almost all
Bills—though a maximum punishment is fixed
there is no minimum, and the sympathetic court,
which mustn’t fine the shark more than £lOO, will
often indignantly charge him 2s. fid. Let us have
a minimum as well as a maximum.

Here is an edifying letter
Editor Bulletin, —
Now that the deficiencies of the Watson’s Bay lifeboat
service have received attention, perhaps the N.S.W.
Department concerned will consider other evils that
retpiire a remedy.
Speaking to a deep-water sailor, the other day, he related
the following curious state of affairs—and subsequent
inquiry has satisfied me that he was stating facts :
When a donkey-engine is placed aboard a sailing-ship
the practice is to reduce the number of fo’c’sle hands.
Now, a donkey-engine is first-class for hoisting sails and
various other things ; but when a dirty squall appears,
and the ship has to be shortened-down instanter, men—
plenty men—are required.
A ship of 1300 tons recently left Sydney with this crew :
—Master, two mates, two apprentices, cook, cabin-boy,
and ten seamen. Having lost two men, their places were
filled by a fireman and a coal-trimmer, both foreigners,
and knowing practically no English.
Each watch was thus composed of a mate and five sea-
men, and is supposed to provide one man for the wheel
and one for look-out, thereby leaving the mate and three
men to work the ship. The look-out man is frequently
regarded by the master as an idle vagabond, and, in con-
sequence, he is not allowed to loaf. So it comes to pass
that he only looks-out when there is nothing else for him
to do. Another of being short-handed is
that there is no regular watch-below (sleep) for anybody,
even in ordinary weather, and the vigilant look-out
required by law for the protection of life and property is
not kept.
Another trouble with some Australian ships is that they
are not properly provisioned. One of them recently
arrived at her home-port, after a 6iJ-da.vs’ passage. Her
former record for the same trip was 65 days, and on this
occasion she was short-handed, yet, on the 58th day, her
stores were down to a barrel of rotten beef, and half-a-
barrel of flour. The crew were painfully thin at that, and
what they would have looked like had the ship been out
10 days longer may be imagined. The same ship, by the
way, carried two Britons—one an apprentice, the other
the cook.
What can the poor wretches do ? They dare not com-
plain, because that would prejudice their chances of
getting another ship.
There is another matter that needs inquiry : How many
ships are thpre in Sydney and Newcastle that have their
permanent ballast stowed immediately on top of the
cement stiffening in their bottoms, so as to give them
more space for carrying lumber ?
These evils require remedies, and the man charged with
that duty must be made responsible in fact, and not in
name only. At present, when an unseaworthy ship goes
to sea and is lost, the responsible man, who is supposed
to see that she was seaworthy, sometimes gets a “ severe
caution ” from his departmental chief. The proper
penalty for negligence of this sort is a heavy fine in all
eases, and a stiff term in Darlinghurst prison when the
negligence is proved to be gross.— Viking.
