SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Royal “ Sweet and Twenty ” and “ The
Bugle Call.”
Lyceum “A London Arab ”
Her Majesty’s Italian Opera.
Empire Fuller Co.
Palace “ World’s Entertainers.”
Tivoli Rickards’ Co.
MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Princess’s Crane-Power Co.
Her Majesty’s “ The Casino Girl.”
Bijou “ Tom, Dick and Harry.”
Theatre Royal “ Taken From Life.”
Opera House Rickards’ Variety Co.
Heroes, male and female, around whom
grand opera is written, don’t seem to have taken
any stock of the fact that the Almighty’s canon is
fixed against self-slaughter. Of course they
occasionally slaughter the other party, but that
seems more natural than permanently effacing
themselves from glorious scenery and palace-halls.
For they always live in great style, and with no
more hint of squalor than is conveyed by a snow-
capped mountain range and a lake glowing ruby
under the setting sun. “ Ernani,” staged at Her
Majesty’s on Saturday night, is founded, as to
story, on Yictor Hugo’s “ Hernani,” and tells the
tale of a lovely lady who is loved by three men (so
unequally are things managed in this world), viz.,
a king, a grandee, and a robber chief. She
(Elvira she is named) loves the robber, who
is described as “ practising his calling in the
vicinity.” This is really a pretty touch and
calls up an impression of a mountain fastness
bearing a brass-plate inscribed, “ Ernani and Co.,
Solicitors on the Highway.” The grandee has the
advantage of the other lovers in the fact that he
is Elvira’s guardian, and he is going to compel
her to wear orange-blooms when the royal lover
glides along (with a cloak over his royal brands
and ear-marks), and strives to get her to go away
with him. Then arrives the robber-man, with
bugle, daggers, big boots, and angel sleeves. He
challenges the king, but apologises when the
king’s kingship is declared. Presently, the king
gets rid of the bandit on a wily pretext. Later,
lovers and maid come together again as the
marriage with the guardian is nigh celebration.
Ernani, in the disguise of a pilgrim, is hidden by
Don Silva, the guardian, from the wrath of the
king, but only till the grandee can do his own
killing. Elvira is “lifted” by the king as a hostage.
Ernani comes out of his hiding-place, but declines
to fight with one who has saved his life. He, how-
ever, offers to die by his own hand whenever Don
Silva shall call upon him by sounding one note
upon a bugle horn which he gives the elderly
lover. Don Carlos, the king, is elected Emperor
of Germany, and in the general rejoicing he does
a lot of pardoning and gives Elvira to Ernani.
They are married, and are trilling joyous duets
when the furious Don Silva blows the fatal note.
The bridal pair sing anguished solos at the re-
morseless Don Silva, then Ernani, after his 16th
century notion of honor, keeps his promise with a
stiletto.
* • * *
Signora Benedetto as Elvira was in
splendid voice on the opening night, and her solo
“ Ernani Involami,” the music of which is an old
and beloved friend, was encored. Larizza, as the
bandit chief, was occasionally too robust in the use
of his fine tenor. The choruses in the conspiracy
scene went with great vigor and brought a lot of
ill-timed applause from the galleries. Larizza
swings and sways to his music; —he sings even
with his legs, and if he wouldn’t put so much dis-
temper into his nose ——. Signor Bozzoli, as the
king, showed off his baritone royally, and his dress
when he becomes Emperor of Germany—well, if
Edward VII. gets nigh its splendor on his corona-
tion day the photographers will be glad. As the
Spanish grandee, Signor Travaglini was a fine, old,
proud basso of a proud race, and Signorina Marchi
filled her usual role of consolatrix of the afflicted,
and sang her few notes with as much sweetness as
Yerdi allows, and that’s a good deal.
'#* * *
Basil Hood, the man who wrote the joyous
libretto of the “Rose of Persia,” has fallen some-
what from his high estate in “ Sweet and Twenty ”
—the latest Broughism at Sydney Royal. It is
called a comedy for probably the same reason that
“colonel” is called “kurnel.” Most things in
this world are called what they are not. A red-
headed handy man (Brough plays the part in a
broad smile and glaring whisker) is amusing
enough, but he doesn’t belong to the plot
to any extent worth mentioning. The tale
itself is built upon a long, pallid parson;
and his son, the pale, plump prig, who is study-
ing for the church ; and his other son, the joyous
naval officer, whose faith in his fraternal prig is
pathetic to witness ; and the girl both these sons
love. In the first act, the handy man breaks a
good many things, and the naval son goes
away to rejoin his vessel. In the next act he
comes back a wreck, dismissed for drunken-
ness, and the handy man breaks more things,
and the prig-son sets himself up as media-
tor, and urges his brother to go away and
redeem his character in Australia. He does this
in order that he may take the girl to his pale theo-
logical bosom in the prodigal’s absence. And in the
third act, after the handy man has broken almost
everything, the prig proposes to the girl, and
tells her his brother is drunker than ever in Aus-
tralia—only just then the brother comes in pro-
sperous and happiy, having given up drink, and
gone to Australia, and made enough to buy a
new suit of clothes, and come back, all in a mar-
vellously short space of time. He becomes
engaged to the girl, though nothing very definite
appears as to his means of supporting her. The
morose prig brother, who is eaten up with
theology, goes out to shoot himself, but doesn’t.
And the handy man breaks quite a lot of crockery
in between whiles.
There are gleams of better things in the
play, but there aren’t enough better things. The
png, with his small meannesses and his small
piety, his dull, leaden, yet intense affections and
knowledge of Scripture—with a black tail coat
and the instincts of a saint looking through a
keyhole into a girl’s bedroom from the best
motives, is a good, though dreary, study, and
Cecil Ward plays him admirably. Yet Ward
looks too old for the part. Miss Temple hardly
looks her part either, yet she has a fine conception
of the character of the girl who is supposed to be
sweet and twenty. Ernest Vere’s sailor brother
is an individual who wails dolefully in his
time of ill-fortune. Mr. and Mrs. Brough and
Miss Vaughan brighten up the play a good deal
in an extraneous way, but the plot is thin and the
tale is small and dolorous. Among the characters
in the real story Charles Garry, as the father
parson, is about the best. Garry looks the
long-established reverend who has mouldered all
his life in a country vicarage. There is also a
mother who plays her part consistently, inasmuch
as she is ill upstairs in all the three acts ; and a
small, consistent alligator, said to have been
brought home in a basket, which remains in the
next room with an attention to detail that can’t
be too much commended.
» * • •
A one-act play, the “Bugle Call,” doesn’t
brighten the programme much. It is the simple
story of a girl whose mother wanted her to marry
a rich man, but she refuses him, and becomes
eugaged instead to a poor lieutenant who is
bound for Africa to burn the Boer farms. As the
lieutenant is going out he hears the bugle-call, and
that is about all. Mrs. Brough and Miss Susie
Vaughan help along this fragment a good deal,
but Miss Dora Battiscombe—the girl who becomes
engaged to the man who hears the bugle—is only
a moderate success.
-* * * #
Mr. F. M. Alexander and Miss Edith
Tasca-Page went through a few nights’ season of
“ Hamlet ” at Sydney Criterion. Mr. Alexander
gave a more hare-brained representation than
usual of the scraggy youth with the bee in his
bonnet, who pretended to be mad till he really
became so, and besides which, was insane from
the start. This drama always gives one the im-
pression that the king was rather a soft person,
considering the grave crime he is alleged to have
committed on the uncertain evidence of a ghost.
Mr. Tait made an excellent Polonius, but when
stabbed by Hamlet it is a pity that an enterpris-
ing super behind the arras didn’t put alittlered ink
on the tip of the weapon. Miss Beatrice Parry,
who took the part of the Queen on Thursday
night, looks much too young to have a son making
disrespeotful remarks to her on her age. Miss
Edith Tasca Page put much energy into her mad
scene, and, as far as a mere lay person can judge,
her insanity was correct in every item. Five
minutes after she left the stage her drowned body
was brought in and promptly buried, the weary
and hopeless Court of Denmark making no attempt
at resuscitation, but evidently thinking it was all
for the best. The humor of the grave-diggers was
gone through with fair but not overpowering
success, after which the Court of Denmark went
to pieces in the usu.il way and strewed itself
about the throne steps, while two bright, young
pages stood, one on each side of that piece
of furniture, looking as if Royal personages
in Denmark were quite in the habit of toppling
into eternity in this manner. Why can’t a page
strain a point sufficiently to give way to reason-
able excitement when he sees the whole royal
family perishing unexpectedly on the floor?
* * * *
The red figure of Shylock followed the
weird, black figure of Hamlet at Sydney Criterion
on Monday night. The most noticeable features
about the first performance were the intervals,
which were long. The shifting of stage furniture
was sometimes done with the curtain raised, and
the Jew’s house was frequently heard banging
itself about in the dark during its erection.
Alexander’s Jew made a better all-round im-
pression than his Hamlet, though it hasn’t
got the dignity of some Shylocks. Dorahy
made a good impression as young Gobbo,
and Mr. Spong, as his half-blind father, delivered
himself of his aged voice very impressively. The
scene where Portia is united to her lover was
adorned by a bevy of young ladies in various
evening costumes, one very tall, pink lady, in
something Grecian, being specially noticeable for
her soldierlike attitude. (There was also a modern
Japanese screen in Portia’s drawing-room.) These
young ladies are understood to be the slaves of
the household, and it was evidently the fashion in
these days for all the servants to be invited into
the drawing-room whenever a young man was
going to propose. They didn’t give young men a
chance to escape in Italy during the Middle Ages.
* * * *
The vaudeville season at Sydney Palace
closes shortly, and for a while, at any rate, the
public will be deprived of the burglar, the bright
dancers, and other people who are entertaining
and, above all things else, polite. For to-day
(Saturday) a new programme is announced, and
Nelstone will illustrate in fresh dances a weird
crookedness, wasted on any sphere but politics,
whilst others will strive to be as unlike their
present selves as possible.
* * * *
As a shrine of true Anglo-Saxon gaiety,
Sydney Empire draws cheery audiences. The
niggers fall upon the stage with a bump
which tells of genuine bruises and livens the house
to honest hilarity. A show which can get its
knockabout men to fall in a realistically painful
way will always have support wherever the Union
Jack flies.
* * * *
“ The Orphan Heiress ” continues to be a
blighted blossom at Sydney Lyceum, and the
shapeless tiger grows more and more weary at
seeing his delicate titbit of distressed heroine
snatched from him by destiny just when he is pre-
paring for the feast. The heroine has been so
knocked about by her guardians that she enters
the den quite passively and with all the shriek died
out of her. The rest of the characters behave
with the usual amount of imbecility. Something
concerning a street arab is to be staged at the
Lyoeum next week,
** # *
The chief feature at Sydney Tivoli this
week is the illusion exlrib tion of Madame Dante ;
anybody who wants to see what looks like the
white souls of a score or so ladies whirling round
in garments that resemble many-colored flames
flashing through the darkness should go to see
Madame Dante as multiplied through numerous
mirrors. The lady also produces the fiery end of
“ She ” and dances a Lily dance —all notable so far
as prettiness is concerned, but stopping far short
of anything remarkable in the matter of novelty.
“ She” has been burned too often of late years to
excite much furore. Skate-dancing Almond once
more pounds joyously on the Tivoli boards, and
Little Gulliver, the pocket comedian, receives a
genuine encore for his reasons why there is no
place like home. Gulliver says he regards home
as a good place to sober-up in.
** * *
The slow and stately season of Wilson
Barrett and co., at Her Majesty’s (Melb.), will
conclude to-night (Thursday) with, or without,
the Doxology. For sundry reasons, “Ciaudian”
has been selected to play Barrett off the premises,
the principal reason, perhaps, being a mighty
earthquake which befalls the palace of Ciaudian in
the second act, and leaves him to work out his
strange salvation amidst the ruins. The enthusi-
astic shilling majority of the audience, which has
remained loyal-to Wilson Barrett all through his
season, will think it fitting that he should perish
finally ’midst an earthquake’s debris, but present
scribe would have preferred to see him make a
farewell bow as Yirginius. “Virginius,” by
Sheridan Knowles, is an 80-year-old tragedy
of almost Shakespearian vigor, and the name-
part belongs to Wilson Barrett by right of
his old legitimate method and full experience in
the art of how to run the show. He did it on
Saturday for once, and the gods saw that it was
good. In fact, they saw that it was better than
his previous production of the piece, inasmuch as
Miss M‘Carthy, who played Virgy’s daughter, is
many points lighter than Maud Jeffries used to
weigh ; therefore,, the spectacle of the ancient
Roman parent heading an insurrection with his
offspring’s corpse in his arms was a more tragic
effect than when he staggered under the heavier
burden. Barrett knows his business in all depart-
ments. A sound, strong actor, with occasional
genius ; a prince of stage-managers in his own
Roman line ; a king of advertisers ; a Great
Grafter. But Bar.att makes mistakes, like
weaker mortals; and one of his errors
this season was the staging of “Quo
Vadis ” for four nights when he could have been
so much more respected in “ Yirginius.” Barrett’s
co. has some capital male figures for hanging
Roman drapery on. The two Majeronis, Basil
Gill and Carter-Edwards carry their sheetings
with an air of distinction, whilst Miss Lillah
M‘Carthy looks so sweet that even her despondent
tones don’t make one feel very sorry for her. And
these figures seem to belong to the time of Yir-
ginius, which was probably centuries 8.0., if
that somewhat mythical citizen ever had any time
at all. One word more on the serious subject of
Wilson Barrett. Last Friday he and his hard-
worked co. contributed a matinee performance of
“The Christian King” in aid of “The World’s
Fair,” otherwise the Abbotsford Convent fund.
Considering that the Fair professes to have a large
and influential committee working for the cause,
and Barrett gave his show on that understanding,
the committee aforesaid deserves no glory for its
neglect to push the matinee and fill the house.
In such cases a full attendance ought to be
guaranteed.
»* * *
J. C. Williamson’s Comic Opera Co., an
organization which ranks as a top brick on the
Commonwealth chimney, and a corner-stone of
Australia’s social system, and is likewise a
sparkling well-spring of happiness, and a heap of
other matters —the Comic Opera co. that went
away from Melbourne six months ago is returning
to Her Majesty’s on Saturday next (sth) for a
short season. “ The Casino Girl ”is the bill, and
a violent colored posterette depicts the young
female in the act of embracing a pale donkey with
her estimable legs.
** # *
Saturday night, at the Bijou, brought a
great gathering of everybody who used to rush
the house in the palmy B. and B. days of yore,
plus many other people who have been coming on
in the meantime. An S.R.O. night, with an
accent on the O, and all because “Tom, Dick,
and Harry” had a ring of frivolousness in its
title, and promised to be a farce of the Smith-
Jones order. There is, seemingly, a great open-
ing in Melbourne just now for an entertainment
named “Thompson” or “Plain Bill.” The world
wants to feel certain that nothing dreadful will
happen in the last act. “Tom, Dick, and Harrv”
is a farcical comedy, written by a woman, and the
slim white hand of female authorship is percep-
tible in its complications. Tom suffers from a
dyspeptic military father, who doesn’t want him
to marry a certain Daisy ; consequently, thwarted
Thomas assumes the disguise of a red-headed man
in a straw hat, and the other man happens to be
the ginger twin-brother of a third straw hat. The
middle twin has a lost wife looking for him, whilst
the third ginger of the trinity gets cut off from
his girl, and the red deceiver who is all the world
to Daisy gets mistaken for either twin, just
according to which woman chances to recognise
him by his false hair. Also, the twins get mis-
taken for the wrong Rufuses by the shrieking
sisterhood, and every man hugs the other
fellows’ bits of property in turn, and the dyspeptic
warrior flies to a lunatic asylum for consolation.
Moreover, the three young men, at whose fiery
heads matches may be lighted, are likewise con-
veyed to the asylum by violence, or of their own
free will, and the three girls chase the wrong
lunatics. Finally, the red-headed deceiver
removes his wig in order to get his father’s
blessing, and the wandering wife recovers the
identity of her' twin, and the joke flickers
out. It is a good joke in some places, and
a tired joke sometimes. Take it for all in
all, “ Tom, Dick and Harry” might be a
weaker joke. All the same, an authoress who
creates three red-headed men in one mould,
and turns them loose upon the stage with in-
structions to finish up in a lunatic asylum, is a
sort of new humorist who deserves little en-
couragement. There are limits to dramatic
license, and the literary mother of “ Tom, Dick
and Harry ” oversteps the limit. The cast is not
very dilferentfrom what it was in Sydney. Herbert
Ross as the unreal ginger lover, and W. F.
Hawtrey as the demented dyspeptic are the main
strength of the co. Miss Ruby Ray and Miss
Roxy Barton shine brightest of the ladies. Some
of the other performers make reasonable attempts
to be funny, but the effort is not always suc-
cessful.
* * * *
“A Highland Legacy,” the mild and pretty
first piece, would be prettier and more exciting if
Thos. Holding, O. P. Heggie—suggestive name,
Heggie ! —and Gregan MacMahon, not to mention
the ladies, were 18 carat actors. Hawtrey, in a
kilt, is a valuable consideration. He dis-
Eenses wealth to the inhabitants of a garret, and
rings the curtain down on a feeling little speech,
and “ A Highland Legacy ” is a pleasant memory
during the red-headed revels that ensue.
** * *
“The Only Way,” now staged at Mel-
bourne Princess’s, is a notable success as regards
the one strong character in the play. Tyrone
Power’s living pictures of Sydney Carton may be
a little too melodramatic, but they are fine, manly
pictures. The actor spreads himself in the part,
and the part bears the strain, although Tyrone
Power is about 62 inches tall and boisterous in
proportion. He convinces from first to last. A new
Mimi (Miss Eva M‘Donald) is an intelligent
little successor to Miss Rose Musgrove; the
Defarge of Roland Stavely is good enough, like-
wise Charles Hammond as Darnay. Miss
Crane and Oily Deering play their former
parts passably. With the two exceptions
of Tyrone Power and the scenery no item
in “ The Only Way ” seems quite all that it
might be. The French Revolution has lost some
of its original tumult, and the roar of popular
approval when a bloated aristocrat steps outside
to get guillotined sounds as though the multitude
had mostly lapsed into a blood-thirsty silence.

In The Bulletin of 20th ult. an item
appeared having reference to a recent Melbourne
railway accident. It has been pointed out to us
by Mr. John Birt (known on the stage as Pro-
fessor Weston), trainer of performing dogs, that
the paragraph might be misconstrued into a
reflection on his honesty. No such meaning was
intended in regard to the gentleman in question,
and we take the first opportunity of expressing
regret for any annoyance that may have been
caused to a reputable citizen.

Queensland has dumped on the London market
a 3 per cent, loan of £1,374,213 at £9l 10s. for every £lOO.
This makes the fourth big Australian loan issued in
London this year, or if the Victorian loan of £3,000,000 is
omitted (it being merely a renewal on most disastrous
terms of an old liability) there are still three new loans,
all issued in September, and aggregating 1 £6,874,213. The
Queensland loan has been underwritten at the usual
charge of £1 ss. per cent., reducing the yield to £9O ss.
for every £lOO, and expenses have to come out of that.
So results are something like this :
Debt Cash received Loss
incurred. about about

Victoria 3,000,000 2,715,000 285,000
Westralia .. .. 1,500,000 1,335,000 165,000
Queensland .. .. 1,374,213 1,229,921 144,292
£9,874,213 £8,959,921 £914,292
Roughly speaking, this means that the credit of the four
States was just about good enough to enable them to
borrow at £3 10s. per cent, all round. But, with the
usual desire to shirk present trouble, they bor-
rowed at a rate which involves paying somewhere
about £3 ss. 6d. per cent, on the money actually
received, and the rest of the interest (about
£914,000) is added to the public debt. In other words, in
addition to leaving future generations to pay for all this

If further proof is needed that Luhrig Vanners
are the best and most economical machines for dressing
fines and slimes, it will be found in the fact that the Tas-
mania gold mine, Beaconsfield, Tasmania, has decided to
increase the number of these machines in the extension
of the present mill and reduction works. When com-
pleted it will have over 100 head of stampers at work, and
60 Luhrig Vanners, including the additional 24 of the
large type. Before finally adopting plans the Tasmania
board sent its manager to inspect the chief reduction
works in other States. Messrs. W. and J. Lempriere, of
Melbourne, are the Sole Agents for the Luhrig appli-
ances.*

• * * *
A fall of £4 per ton took place in copper last
week immediately following the announcement that the
Amalgamated Copper Trust had declared a disappointing
div., and had thereby paralysed operations in Wall-street.
It was followed by a £2 rise, which was immediately suc-
ceeded by a£2 10s. drop. Then came another rise, and
at time of writing the price is in the vicinity of £63, or
£4 lower than it was a week before. No definite explana-
tion has been given. Probably the big Copper Trust of
America has manoeuvred it. Some two months ago
shareholders in copper companies which had joined the
Trust against their wish threatened to bring an action
and to have the Trust declared an illegal organisation. It
is not unlikely that the proceedings have started, and
that the Trust, in the declaration of a disappointing div.,
has some other fish to fry; perhaps it has put copper
down to foreshadow what would happen if the Trust
itself collapsed.

* * * *
The usual Bill to authorise the Sydney Municipal
Council to borrow £200,000 has slid gently through
N.S.W. Assembly. The bulk of the money (£167,791) is,
as usual, to pay off the overdraft which has accumulated
through the Council not raising enough taxes to cover its
necessary current expenses. The increase of the rates to
Is 9d. in the £ has made ends come nearer to meeting,
but there is such a vast interest-bill to pay on the old
shortages that it would probably need 2s. in the £ to
wholly stop the drift.

The Australian Metropolitan Life Assurance
Co. (Brisbane) has closed its best year up to date, and it
has again—for the second time in about three years—
written up the book-value of its office. This is how
things hare gone with the co. since its inception :
Expenses Total Added to funds
Total and Out- (apart from
Income. Claims. Dividends, goings, writing up of
property).
1896 £250
1897 .. £4540 £133 £2754 £2887 1653
1898 .. 6393 751 4423 5174 1219
1899 .. 7252 992 5102 6094 1158
1900 .. 9386 1277 6934 7211 2175
1901 .. 11,023 1483 6551 8034 2989
£38,594 £4636 £24,764 £29,400 £9444
Office Property written up (1898) .. £10,095
t> >, written up again (1901) 5500 15,595
Nominal amount of Assurance Fund.. £25,039
As far as can be ascertained from the information
available the Australian Metropolitan Co. bought
its office property for £14,405 and by adding £15,695 on
to the valuation it now makes it appear as an asset worth
£30,000. Therefore, over three-fifths of the nominal
assurance fund of £25,039 consists of the extra value
tacked on to that office property. There is, of course, no
positive evidence that it isn’t worth £30,000. The invest-
ments consist of said office property, £30,000, and loans
on mortgage £3103 ; total £33,103. Interest and rents
received are set down at £1521, which represents
£4 12s. lid. per cent, on the average funds for the year.
If this rate can be maintained the writing-up of the
property may be held to be justified, but at the same time
the rate isn’t a high one, and it doesn't look as if the
building could stand any further inflation.
At August, 1900, the Australian Metropolitan
held its first quinquennial investigation, and decided that
the present value of the liabilities under its policies was
£10,324. It had actually accumulated £6455, so but for
the writing up of the office property there would have
been a deficit in the assurance fund of £3869. As it was,
there was an apparent surplus of £6226. As most of this
was carried forward, and as the office property has since
obligingly increased in value by another £5500, there will
probably be another handsome surplus at the investiga-
tion in 1905. Also the heavy expense rate is diminishing
somewhat, though it is still very massive, and the co.
may really be in a position before long to pay its way out
of earnings. Expenses run as follows :
Expenses in Proportion to Total Income.
1897 ..£6O 13 2 per cent. 1900 ..£63 4 6 percent.
1898 .. 69 310 „ 1901 .. 59 8 7 ~
1899 .. 70 7 0 „
The business is partly ordinary life and partly
the expensive “ industrial ” racket, and as the accounts
and assets of the two branches are mixed together, it is
quite impossible to say whether the Australian Metro-
politan runs its ordinary life business on anything like a
reasonable expense rate or not. The best thing the co.
can do now is to put £ll,OOO, being that portion of its
written-up values which was not absorbed at last year’s
valuation, to a reserve fund, and to let it accumulate at
(say) 3 per cent, compound interest, so that if the build-
ing, which increased in value so fortunately, should
decline again it will be possible to write it down without
breaking anything. Then the co. should start to keep
separate accounts of its ordinary and industrial business.
And, above all, it shouldn’t write up that good old office
any more. Up to now expenses and dividends have
absorbed over five times the amount paid to policy-
holders. So the latter class can’t be said to have made
much out of the concern so far.

Adelaide is a city of many small Tramway
Companies whose more or less antiquated horse drags an
antiquated box-on-wheels along streets which deserve to
be infested with something better. Yet, even these
prehistoric vehicles make something out of the business.
Here are the accounts of three of the companies for the
past two years :
Adelaide and Suburban Tramway 00.
(Paid-up Capital, £130,000.)
Total
Profits. Dividends. Reserves.
July, 1899 £21,244
January, 1900 £7,428 8 per cent. =£5,200 23,472
July, 1900 6,858 8 „ „ 5,200 25,130
January, 1901 8,189 8 „ „ 6,200 28,119
July, 1901 9,165 10 „ „ 6,500 30,784
Adelaide, Unley and Mitcham Tramway Co.
(Paid-up Capital now £31,250.)
Total

Adelaide and Hindmarsh Tramway Co.
(Paid-up capital, £37,297.)
Total
Profits. Dividends. Deficit.
June, 1899 .. ~£14,383
December, 1899 ... £489 Nil .. 13,894
June, 1900 .. 121 Nil .. 13,773
December, 1900 .. 372 Nil .. 13,401
June, 1901 .. 1065 Nil .. 12,336
These are only three out of five Adelaide tramway co’s.
and they include the most utterly unlucky one of the
crowd. Yet these three, taken jointly, have earned
during the last two years an all-round average of over 10J
per cent, per annum on their paid-up capital. Their
position, too, is distinctly improving ; last year’s earn-
ings, taken separately, were nearly 12 per cent. If two
other tramway co’s. are thrown in, the average earnings
of the five are well over 10 per cent.—this despite a most
antiquated and unprofitable system, and the waste
entailed by all this extra management and accountancy.
If the State or a municipal Tramway Trust
took over the whole business and converted it into an
up-to-date electric system with only one management,
there should be no difficulty whatever in making 10 per
cent, profits. Even if the work wasn’t done until the
present concessions expire there are only a few
years to wait, and Adelaide can think of something else
meanwhile to divert its mind. The Snow Syndicate, if it
gets its concession, has arranged to buy out the existing
concerns and start building a new system immediately,
and the State could do the work quite as immediately as
Snow could. But even if it couldn’t, there is no such
desperate haste, after all these years, that the most
profitable State enterprise in S.A. should be given away
for a song, merely to save another year or so. An arti-
ficial agitation has been frothed up to make Adelaide
believe that something unspecified has happened which
makes it absolutely necessary to have things rectified at
once regardless of cost. The sleepy city wants its little
penny doll on the instant, even if it pays £5O or more for
it.
» ♦ * *
Adelaide’s horse-trams were reasonably up-to-
date when they started, but the horse became antiquated,
and the old co.’s simply said : “ It isn’t worth our while
establishing anything new, as our lease will expire in a
few years; the old horse will last our time.” Similarly,
if S. A., as proposed, gives away its tramway rights for a
great number of years to Snow’s syndicate, that syndi-
cate will probably supply an up-to-date service— but, in
time, it will also become as antiquated as the horse is
now. Then Snow’s co. will simply say: “It isn’t worth
our while making any changes, as our lease has. only
another (say) 20 years to run. This old rattietrap
electric arrangement, which was new in 1901, will
last our time.” The fool city which gives away
its tramway privileges to a syndicate is always
being treated like that, unless it gives away its rights for
ever, in which case it is treated a great deal worse. It is
always either giving its "syndicate extra concessions to
induce it to make improvements and bring its concern up
to date, or paying it large 6ums to get out of the road
before its time and let someone else make the improve-
ments, or putting up with a dilapidated system and
waiting anxiously till the concession expires that it may
get reforms.

The Colonial Bank (Melbourne) announces that
the last of its reconstructed deposits will be repaid on
30th December. They amounted to £3,043,196 in May,
1893, but by last March they were reduced to £123,990.
The Royal Bank of Queensland pays off its last instalment
at the same date, and then the only banks with recon-
structed deposits unpaid will be the Queensland National
(Queensland), Australian Joint Stock (N.S.W.), Com-
mercial of Australia (Victoria), and the London Bank and
English, Scottish and Australian (England).

The co. made £l2 3s. 3d. per cent, last year on its capital

growing concern ; still, the total shareholders’ funds are
only £42,926, and it owes £98,832 to depositors, and has
£131,951 lent on mortgage. If Maoriland struck an
experience like that of Australia in 1893, and there was a
rush to withdraw deposits, and mortgages became practi-
cally unrealisable for the time, something remarkable
might happen to a co. even as strong as this which trades
so largely with borrowed money.

» » * •
Another important development announced
from Lucknow, Mr. Newman, manager of the D’Arcy
Wentworth mine, having exhibited in Orange samples of
auriferous ore obtained from the deep levels, and the
writer knows from experience what the effect of that
general!}' is. “ The samples are very rich, and equal to
several hundred ounces to the ton. This development is
regarded as of the highest importance, as it proves that
Lucknow is about to experience a revival. The miners
say that they are continuations of the veins which, in
previous years, yielded such high returns, besides which
there are almost unlimited quantities of low-grade ore in
sight.” A puff paper par. from Lucknow comes now
about once a week. The place is sadly in need of resur-
rection. All the mines, with one exception, are shut
down. A place that employed upwards of 1000 men
is practically deserted ; further exemption from
labor-conditions has been granted. For the last
two years periodical attempts have been made in London
to amalgamate these properties and raise more capital
for their further development. So far, these attempts
have ignominiously failed, partly because of the shameful
way in which the mines were manipulated some years
ago, but mostly on account of faith in Lucknow having
departed. And, as there is not much hope of getting any
money in London at present, and no possible chance what-
ever of raising any in Orange, Lucknow’s resurrection may
be much longer delayed, these puff pars, notwithstanding.
Writer would suggest the holding of a revival-meeting to
pray for the ending of the Boer war. That would do
more good than the exhibition of specimens in the streets
of Orange, which always leads to drink.
* * * •
Grassy Gully, N.S.W., has another new man-
ager. If the mine is half as good as his last
report (as printed) it has no equal in the Common-
wealth, much less in N.S.W. “Kept the battery going
almost constantly last week ; place looking well; crushing
good stone. Splendid stone is now coming up every day.
Have opened out on the rich chute and the stone goes
from 20oz. to 30oz. to the ton. This class of stone, with
as little mullock as possible, is now being landed every
day and is ready to crush. Next month I shall have a
splendid result. I think I will be able to keep the battery
going constantly for I have a lot of stone at grass besides
the stone at the battery. There is a face of stone 15ft. by
15ft. which looks like a sovereign—coated with gold.
There is a lot of good stone here which I will have
to leave for a while because of the way the mine has
been worked.” Then there is something concerning
the way the new boss is going to work it : “ Before
another couple of months I will astonish you with the
good results. Will be starting the cyanide plant in a few
days. Am also getting some good gold out of the new
shaft. I have a few tons here ready to crush, and some
splendid-looking ore is coming from here.” This takes
the cake, and the new boss at Grassy Gully must wear the
belt until he is deprived of it by a better man. “20 to
30ozs. to the ton ! and the battery to be kept going on
it 1” Ye gods 1 What is the matter with the market ?
Sellers —paid-ups at 2s. 3d. and no buyers ; contributing
might be given away with a little difficulty; certainly
there are no buyers at any price. And yet while there are
ounce-and-a-half mines in Westralia selling at millions
the Grassy Gully, lock, stock and barrel, has only a
market valuation of £2,000 or £3,000. The public refuses
to be astonished.

* * * *
Mt. Garnet, Chillagoe and Exploration Co. is
mining on a most restricted scale, so that the estimate of
ore in sight is still practically 95,000 tons. “As the Mt.
Garnet Freehold Railway will pass within four miles of
the lease, the directors hope to make satisfactory arrange-
ments for the disposal of the co.’s ore, particularly as it is
an ideal smelting ore. The strictest economy is being
exercised consistent with the proper carrying on
of a certain amount of exploration, and the
directors trust that as soon as the line is
ready for traffic, which is expected to be in
about nine months time, the property will be in a
revenue-producing position.” The same old tale, told in
the same old way. Everything is going to be all right
when another co. builds a railway and starts smelting
other people’s ore—as if it were the usual practice to
carry ore and to smelt it at less than cost, whereas experi-
ence all the world over has shown that it generally means
absorbing all that is in the ore, no matter how much.
And these directors have the audacity to speak of
“ strictest economy,” when they spent in Melbourne in
management during the half-year more than one-fourth
as much as was spent in mining. No explanation of that
fact is given.
* * • •
Mount Garnet Freehold : “ Owing to the short
time No. 1 furnace was running during the half-year
ended June 30, the directors have not this half-year sub-
mitted a working account showing profit and loss re-
sults.” Under these circumstances, every shareholder
can bet his bottom dollar that there are no profit results.
It is also even money that the loss results are something
substantial. If there were any profits, those not only to
the end of June would be shown, but it would be found
not a difficult matter to show what they were from June
up to date. Last week it was published of this mine that
the smelter was doing good work, that the output for the
past month was in the vicinity of 45 tons of copper and
55000 z. silver weekly. “This gives a nice profit,
and the co.’s revenue will be materially in-
creased shortly, as another furnace is to be blown-in
next week.” If this were not a romance the
truth would be revealed in the report, which says there
are over 200,000 tons of ore available, also that the finan-
cial position rendered it necessary to spend a limited
amount of money on mining operations and procuring
regular supplies of ore to the furnaces and permitting of
smelting operations being carried on continuously.
Well, if one smelter gives a “ nice profit,” two will give a
nicer profit, and the more ore smelted the better for
everybody—whereas the report clearly shows that smelt-
ing is done at a loss, and the directors thought it wise to
minimise that loss. Some time ago The Bulletin declared
that it believed this was so on the best of authority. No
official contradiction to that statement was made, and in
this report it is tacitly admitted. Possibly, the running of
two furnaces may do away with the loss, but with the
carriage of coke in and matte out on camel-back there
can’t be much profit. Writer advised some time ago the
shutting down of the furnaces till the starting of the
railway, and if things were clearly explained in the direc-
tors’ report the shareholders would soon see that that is
the course to pursue. The railway will not be
finished for a year, and although it may be a sorry busi-
ness to shut down furnaces after erecting them, it is
better to have a little sorrow than to lose a lot of money.
* * * •
Mt. Garnet Freehold : The directors think that
the electro-magnetic separation of zinc on a commercial
scale has been accomplished, and their reason for drawing
attention to the matter in their half-yearly report is that
a large tonnage of low-grade material containing copper
and zinc exists on the mine, and this ore may now be
turned to profitable account. At last we have an admis-
sion that there is ore in this mine containing a heavy
percentage of zinc, and the thing is only confessed when
there seems some chance of turning such ore into money.
Recent developments in diamond-drill prospect-
ing at Croydon should give great incentive to mining on
the Far-Northern field. Years ago, the Iguana reef, a
big lode which crushed up to lOoz. per ton, was cut out by
a hard wall of granite, variously termed a break,
“ slide ” and “ fault.” In Ophir Block shaft the slide
carried the reef up on its course, thus leading to the
belief that the reef was cut in two by an intrusive mass of
hard granite. This was Geologist Band’s theory, and he
suggested putting down a bore beyond the probable limit
of the supposed intrusive mass, pointing out a suitable
site. A syndicate of 20 Croydon business men was formed
about two years ago, and boring was begun on the site
indicated under the experienced direction of J. I. tow,
formerly well-known in Sydney. Without a hitch Uole
bored to a depth of a quarter-mile, cutting at a
splendid reef formation, carrying numerous gold-bearing
quartz-leaders. This formation was proved 29ft. thick.
Drill thus proved continuity of Iguana lode m the deep
ground beyond the supposed intrusion, and the syndicate,
which has gold-mining leases nearly 50 acres in extent,
talks about sinking a shaft to explore the reef. Estimated
cost of shaft, £20,000.

From Clermont (Q.): Mount George and other
Sandy Creek finds fizzled out ; it is dangerous to talk
ironstone shows in Clermont now. Bedford’s have 15ft.
wide of crushing stuff, and not across it yet ; about
500 tons have been put through, averaging 30dwts. _ Two
local battery proprietors bought the show from original
holders. At Fig Tree another couple of reefs have been
struck showing good gold, and a lot of stone is now being
carted. An Adelaide syndicate has one slag-heap at the
old Copperfield Copper Mine under offer —200,090 tons.
A Gympie man is after the mine.
» * * •
The Star of Hope reef at Copperfield is again
being worked. The trouble in the past has been copper.
A local cyanider seems to have mastered this, put-
ting through 50 tons for 25ozs. smelted gold. At
present the Star has 7ft. of crushing stuff, promising
lOdwts. free gold. Nixon —on same line—is also raising
stone. A mill employee got away with the amalgam
from last crushing. From this show, 100 tons of tailings
cyanided 80oz. Present holders got the mine from a
party of working men, who held the ground for seven
years, during which time they raised 350 tons from a
3ft. reef, and got exemptions whenever they wanted.

Some of the history of Wax. Madden’s
tenacious grip on the Vie. State Governorship
was made known to Parliament the other day. At
His Wax.’s earnest solicitation, ex-Premier
M‘Lean engineered that he should keep the job
until all the Federal pomp and ceremonial were
over; but this was only the thin edge of the
wedge—the Chief Justice’s idea was that if he
kept the Governorship 18 months he would have
it altogether. But the present Government,
which doesn’t seem fond of “John,” stepped in
and probably stopped the Australian States gener-
ally from getting locally-grown figureheads—
which is to be regretted.
* * * *
How the bitter racial hate is brewing in
the black-specked Australian States ! Thus the
Spectator (W.A.):
The Afghan of the back country is, when he dares by
force of numbers to be so, a ruffianly bully, ever ready to
insult the white man, for whom he feels, and is at no
pains to conceal, the most utter contempt. ... He
camps at the wells, water-holes, and soaks, and
pollutes, often wilfully, the supply to prevent its being-
availed of by the despised European. It is not so many
years since one of these alien invaders, after watering his
camels at a soak in the presence of white men waiting to
give their thirsty horses and themselves drink, delibe-
rately drew off his boots and started to wash his dirty
feet in the slowly incoming water. He was promptly shot
dead by one of the prospectors, against whom—if we re-
member rightly—the Attorney-General refused to permit
a bill to be filed.
Give the black man a few more years’ grace of
entry, and no Australian daily paper will
be without its “special reporter” to attend
burnings at the stake by lynch law. The
Australian who boasts in letters to his mother
of the piece of Boer flesh, or of the dead
Boer’s whiskers, he is bringing home as a
trophy, or of how a mate of his deliberately blew
the top of a Boer prisoner’s head off (“ and serve
him right ”), is quite capable of setting light to
kerosened niggers.
SIGNOR TREBELLI.
A Southern Senator who is understood to
cherish a weakness for missionary enterprise
among the heathen, and is therefore opposed to
the white Australia proposals as “ un-Christian,”
“un-British,” etc., may now pose as a person of
experience. Housed in the Senator’s suburban
mansion was a black female waif, aged 17. She
was caught young, and until recently was
a pattern of propriety, and a credit to
her up-bringing. Latterly, however, she
has developed giddy tendencies. Finally,
the savage maiden set the whole plantation at
defiance, and carried on so alarmingly that the
Senator himself had to be sent for. He was in his
place in Parliament, urging the claims of the
colored alien to free admittance to Australia,
when the call to duty arrived. He got home ere
the heathen had made up her mind whether to set
fire to the house, or eat the panic-stricken family
raw y° un £ lady was promptly driven off
the premises, and, at latest, was looking for some
other kind, Christian family to adopt her. Those
in the know are awaiting with much interest the
Senator’s next pronouncement on a white Aus-
tralia.
* * * *
It would be hard to think of a meaner
crowd than that Petersham (Sydney) mob which
tried to deal out a push death to one Maslin, last
week. Maslin’s wife, after 20 years’ matrimony
and six children, left him. He searched out his
wife, and, on her refusing to come home, shot her
with a revolver. Running away, he was followed
by a big crowd, which didn’t dare to rush him, but
kept always at a respectful distance. When,
however, the police arrested and handcuffed
Maslin, then the brave citizens came to close
quarters, striking and kicking him. It was a
noble way of showing sympathy with woman’s
rights.
* * * *
In regard to the scourge of consumption :
A mail steamer which reached Australia the other
day had buried en route two phthisis cases, and
brought to port two other consumptives nearing
death. After barring the colored man out of
Australia, the Commonwealth will have to legis-
late against the too-white tuberculous man.
* * * »
From British Medical Journal:—
' " A well-known medical man in Geneva lost a
daughter of 17. In perfect health till 1892, she began to
waste away in ’93, and in ten months died. A post-mortem
examination revealed extensive tuberculous disease of
the intestines. How had the disease been contracted ?
There was no family history. But it appeared that every
Sunday the doctor and his family spent the day at a small
estate on the hills, and one of the greatest delights of
this young- girl was to drink milk fresh from the cow. It
was proved afterwards that four out of the five cows on
the estate were tuberculous ; and two of these were found
to have tuberculous disease of the udders.
Which seems to hardly fit in with Koch’s recent
statement that the risk of infection by tuberculous
milk and ox-flesh was so small that he did not
deem it advisable to take any measures against it.

has managed to dodge an appearance at an in-
quest.
• * * *
“ M.C.” joins in the population racket to
cast another stone at “Father in Israel ” :
It is the disinclination of young husbands and wives to
have children, not the cost of doctor and nurse (combined
generally under £l5), which causes the decline of the
birth-rate. Married couples say they can’t afford to keep
them. Foolish people ! In the long run they will pay
more ; he out of his pocket for medical fees, she in pains
and aches. lam a trained obstetric nurse, and think the
comparison between the modern, scientific nurse and the
“ kind motherly person ”of old simply absurd. What did
the latter know of anatomy, of how to avoid puerperal
fever, of treatment of hemorrhage, that frequent and
dreaded incident of childbirth ? I have a relative who
has been 20 years an invalid through the incompetent
woman who nursed her with her first child. Convincing
proof of the value of the trained nurse may be found in
the records of the big maternity homes, when, since the
era of modern scientific aseptic nursing, the rate of mor-
tality has shown a splendid decrease.
» * » *
The story of Mr. and Mrs. Dixon, of
Newstead (Vic.), amounts to an idyll of the Old
Age Pension Act. Dixon was 86, and his wife, a
comparatively vigorous female of 50 odd, used to
collect his pension for him, leaving him at home
with his legs tied to the sofa, lest the poor dear
ancient might fall off and hurt ’isself. On 14th
Sept. Mrs. Dixon bought a quart bottle of whisky
and some chlorodyne, and that night neighbours
heard sounds of domestic ructions in the Dixon
hut. Subsequently the old man was found dead,
with “70 to 100 wounds” on his naked body, and an
effusion of blood on the brain, probably the result
of blows inflicted with “a batten studded with
nails.” Two grey hairs and sundry clots of blood
were sticking to the batten. Mrs. Dixon had
some stabs on her hands and head, and she
explained that the injuries to both parties were
inflicted by the infirm old man “in a frenzy,”
though the neighbours had certainly heard Dixon
cry—“Oh, mother, don’t!" Last act of all:
Mrs. Dixon died at Castlemaine hospital a week
after her husband had yielded up his ghost and
his pension. Thus was the detective in charge of
the case defeated in his intention to “cast a
strong net of evidence around Mrs. Dixon.”
* * * *
The Long Arm : Three months ago, a
young Sydneyite was run over the leg by an
electric tram and died from shock. The other day,
bis mate was run over the leg by a steam tram,
and died from shock. The trams have their
uses, but they are fast thinning out Sydney’s
population.
* • • *
Geelong (Yic.) goodies are trying their
surreptitious hardest to sneak back the liquor-
license for a very big hotel, which has languished
since they took it over with great eclat in boom-
time to run as a teetotal show. A similar move was
made at Melbourne Grand about Exhibition time,
when the fussy, good people publicly rent the
license of that big pub. into fragments—and were
very sorry afterwards.
THE COLOR BAR.
dead-beat : “I throwed up me job in the
country, missus, to join the bushmen’s kin-
tinyent an’ then found I couldn’t except the
kinditions.”
YOUTHFUL HOUSEWIFE: “Poor fellow ! What
did they want, you to do?”
D.B.: “ They, wanted ter paint me nose kharki
color, an’ I wasn’t takin’ any.”
The harsh conditions attached to old-age
pensions are severely felt in country districts.
Kiandra (N.S.W.) applicants, for instance, have
to travel 100 miles of rugged country to Tumut to
obtain their pittance. Why can’t the pension-
claims, once approved, be paid at the local P. 0.,
thus saving much hardship and expense to the old
people ?
* * * *
There was humor in barrister Coldham’s
cross-examination of Prof. Morris at Melbourne
City Court as to the character of certain French
books which had been tabooed by the Customs
department. After the Prof.’s statement that the
books “demoralised his mind, and made him feel
the need of a bath, ” Coldham proceeded to ask,
gravely, “ Do you think they would affect educated
men of high moral character ? ” Audible smiles
throughout the Court.
Certain evidence given in aforesaid case has
reminded many ex-’Varsity students that, years
agone, one of the books prescribed for French
honors at a certain Australian University was
Aucassin et Nicolette, an example of old French of
the 12th or 13th century, which, for red-hot
detail, can hold its own with the “bluest” of
them. Yet, before his honor class, which one
year consisted of one man and one woman, a cer-
tain professor went through every word of the
blistering stuff.

* • * *
One N.S.W. hotel-keeper points out a
possible further move on the part of the brewer
when ties are abolished: he may set himself to
acquire hotel-leases and then hold the publican as
a weekly tenant at his absolute mercy. It seems
necessary to forbid the licensing of a house leased
by a brewer; and then the brewer might hold his
leases, and work them as ties, through a dummy.

The last material relic of the Duke’s visit
to Melbourne was swept away the other day,
when the Princess Bridge arch was demolished.
The original intention was to let the big gewgaw
block the thoroughfare for a year, but it got dry-
rotted, the tacks of its covering gave way, and
loose strips of zinc began to rattle about it with
a gallows noise. What has become of the project,
enthusiastically taken up by several leading citi-
zens, to erect the arch permanently in marble at
a cost of £20,000 ?

cently a man sentenced to three
months for stealing Is. 3d.-
worth of old iron asked the
magistrates—“ And what about
the toffs who sneak their thou-
sands, and get no more ? ”
What, indeed !

“ Melb.” : Here is some-
thing which would be ruth-
lessly cut out of any local paper.
Last Sunday night I went to
St. Francis’s (R.C.). Lonsdale-
st., where they had Gounod’s
Oratorio of Tobias, with pyra-
mids of threepenny-bits on the
plates at the doors, and such
a jam, of (say) 1300 people
inside, that every passage was
choked right up. Though near
the door, I thought to myself
“ I’m a goner if there’s a nanic,”
not being in the first flush of
youth or health. It was truly
awful to note the slowness with
which this clot of populace
extricated itself from the
building. My thoughts ran
upon that unparalleled
holocaust in the San-
tiago Jesuit Church.
Wesley Church, also
in Lonsdale-st., is
nearly as bad as St.
Francis’s, where the
dreadful trap of the porch
ought to be knocked away
instanter. The Board of
Health is absurdly exigent
about new buildings. The old
ones, especially churches, are
just allowed to rip, till they horri-
bly Hordernise. Why not prosecute
a church, as well as a playhouse, for
overcrowding?
A beautiful piece of Bumbleism. On the
day of the announcement of the death of President
M‘ Ainlev, flags were flying at half-mast on every
public building in South Melbourne, except the
town hall. The omission, which was tardily
corrected, was due to an official inadvertence.
Some time ago a council committee, to check the
indiscriminate hoisting of the flag, drew up a list
of persons, from the sovereign downwards, whose
decease was to be signified by the flag at half-mast.
The list was confined entirely to distinguished
persons in the British Empire. The council, in
preparing the, list, was somewhat conservative as
to the display of official bereavement. If a
councillor or his wife dies the flag is placed at
half-mast, but for council officers this token of
respect is restricted to the town clerk and city
surveyor. Thus, when the city treasurer (Mr. J.
Sayers) recently died, after a long and honorable
service, the flag was not displayed.

Four dipsomaniacs of means, hailing from
the one Maoriland town, went through a curing
process at the Hagey Institute some time ago.
Within the last year three of them met death in
one form or other ; then the fourth remarked that
it was time for him to strike camp also. On a
recent morning he was found dead with his head
in a tub containing Bin. of water.

trial was set down at Melbourne, and, after 11
months finality was reached. Accused was re-
presented by Purves, Q.C., and his defence

The Gold-Jubilee Exhibition at Bendigo
promises to be all, or nearly all, that Bendigo has
hop.ed for. Space-applications came in with a
rush at the last moment, and settled any doubt as
to the show being well-stocked when all the ex-
hibits are there. As for an announcement that
the gardens and grounds and cool palm walks will
be brilliantly illuminated, the management will
probably see the wisdom of turning down the
lights in unconsidered places. An exhibition
ought to cater for Sunday-school patronage in the
dim, religious manner which ever appeals to the
Nonconformist conscience. Secretary G. V.
Allen must certainly provide breaks in the mono-
tony of illumination. Allen, by the way, is the
same George who was one-time leader of the staff
on Melbourne Age, and the hand of the wily
pressman shows itself in all parts of the advt. save
the reference to those “cool palm walks.”

The sort of letter an up-to-date lover
writes to his girl nowadays. He was landed for
£lOO and costs for breach of promise in Melb.,
last week :
My own darling Mag.—l was beginning to think you
had forgotten me. . . You were saying you were lonely
and sad, and I, darling, missed you, too. . . I often think
how nice it must be to have a good little wife, and a nice,
snug little home, for I know you love me dearly.
(Kisses.) . . I don’t think I would care to settle in
Colac. I shall certainly pick some other place for us to
live in and rear our family (which must be a small one).
. . . Poor little Fairy’s boy is far away, but may give
you a surprise any day. Now, my poor little love, I
think I have told you all. Good-bye, my sweet little
pet.—Your own true lover, Easton Johnstone.
Then he went and married another girl, with
money ; and the sweet little innocent, who was
to have only a small family, was l“ f t lamenting.

Vic. Factories’ Act Commission has
brought to light some horribly unappetising facts
re restaurant food and its cooking. One
witness said he had worked in a smah
kitchen with nine other employees whose
backs got much baked by the heat of
the adjacent stove. At some restaurants
the perspiring slaves work 14 hours a
day in the mysterious region where the
meats and fixings are prepared. Men
get as little as os. a week and their
food, the food of which they
know so much. And the pro-
prietor of one such place goes
to church three times every
Sunday, said the witness.
One can imagine that pro-
prietor chipping in with a
greasy “Amen” when
the parson reads “Take
no thought for the
morrow, what ye shall
eat.”

A Melb. sign : “ Beds 4d., clean beds 6d.”
The proposals for the building of the new
Sydney railway-station, right alongside the old,
inconvenient site whose remoteness has made Syd-
ney curse for 35 years, is accompanied by a scheme
f or the necessary reconstruction of surrounding
streets, which, it is said, will take a dozen years to
complete. And in it all there isn’t a mention of
the extension of the railway into the city, or
the construction of a good suburban system;
a ll that is an extra. Long ere the new idea
is half finished, the demand for the city
extension will be as loud as ever, and the pres-
sure on the streets through the railway traffic
being transferred to the tram-cars at Redfern will
be worse than ever. And then when the city ex-
tension has to be considered it will be found that
calling it an extension instead of a main line
hasn’t made the selection of the route a whit
easier. The only difference will be that, by the
new O’Sullivan policy, the extension will leave a
big, new, costly station behind it at Redfern
instead of an old, small, cheap one. Everything
else, barring the huge debt contracted for building
the new station in the old, inaccessible suburb, will
be as it was. The question of the up-to-date
branch system to the eastern and western suburbs,
and the convenient city station, and the connec-
tion with North Sydney are all left where they
were. Practically all the things that the public
has been clamoring about for 35 years are left
where they were.

passengers booked to go from Sydney per Harlech
Castle back to the land of burning farms only 49
turned up. Many of these admitted that they
were not going back so much for another term of
war as to settle in S’Africa for good, in the hope
that life there will prove “more congenial and
profitable.”

The newest girl-romancer—a Me'bourne
girl of 15 —broke out in a comparatively harm-
less spot. She and her sister, aged 11. were sent
out after dark one Saturday evening, to buy
groceries, at an outlying Melbourne suburb. Mary
had a £l-note concealed in her stocking, and
brought back the change honestly enough the same
way. She also brought back a terrible tale of how
she had found a half-sovereign—-how she was
bringing the coin home rejoicing—how four large,
desperate men, all masked, with three revolvers
and a knife, had leaped over a hedge at a lonely
part of the road, and, with horrible cries, de-
manded money or ahuman sacrifice—how M ary had
pacified them with the Providential half-sov., and
then they had fled across the fields, using terrible
language. Mary’s mother believed the tale, and,
after extracting the change from Mary’s stocking,
put the house in a state of siege till Sund iy
morning. Then she reported the blood-curdling
incident, and Mary was interviewed by the police
on her way home from Sunday-school. She
adhered to her tale of horror bravely enough, but
the small sister, aged 11, broke down, and admitted
it was all a work of art. Still, it was a harmless
romance compared to the usual kind, and Mary
might almost as well have been left to enjoy it in
peace. Apparently, the Australian girl must
invent something of this kind once at least in her
life, and the sort of story she invents generally
threatens some man’s neck.
* * * *
At Carlton (Melbourne), the other day,
Charles Rose, aged 81, was fined £lO, in default
three months, for “using profane language in a
public place.” Defendant had been addressing a
crowd in the Carlton (Melb.) Gardens, and was
alleged to have “ made use of profane words about
Christ.” The prosecution was instituted by per-
sons who had debated with accused on such sub-
jects, and who admitted having called him a
gorilla, an ape, an ignoramus, and a liar. This
Carlton Bench is notorious for its lenient treat-
ment of the committers of brutal assaults, and
not long ago it let off with a fine of five shillings a
ruffian who, by kicking a pregnant woman, had
caused premature confinement, the death of the
infant, and permanent injury to the mother. Yet
it penalised in 40 times the amount a man who
declared his disbelief in the Christian reli-
gion (which nobody practises), time to pay being
refused, although time is always allowed to the
beastly Melbourne larrikins. The case was simply
one of organised persecution of a Freethinker by
alleged Christians.

Now, where did this happen ? A carriage,
heading a procession that passed before the Yorks,
in Australia, contained, says an English paper,
“the nearest approach to a double of the King
that Australia could supply.” He was clad in
royal robes, with crown and sceptre, a Queen
Alexandra accompanied him, and “the Duke’s
amazement at this astonishing spectacle could not
be concealed.”
* * * •
Perhaps because of the threat of restrictive
laws, there has been a sudden influx of Hindoos
and other colored peoples to Australia lately.
They are swelling into Victoria especially, for the
State immigration laws there are most lax.
Agents who let slum houses in Carlton and Fitz-
roy to Oriental nigs, find it impossible to provide
all the accommodation asked for. Even with the
system of sleeping ten in a 10-foot room the pre-
sent houses usually devoted to Hindoos have
E roved inadequate to hold them. Fresh places
ave accordingly been found at rents which few
prosperous white citizens could afford to pay.

A predominant factor in the Australian
Star (Sydney) proprietary is big brewer Toohey,
M.L.C. The following, which appeared in the
Star’s leading columns t’other night, will, there-
fore, be read with added interest: —
Some few persons, whose intellect has been weakened
by long courses of unrestrained indulgence, may mourn,
hut all right-thinking persons will rejoice, that there is
likely to be a shortage in the supply of the peculiar curse
of Australia. Of course we allude to that bane of
Australia —TEA. Tea-drinking is the national vice.
Morn, noon, night, before breakfast, at breakfast, lunch,
dinner, tea, and in between, the fateful and hateful pot
occupies the chief place at the board. It would be easy
to show that the vile practice of tea-swilling fills our
hospitals, lunatic asylums, and prisons, and has brought
misery into thousands of households. Advanced
scientists tell us that the difference between virtue and
vice is largely a matter of stomach and digestion. Tea
and tannin are the deadly foes of digestion. How can
the tea-bibber, who has no digestion, and is merely a
walking mass of dyspepsia, be virtuous ? It can’t be
done. Happily, the time has now arrived when a great
and united effort may be made to purge this fair young
country from the loathsome incubus, which is sucking its
life-blood, poisoning our sons and daughters, and weaken-
ing the race to so lamentable a degree that there seems
to be a more than possibility that future generations may
be born with a handle, a spout, and a lid.
A light-hearted Vic. woman, with a grown-
up family, put on liat and cape t’other evening
and started out to meet her “ boy ” —a fascinating
foreigner. Husband and a son were waiting
adjacent and duly followed. They saw her meet the
lover and stroll off towards a park. The sight of
this philandering doesn’t seem to have aroused the
lawful husband and son to any extent. Anyhow,
though they had a club apiece, they merely pur-
sued slowly at a respectful distance, till the lover,
brandishing a revolver, ordered them to retire or
be “potted.” They retired in good order, and
next day summoned the lover for illegally carrying
fire-arms. The husband admitted in court that he
had a pot of tar ready to be spread over the
foreigner at a convenient opportunity ; also if he
caught him in the gardens with his wife he pro-
posed to strip him and send him home nude ; but
he would do nothing rash or hasty. It was also
stated that two of the lady’s sons declined to take
any serious offence at her conduct, and held that
it was none of their business. Altogether it seems
to have been a somewhat languid family.
STAR-SET.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
No star sinks but the earth grows darker,
No man dies but a love doth perish ;
Farewell, O Star, in the far night sinking ;
Farewell, Dear One, whom we sought to cherish.
Courage ! we say, all stars must follow
Yon fair star that has left us lightless ;
And eyes that weep, like the eyes past weeping,
Soon shall be dust, amid dust, and sightless.
O Star, far-setting, thy gleam is guidance
To ships through a peril of night-seas sailing :
O Life, slow-creeping o’er Death’s dark border,
Leave thy love lest a heart go wailing !
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
FAIR ICONOCLAST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To me your love was heaven ; and I entered.
I built a world-beyond on your assurance ;
Since you were mine, I could not doubt; I centred
My faith, my fate, upon your love’s endurance.
Now, false one, that I find the proposition
A pitiful, ridiculous delusion :
Must still the soul accept my definition
And draw the only logical conclusion ?
Berlin.

Mr. C. J. Saunders has received the
temporary appointment of Acting-Member of
N.S.W. Public Service Board, vice Wilson, in-
disposed. The rise of Mr. Saunders has been
rapid. When the P. S. Board delegated the
grading of the Depts., some five years ago, to a
sub-Board of three, Mr. Saunders, then only an
inspector in the Lands Dept., was chosen as chair-
man. He pleased the Board so much that he was
promoted, and promoted, until, when Federation
made complete changes in the Customs Dept, he
was made one of the Commissioners of Taxation
under the Treasury.
A Bill has been introduced in M.L.
Parliament making it an offence for any person
under 16 to smoke tobacco in any form in public,
the penalty for the first offence being 55., and 10s.
for any subsequent breach of the law. A penalty
of £5 is provided for supplying tobacco to the
young person, but no conviction is to be entered
“if a medical officer certifies that the using of
tobacco is beneficial to the health of the young
person.” The Bill has no chance of becoming law
for several years, M.L. Govt, being too busy now
attending to the navigation of the party vessel.

bachelors, impressed in their own despite by the

admiration and alarm, “Another victim to the
wiles of the P. D. figure. ” This cant phrase is a
fine testimony to the man-alluring qualities of the
P.D. Marriages, nowadays, are mostly made in
the P.D. Corset Factory.*
