A WOMANS LETTER.
Sydney, October 1, 1901.
My Dear Arini, —
Sydney has not been a valley of quite dry
bones since my last letter. The Upper House
threw us a conversational bone with much tough
meat on it by a vote of 26 to 21, when it rejected
the Woman Suffrage Bill. Mr. Wise, in an excel-
lent speech, pointed out that the measure had
been endorsed by the general elections and carried
in two Assemblies by large majorities. But out
it went ignominiously, and after it filed Miss Rose
Scott, Mrs. Martel, Miss Golding, Miss Vallen-
tine, and a host of its friends. But they didn’t
look downcast, only disgusted, and they said in
effect, “ The morrow is also ours.”
Another bone —much pleasanter gnawing this
one—has been dropped by a clergyman, the Rev.
S. S. Tovey, in a discussion on the employment
of barmaids. He urged that it might not be quite
a good thing to abolish barmaids, as their presence
behind the bars may tend to elevate those places.
Anyhow, he would rather see a woman behind a
bar than a barman with a revolver, as was the case
in America. Now this is really a novel view of the
question. Given the hint the barmaid may aim
at hitching her waggon to a star. And, in
time, a new type of barmaid may evolve—
women after the stamp of Hypatia. If the
calling of barmaid come within the scope of the
university woman graduate, then would begin the
ascension of man. At the counter-lunch she could
read him snatches of Emerson, Matthew Arnold,
and Philip Gilbert Hamerton. She could dilute
Buchanan’s Blend with the persiflage of Schlegel
or Kant. And the ascended man would recipro-
cate with little presents such as a “Treatise on
the Categorical Imperative.” No more bangles
or jewelled hat-pins—just books and flowers. The
university woman-graduate would be a perfect
goddessend to the hordes of young men—who
would stay away from bars for evermore. The
movement would have far-reaching consequences.
It would go some way towards solving the
domestic-servant problem, and would put some
thousands of able-bodied “generals” and house-
maids on the market.
Concerning the domestic-servant problem, 700
house-workers in Melbourne have put a requisition
before the State Peacock. Their specific requests
are that their hours of work may be restricted
to 60 hours a week (ten-hours a day); also they
demand a weekly half-holiday, and every Sunday
off. And already a group of matrons, scared by
the “ Sunday-off ” clause, are taking measures to
form a Guild of Household Dames, such as has
been formed in London, to encourage “ women of
gentle birth ” to enter the “profession” of
domestic servants. The principal of the training-
home for Household Dames is overwhelmed with
applications from would-be-employers. It is
desired to give the gently-born as much as possible
the social status of trained nurses. They have
fixed hours of leisure, and wear a dress of Liberty
design, with apron and guild badge.
That magnetic lady, the laterally-extensive
Signora Benedetto, appeared on Saturday night as
Elvira in “Ernani,” and got a loud and long
reception when she came on, “ moving as if on
inyisible skates.” Doesn’t that just describe the
gait of some very stout but graceful women 1 Her
first dress, of perfectly plain pink satin, is em-
broidered with gold; her white bridal satin glitters
with silver tracery, and with both gowns ropes of
pearls encircle the waist. Larizza’s (Ernani’s)
wedding garments are as well worth chronicling
as any woman’s. Cloth of silver, white em-
broidered silk continuations and a large diamond
order—for valor, no doubt, in the “ bail-up” busi-
ness. Even when in working dress, Ernani wears
the Order of the Golden Fleece, a yellow sheep
hanging by the middle of its spine, on his broad
breast. Ernani is, of course, a noble robber with
a magnificent castle.
Melba asks our Mr. Williamson for £5OO a
night! And elsewhere she gets it. Her Majesty’s
theatre holds, at present prices and when crowded,
only £3OO worth of body and soul, and Australians
wouldn’t pay more to hear the Angel Israfel.
£5OO a night! What a fearful loss to Melba if she
should ever run a nail into her voice.
Miss Mary M‘Camley, an 18-year-old Queens-
land girl, with a soprano voice of great range and
power, gave a concert under the direction of her
teacher, Mr. Burns Walker, last week. Several
of the heavy operatic numbers she rendered were
too ambitious for so young a voice, and a voice
which might, if Miss M‘Cam ley doesn’t go on
training backwards, bring in £5OO a night, ought
to be tenderly nursed. Miss Lydia Larner played
a brilliant composition, “Danse Oriental,” of her
own, and Miss Marion Tweddle gave a capital
recitation of the court scene from “ The Merchant
of Venice.” The calm judical tones of Portia and
the harsh voice of Shylock were most cleverly
differentiated.
Now, isn’t it easier to ask for “smoked lamb”
than for “ Rulepolse ” ? Yet in Danish the two
are synonymous. Also, “Roget Sild ” means
smoked herring. You could get these Scandi-
navian dainties and plenty others at the late
Scandinavian fair in aid of the Cot Fund of the
Children’s Hospital. Acting-Governor Darley
declared, in his fete-opening speech, that he was
proud of the Scandinavian descent of the Royal
family. Then he went and bought two little
statuettes, copies of Thorwaldsen’s sculptures.
Most of the Scandinavian ladies resident in
Sydney worked their best for the fete, and dressed
themselves and their assistants in picturesque
national dresses. Mrs. Marcker wore the holiday
garb of Bornholm, a little island in the Baltic,
black satin skirt and bodice, with Oriental ribbon,
and a big, wide bonnet of pleated muslin and lace
and pink silk covered with flowers. Mrs. Tom
Marshall was an Armager girl (Armager is the
cabbage-garden of Copenhagen), and wore a short,
black skirt, covered all round with a blue apron,
a black satin bodice, and a poke-brand of bonnet,
lined with blue. Mrs. Pauss had the Norwegian
stall, and Mrs. Boesen, Mrs. Marcker, and Miss
Vallentine ran the Danish stall. Miss Aileen
Knaggs, one of Mrs. Boesen’s assistants and that
lady’s future daughter-in-law, wore the dress
of “Dyveke,” a pathetic, romantic figure in
twelfth-century Danish history, and somewhat
similar to the fair Rosamond of England.
Cranbrook, in Rose Bay (the beautiful residence
of the late Hon. James White), which has been
going begging for a tenant since the Harvey
Lovelys sold off and quitted it some two years
ago, has been taken by the Government for the
State ruler’s residence. It has a superb view to
seaward, and fine grounds, and its rent (£4OO
yearly) doesn’t represent a fraction of the interest
on the money spent upon it.
Those two large oak-framed portraits of the
Duke and Duchess of York, which Mr. Newman,
of George-street, sent to the King, have been
acknowledged by cable to Mr. See. And a letter
to Mr. Newman is being rocked in the cradle of
the deep. Edward VII. thinks the portraits
excellent. That of the Duchess shows her stand-
ing at a table, one hand on a silver casket, the
other holding a spray of roses. She wears a black
brocaded dress and half-a-mile of pearl necklace.
Lord Hopetoun celebrated his forty-first birth ■
day a week or so ago. He doesn’t look his years,
not by five or six. His Countess, on the contrary,
appears more than her3—at least since the Yorks
stayed with her. Whenever a Eogtown society
paper has occasion to refer to Lady Hopetoun it
prefixes her with “Irish.” “The Governor-
General’s popular Irish wife,” for instance, or
“ handsome Irish wife.” Madame Hersey is not
Irish ; she is Scotch, as her consort (that’s a good
word), once told his entertainers at a banquet,
and if she were Irish what of it? It’s not more
distinctive than being English or Scotch. Now,
if Lady Hopetoun were Japanese one could
understand the insistence of her nationality in
newspaper mention.
I had a look at the inter-State lawn tennis
tournament on Saturday and found it tamer than
a pet rabbit. A bitter wind blew over the cricket-
ground. The champions hitched their principal
garments constantly after the fashion of the
Musical Burglar at the Palace, and a brilliant
square of women sat in sixpenny chairs around—
no asquare—-the tennis court. It was a floral
square, a pink-and-blue muslin silk and lace
square, with men lolling on the chair backs and
men serried in the rear. It wasn’t quite the kind
of square that Fuzzy Wuzzy broke, and it looked
chilled, and longed for its winter clothes. I used
to play tennis and noughts and crosses and such
exciting games for years. Now I’m trying to in-
vent a game—we want a new game badly—that
will combine the best features of tennis, chess and
football.
Genial Dicky Speight, who died in W.A.,
had a large family. Of his daughters, Mrs.
Lancelot Gordon was the prettiest, and she was
gifted with a splendid soprano voice which would
have brought her fame anywhere had she listened
to business offers. Her husband, a son of General
Conway Gordon, was a rather wild boy before he
left Fogtown to settle down at Mildura to lemon-
growing and the management of Lord Ranfurly’s
ranch.
I have to acknowledge the first number of a
paper called the Kat, edited and illustrated by
Sydney Smith, “ for amusement and educational
purposes.” Its editor, who is a son of the manager
of the Australia Hotel, has reached the palsied
age of 15 years. The Kat will mew every fort-
night, and it is a penny a mew, or 6d. for a quar-
ter’s mewing.
A very fashionable and haughty— not to say
haw-hawty—Sydney club gave a ball the other
night, and, as is customary, each member
had the privilege of nominating two non-member
guests, subject to the approval of the committee.
The guest—a big business man—nominated by
one member wrote to the secretary thanking him
for “the tickets,” and adding that he would be
glad to send four dozen of beer—colonial !—as a
contribution to the supper. When the unhappy
nominator heard this he almost died.
Brough’s new play, “ Sweet and Twenty,” will
be popular with everyone of that style and
age, as well as with .sweet though seventy. Miss
Emma Temple looks the part well, and dresses in
pretty pinks, cornflowery blues, and whites. Her
hair, much crimped, is parted in the centre, and
hangs in a half-loose tail down her back. Mrs.
Brough, as the old family servant, makes up as a
very sweet, barely middle-aged woman in a black
frock, lace apron and mob cap. '
The Fair in aid of the Home for Destitute Boys,
Westmead, will be opened on Saturday next by
Cardinal Moran at the Town Hall. Messrs.
Quinlan, Hepburn, and Bartlett, of Lyndhurst-
chambers, are hon. secs.
Mr. Hugh M'Kernan, the editor and proprietor
of a W.A. paper called the Vindicator and
Matrimonial World, is a man who has suffered
for years from some awful grievance against
society in general, and Michael Davitt in particu-
lar. Considerations for the peace of mind and
happiness of a possible wife kept him a bachelor
during this fiery-furnace time. “But now his
character is vindicated ; the tables are turned on
his enemies, ” and he is free to look a floral toque
under the brim and clasp a maiden’s hand. But,
to be perfectly impartial, to give every free woman
of his acquaintance, and all the unfettered women
of the Commonwealth a chance, Mr. M‘Kernan
offers himself for competition in marriage after
the manner of a “Tatt’s” sweep. The event is
for £lO,OOO, in tickets of £1 each. Ladies over 30
are disqualified as being “lazy, or they would
have married before.” All tickets remaining un-
sold may be purchased by one woman. The winner
need not claim, the prize. The fashion of the
drawing will be quite above-board—a marble cor-
responding with a numbered ticket, a barrel and a
long stick. “ The conditions having been fulfilled
I will marry the drawer in the usual way,” says
Mr. M‘Kernan, who seems to be most dreadfully
in earnest. Photographic reproductions, show-
ing Mr. M‘Kernan with and without a beard, are
printed in the Vindicator so that the girls of the
Commonwealth may have a chance of admiring
the prize.

Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ The Gov.-General, back from Bananaland,
showed a small, wizened, parchment-like face at
the Hunt Club races on baturday. The trip seems
to have benefited him very little. Even top-
coated, his figure looked gaunt and reedy. Still,
lie was cheery, and cracked jokes with all and
sundry, lhe Exs. arrived before the crowd—
°‘ice more evading a reception stare. A few of
the Naicest, arriving at the eleventh hour, found
the Countess’ broad back confronting them from
the rails beside the judge’s box, beyond the special
Ladies’ Reserve ; and most of the day the cold-
shouldering lady remained there, chatting with
Messrs. Miller and G. Chirnside, or the horsey
members of the suite, until at last the ruler of
Moonee Valley brought an easy chair from the
discarded tent, and there her Haughtiness
perched, surrounded by male flunkeys. The
Naicest were raging.
“A negligent grey costume, a pale biscuit cloak,
akin to a belted dressing gown, and a black tulle
toque with pink roses for its fetching frontage,
comprised the Countess’s outfit. It wasn’t smart,
it wasn’t becoming; it was almost careless.
at • i S cruellest cut of all, its carelessness.
Neither “Cliveden” nor “ Rupertswood ” was
represented, so that showy frocks were an un-
known quantity. Mrs. Donald Mackinnon looked
well, though her dressing was of a nondescript
order. The Barr-Smiths were the frilliest of the
women in the gathering. Several summer frocks
were given an airing by leading dazzlers of the
stage and turf, the most notable being a wistaria
blue costume with trappings of priceless lace,
from the ventilation-collar to the silk-lined skirt-
hem.
“ ‘ Better-not ’ Wally wore the deepest collar at
the races that has ever been seen on any neck save
Will Whitburn’s or that of a black brother of
the music-halls. This territory of white starch
sawed his ear lobe every time the splintered
wearer turned his head, so when it became too
painful to use his neck as a pivot, Wally kept his
eyes to the front, and avoided all flank move-
ments. Capt. Corbet, the most rakish horseman
who has ever tailed on to an Ex., wore fashionable
riding-pants, and took more than one turn down
the straight with Godfrey Watson. Talking of
riding-wear, George Lorimer took the breath out
of all brother Nimrods at the Point to Point
Steeplechase, when he appeared in baggy checks
—the very latest in London astrides.
Mayoress Gillott, in her way of receiving, is
a striking contrast to her ailing predecessor. The
present Mayoress is delighted with the responsi-
bilities and honors (?) of the office. Last week
she could scarcely restrain her joy in acknow-
ledging the smiles, nods, stares, gapes, and bobs
of the multitude. Mayoress M‘Eacharn used to
give a martyred smile on her friends, and turn a
countenance of care on the mob. Miss Wilson
Barrett, who religiously attends all the Melb
gatherings, stood at the side of the dais, with the
air of a reviewer, or a novice at dress-chronicling.
The daughter of the Barrett seemingly goes
through life on a tour of inspection, but, so far,
has never had anything to remark concerning her
observations. Some day, when her stock-taking
season is at an end, she may show her wares in a
typical Barrett window of wide front.
Olive Kingston, the Dolores of Australiennes,
was worth a second inspection in a Frenchy cos-
tume of deep strawberry tone and a delicious toque
man even deeper shade. The demure brunette
has the Parisian’s art of looking as if her clothes
were part of her character.
“ It was a hot enough crush in the main hall,
yet only a mere scratch of martyrdom beside the
grilling in the tea-and-bun room. The tide bore
many in and out again, affording them as refresh-
ment only a glimpse of waves of people seething
round a shore piled up with used cups and saucers,
broken glasses, cake crumbs, and squashed sweets.
Once a Senator cried : ‘ Something wet and cold,
waiter, for a fainting lady.’ When the dose of
champagne arrived upon the scene, the anxious
Senator promptly swallowed it himself. He was
his own fainting lady.
“ Philharmonic concert (Wednesday, Sept. 25)
included Hamish MacCunn’s ‘Lay of the Last
Minstrel.’ This interesting work, ‘ excellent
though not desperately original,’ resembles that
nursery favorite, ‘ The Story with a Cake In It,’
inasmuch as it deals with popular themes, depict-
ing to the ear by musical sounds love, war,
chivalry, superstitious fear and patriotism. The
Philharmo chorus and orchestra did their work
well, especially in the eerie grave-snatching bit,
but two of the four principals were regrettably
weak. Armes Beaumont treated his hearers to
such a sweet, plaintive note now and then, that
his weakness in the bulk of the idyllic tenor music
seemed all the more lugubrious by contrast.
Nellie IMcClelland, as substitute for IMrs. Palmer,
bravely tackled Lady Buceleuch’s soprano defiance’
butasparrow shouldn’t essay the scream of an eagle.
Williamson’s dark, vociferous soprano Benedetto
would sound well as Lady Buccleuch, I guess.
Horace Stevens held up his end comfortably as
Cranston, and Allie Mattinson as Margaret
gave a simple, sincere, round-voiced rendering of
‘ True Love. ’
The audience seemed less thrilled by the
* Last Minstrel ’ than at a former concert by the
‘ Damnation of Faust.’ We’re a melo-dramatic
ot, 1 fancy, loving our pleasures not only sad but
horrible, with lashings of spectacular or melodic
blood and brimstone, and lavings of histrionic
wailing and gnashing of teeth.
“Colonel Campbell deserves the V.C. for his
heroic action at the end of the Philharmonic
Society’s concert. The Countess (but not the
Earl) was present and chatted quite gaily through
all the items with His Wax., though she seemed
most uncommunicative as far as his better-half
was concerned. The concert over, their Excel-
lencies proceeded to form the usual procession.
Then the gallant Colonel was seen vaulting over
benches, and with outstretched arms and excited
gestures trying to prevent the ruffled spouse of
His Wax. from moving along the passage. Mur-
murs of horror filled the air as the intrepid matron
quietly brushed away the danger-signaller and-
swept forward with Waxellency, thus taking prece-
dence of the Countess. The procession advanced,
headed by ‘ John’s ’ noble shirt-front; after him
the partner of his joys ; then the Countess, biting
her lips to hide her amusement, treading on and
tripping over the voluminous chiffon frills of Her
Wax’s train. The question is—what sort of time
did Col. Campbell have when their vice-regalities
got him alone ?
“ ‘ Fast Lady ’ won the third place in the recent
‘ Rupertswood ’ Bracelet Stakes, a sporting-item
provocative of many smiles.
“ ‘ Sweet simplicity ’ seems to be the prevailing
dress sentiment of the hour ; and so much the
worse for poor people, because flimsy materials,
costing more to make up and trim than substantial
ones, droop far sooner. White muslin frocks are
much in evidence in imported wardrobes, girded
girlishly with a long sash—black or pale-blue.
Among a travelled friend’s fresh-from-Paris mil-
linery, I note a pancake of burnt straw, support-
ing a cloud of white embroidered chiffon and
forget-me-nots, but all her hats are not flat. A
white tulle Gainsbro’ shows under its turned-up
leaf four rich black plumes and an enamel buckle,
while the whiteness of its under-brim is further
relieved by narrow black-velvet cords and band of
bhtck chiffon-applique. This hat is the ward-
robe companion of a gown of finest white
cloth, with sailor-collar, turn-over cuffs,
and chemisette of ivory-silk point-lace, and
nautical breast-knot and waist-scarf of black
silk-muslin. Black lily-of-the-valley predomi-
nates a toque of paint-green chiffon and
applique, and the usual sailor hat of yore is
superseded by a rustic burnt-straw, which travels
with a number of big, smart, detachable bows of
black chiffon, white tulle, yellow muslin, and
cherry silk respectively, to be pinned on by the
owner to suit the rest of her toilette. At present,
Parisian modistes send home with cotton frocks
soft turn-over collars and cuffs of tucked white
lawn and embroidery, while more ambitious robes
demand darned-net tuckers, Honiton lace, &c.
“If Premier Peacock doesn’t mind, the credit
he gained when Minister of Education as the
founder of cookery centres in Vic., is likely to be
obscured by their gradual decay through inanition
during his Premiership. Three centres have now
abruptly closed, because there are no teachers.
Of the first and only batch trained, one has died,
two others are married or are going to be, and
there never was an emergency man provided for
relieving work. At this moment, not a teacher is
in training, although the Dept, must be aware
that many more will be wanted. If the work
is to succeed at all it must grow and extend to
many country centres, instead of always being
confined to Melbourne and her suburban horse-
leech daughters.
“ A well-known Collins-street photographer
relates that only once did he ever have to re-
monstrate severely with a sitter—and this one a
honeymooner. The ecstatic bridegroom wished
to be taken in the arms of his wedded, love. But
the artist, who has nothing to do with the
emotional side of life, his business being with the
purely mechanical inanimate, demurred, and re-
fused such a ‘ sitting.’ The amorous groom, how-
ever, insisted. Ashe was a professional maninMel-
bourne, and a moneyed one, the camera angel
gave in and ‘ took ’ the pair in their chosen
position. The poor man didn’t live long to enjoy
the fame of this love-lock, but dozens of the
pictures are now extant—proofs of his sweet brief
bliss.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.


There is one place in Sydney where the
business of money-lending is carried on in an
equitable manner and where those in need of
financial accommodation can rely on being
decently treated. The sensible man who wishes
to get rid of his monetary worries at a minimum
of cost and trouble to himself goes to the offices of
the Australian Equitable Investment Co., Ltd.
The co. has a large capital for investment at low
interest, and is always open to advance money on
risks which the banks would decline; -and acts
generally as a medium between the banks and
ordinary money-lenders. Men in business can
obtain assistance for their venture at a figure
which will leave them a fair profit for themselves.
The eo. advances money to any extent on deeds of
land, bond warrants, merchandise, stock in trade,
scrip, interests under wills, furniture and all classes
of security. Press notices which have been pub-
lished from time to time express the favorable
opinion which generally exists regarding the co.
Mr. J. E. O’Brien is the Managing Director, and
the co.’s offices are at 45 Elizabeth-street, between
King and Hunter-streets.*

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Mr. Jos. Symes, of Melbourne, who knows
no God, is a strong Jingo and black-labor advo-
oate. A man must have some superstitions.
Probably Premier Jenkins of S.A. is the
only native of the U.S.A who ever controlled
the political destinies of a British possession.
Page, the Maranoa (Q.) representative,
who challenged Knox to hump bluey through
Leperland, is the top weight of the Federal Parlia-
ment. He tips the beam at about 18st. 71b.
Capetown University beat the Australian
declension shops in the matter of crawl. Whilst
we only gave the Duke of York bogus degrees, in
South Africa they made him Chancellor of the
University !
A member of N.S.W. Army Medical Corps
named Wooten returns from S’Africa vigorous
and happy after having been shot right through
brain, lung, and arm, and wounded in the back !
Probably the world’s prize surgical curio.
Artist Souter isthenew N.S. W. Art Gallery
trustee. His appointment will give the rival
Sydney art-cliques each two reps, on the trust,
and no doubt the Daubers versus Potboilers row
will be carried into the Gallery board-room.
“P.X.”: I come to the rescue of the
reputation of the “ fiery Australian ” who —four
of him —propped up the fence while the White
Cliffs mail-robber went through the bags. The
four “Australians” were—Rosanove, Kanter,
Pedrana, Ortloff.
Jonathan (good name) Roberts, who sen-
sationally escaped from the prison at Ripa Island
in Lyttelton Harbour (M.L.) 13 years ago, by
methods yet undiscovered, has written a diary of
his adventures, and the Lyttelton Times editor
says he’s going to publish it. Roberts is supposed
to be in S. America.

from the British Government regarding the Aliens Bill aud its treatment of
Japanese, laying particular stress on the aid expected from Japan in the
settlement of Chinese and other Eastern questions.”— NEWS ITEM.
NURSE BRITANNIA (to Australia): “You have poured out your
blood in South Africa for us ; let me suggest strengthening
your Constitution by a little transfusion.”
The career of the late John McKenzie,
leading Maoriland politician, was curiously por-
tioned into decades. He arrived in M. L. in 1861,
was elected to Provincial Council in 71, entered
House of Reps. ’Bl, became Minister of the Crown
’9l, and died in 1901.
N.S.W. Crown-Solicitorship, now vacant,
is the highest-paid post in the Public Service —
£1650 a year. The acting-Crown-Solicitor is an
official at a few hundreds a year. There will be
some skilful wire-pulling to guide the bestowal of
the permanent billet.
It isn’t likely that Mr. H. M. Chomley
(who retires on the verge of 70 years) will be
succeeded, as Vic. Police-Commissioner, by Supt.
O’Callaghan. The latter is now 57 years old, but
that is not the only fact likely to debar him from
the promotion he seeks.
When Inspector-General Fosbery gives up
charge of the N.S.W. police, De Wet, when
caught, should be appointed in his place. De Wet
is the only man The Bulletin can think of as
capable of coping with the approaching plague of
returned-cohentingenter.
According to the cable-man “ Kruger’s
funds are almost exhausted, and the Boer refugees
in Europe are on the point of destitution.” Why
can’t these folk maintain a fair consistency in
their romances ? A few months ago Kruger was
wallowing in tons of ill-gotten gold.
The King, it is reported in London, has a
severe throat affection, and there is a ghastly
rumor as to cancer, from which two of his blood
relatives—Duke Alfred and the German Dowager-
Empress—have lately died. The King’s pathetic
reference to cancer at the Tuberculosis Congress
was marked by many as showing the shadow of a
personal fear.
Melb. Australasian Editor Watterston
goes for a six months’ trip to Europe. Like most
of the chiefs employed by the Argus proprietary,
over-work has broken him down. The decision to
go. was arrived at rather suddenly, and horses
were swopped while the paper was going to press.
Editor Bulletin. —The expression, “ Six
Families,” so frequently used in connection with
W.A. is a mistake. It should be “ Fixed Fami -
lies,” from the fact of the families in question
having been so placed to administer affairs before
the advent of Responsible Government in 1890.
J.M.
Colonel Finn, now commanding Q. forces,
is a strong candidate for the N.S.W. commandant-
ship. Others mentioned for the post were Col.
Waddell (brother of the N.S.W. Treasurer) and
the fierce-looking Senator Neild. The latter,
indeed, is said to cherish a wild hope that “ with
a change of Ministry” he may get the Federal
command.
Before Salvarmy “ Commandant ” Booth
sailed for the land of his hook-nosed progenitor,
he shed more light on the Jonah fish-story. The
“ Com.” evoked fresh sympathy for Jonah by tell-
ing an audience that “he had a very hard time
in that whale’s belly with nothing to eat but
castor oil for three days.” The inspired teacher
didn’t explain why Jonah’s whale was full of
castor oil at the time. What had the whale done
to deserve such a purgative ?
As the English Judge Emdeu, in his plea
for the reform of law-practice, lately pointed out,
one of the wholly-unnecessary burdens put upon
those who go to the courts is the rule of Bar-
etiquette that a K.C. must have a junior with him.
In Australia the rule is being steadily got under
foot, but Westralian Judge Stone, on his appoint-
ment as C. J., revived it the other day, expressing
the hope that it would be adhered to. But, then,
as Judge Emden also said, it is perhaps stupid to
expect the ordinary judge to be more anxious for
the welfare of the public than of the Bar from
which he came.

When N.S.W. Upper House rudely
downed the Womanhood Suffrage Bill on the
third reading, a charming woman, to whose advo-
cacy the measure owes much, scared the souls of
its opponents by weeping in her seat behind the
President’s chair. If the vote could only have
been recalled then, the Bill would have gone
through without a division.
There is not a man in the Victorian railway
service who worked under Richard Speight who
will not heartily regret his demise. From the
men’s point of view, he was the best “boss” they
have had. He had been through the mill himself,
and his administration has never been proved
seriously at fault as regards existing lines, though
with the construction of new lines perhaps it was
different.
Writes a Bulletin correspondent from
Munich:—
The death of Empress Frederick mu9t have come as a
relief to her. She died of cancer, and for the last few
months was in an unspeakably dreadful condition. Also,
notwithstanding continuous injections of morphia, she
suffered terribly. She was, on the whole, cordially hated
in Germany, and she has done little to earn that country’s
gratitude. As to her love for the late Emperor—soon
after her husband’s death she married secretly her M.C.,
or whatever else you might call that imposing dignitary,
a Count Veckendorff. By-the-way, William 11. is an actor
born. When called to his mother’s deathbed he appeared
at the very last moment, clad in the uniform of the 2nd
Black Hussars, of which regiment the poor dying woman
happened to be honorary colonel. A man who, at such
a moment, can think of such a trifle, has missed his
vocation.
WILL IT COME TO THIS?
THE KANAKA: “Driven out of my own home,
I can see more than ever now the need for a
Black Australia .”
Apropos the fact that at Cairns a drunken
ruffian threw a rotten egg at a carriage containing
Gov.-General Lord Hopetoun. At a long ago
Sydney election-meeting a rotten egg bit John
M'Elhone in the eye. “Aha!” triumphantly
exclaimed a voice from out the crowd, “it
stinks!” “Yes,” replied the ever ready and
always-elegant John, “ but not like the breath of
the bastard chat threw it.”
Mr. A. B. Worthington has been appointed
minister of the Sydney Unitarian Church. A
handsome man, of great personal magnetism, lie
easily convinced the trustees that there was
nothing in an interesting past to prevent him
doing good service to their tabernacle. Details of
some very fine gimlet work on the part of Mr.
Worthington’s Maoriland disciples some years
ago kept the good in a state of salacious piety for
months.
More irony of fate ! A many-stationed
Victorian squatteress was once driving over one
of her estates with her lawyer, who chanced to
remark, “How much better would this land look
if it were dotted all over with farmers’ home-
steads?” “I would rather see it peopled with
pigs,” replied the rich woman. Well, she died
lately, and that very estate was, but a few months
ago, bought by Government for “closer settle-
ment,” and is now cut up into about 100 small
farms, on which reside as many Crown lessees.
Church organist J. R. M‘Cann, who has*
finally taken his feet off the pedals of the R.C.
Cathedral organ, Hobart, tells of the
fate of a choir which he trained for a
Collingwood (Vic.) church. It was in-
tended that the charm of Mozart and
Haydn should burst as a surprise on the
congregation, so one Sunday, in the
middle of the service, organ and choir
plunged into a highly ornate “Gloria.”
The priest (Dr. Bleasdale, familiarly
and tenderly known as “Father
Blazes ”) was more than surprised, and,
turning round, called out: “Will you
stop that noise ! I can’t go on with such
a distraction.” The choir was effectu-
ally scattered, and “Father Blazes”
was no more distracted.
Attorney-General Deakin thinks
of presenting to Melb. Museum an old
letter, written by grandfather Deakin in
London to his mother in Nottingham
(granfer being a boy at the time). The
letter tells of a strange-looking animal
which had lately arrived at the Zoo-
logical Gardens in Regent’s Park, an
animal with a long tail and short fore-
legs. Captain Cook and Dr. Banks had
brought it from a country called New Holland
and its name was “kangaroo.” To think of the
English natural history of Australia being no
older than Alfred Deakin’s father’s father !
Donald Macdonald is back again on the
Argus staff, taking up his work just at the spot
at which he left it off two years ago. Already
Mac. is doing the meetings of the Harbor Trust,
the Chamber of Manufactures, the Women’s
Hospital committee, and other such warlike
engagements. Three Sydney war-correspondents
are back again to the Equity Court, “the
suburbs,” “ Parliament first turn, ” and seem to
agree with their peaceful duties. Alas ! that the
cohentingenters cannot drop back as calmly to the
ways of work.
Jimmy Mirams, when he sued Melb. Age,
the other day, for £lOOO libel damages, was so
favorably impressed by his counsel’s statement of
the case that he wouldn’t have sold his chance for
£lOO had a speculator made the offer. The little
man has a truly bantam eocksureness. He didn’t
even go into the witness-box to stand a cross-
examination, yet after hearing himself sweetly
spoken of by his hired advocate Jimmy felt sure
of a verdict. The Judge’s summing-up never
gave him a “possible.” As a matter of fact, the
Age remarks upon the vanity of J.M.’s hopeless
candidature for the Federal Parliament were fair
and mild comment, and the jury had gumption
enough to tell him so.
Cardinal Moran avers that he will return
to Australia from Rome if spared to do so. The
Bulletin sincerely hopes he will change his mind.
The Cardinal may be, as his Consecrated Press
affirms, a polished diplomat and a politician of
extraordinary power 5 but his .Australian record
(read his Newtown speech!) is one of clumsy
bungling. His proper weapon is a shillelagh.
When he takes up a rapier he always pokes
himself in the eye. The Bulletin hopes to see
the Cardinal stop away as a resalt of his election
as Pope.
To hear that our dear old friend, John A.
Dowie, now of Chicago, lately fitted on to him-
self purple wings and a golden robe, and flopped
about on a public stage like a wood-grub m
butterfly garb, as Elijah 11, evidently gave the
idea to someone else that two could play at a
game which was as simple as all that. For we
hear now, with joy, that John Hoop, of Minnesota
(U.S.A.), has set up in business as John the
Baptist 11, and wants to argue things with Elijah
11. “After the argument,” says John the
Baptist, “there won’t be any more Elijah.” So
far, the Baptist only runs to a tin star as an
announcement of his mission.
“H.S.R.”: Amusing to read of Lord
Milner, of Golgotha, spouting about “the Im-
perial policy of England.” This Lager Peer is a
German by blood, his father having been Carl
Milner, of Weiss, in Germany, and It is mother a
Miss Von Rappard. Alfred Milner was even
born in Germany, so that there is nothing English
about him unless it is his pharisaism. And he is
thewnan who, only two short years ago, told, the
S’Africans " that other parts of the empire might
possibly be as loyal, but none more so ’’—having
at the same time the dagger up his sleeve. It is
truly a case of dog eat dog.
Prondergast, Victorian labor member and
hurricane talker, was “father of the chapel ” on
Sydney D.T in the early days when that paper
was known from the habits of the staff as The
Morning Rum. The methodical old advertisement-
clerk had a fixed time for going upstairs with his
few columns of “fat,” anxiously awaited each
night by the comps. As his head appeared at
the head of the stairs it was saluted with loud
applause and sometimes with strange missiles.
One night, somebody let fly a huge wet sponge,
but the head that came spluttering up was that
of Manager Wynne. He sacked the comps, in a
body, but afterwards relented, because he couldn’t
get the paper out.
N.S.W. Senator O’Connor, as pictured by
Adelaide Critic :
However, here is Senator O’Connor standing by his Bill.
He is tall and robust-looking, with powerful shoulders,
carrying a well-poised head. His heard is close-cut, and
just tinged with grey. His hair, fine as silk, is much
ruffled just over the forehead. His body is rigid as he
stands ; and his earnest eyes, instinct with deep sin-
cerity and thought, let their gaze fall at the feet of the
front Freetrade bench. His right hand is pushed deeply
into his trousers-pocket ; his left hand holds a copy of
the Bill under discussion. He emphasises his points with
this, and when he is very emphatic, his shoulders stiffen
visibly. He is a plain' orator, with neither quips nor
cranks nor fine phrases, but his clear wisdom goes
through sophistry like a fire through the dry under-
growth. Senator O’Connor standing to his opinion is a
picture of mental strength and power worth going far to-
see.

The newly-elected teetotal Mayor of a
Melb. suburb, in giving his reasons for declining
to supply councillors with grog, solemnly declared
that it was the duty of “ those in high positions ”
to set a good example. His brother councillors—>
the usual mixture of small drapers, grocers and
publicans—naturally applauded this tribute to
their collective exaltedness.

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors. —In the ease of ethnological and
natural-history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
The new lady-teacher had just taken
school in, when there was a knock at the door.
Opened it, and was asked, “Moight Oi addhress
the childer for a moment, mum ? ” The teacher,
thinking it was possibly a candidate for Parlia-
ment—or some other public character —said :
“Certainly, sir! Come inside?” The gent,
stepped in, cleared his throat a few times, and
said: “Now, bhoys and gur-rls, lisliten to me !
Did any of yis see a red poley cow on your way to
school this marnin’?”
* * * •
Dr. MacGillicuddy (begorra !) lecturing at
Collingwood (Melbourne) on medicine-mania and
the capacity of cranks to swallow rivers of mixture
and pyramids of pills, went to America for his
illustrations. He should study the Australian
bushman, but more particularly the “hatter,” in
his lair, if he wishes to discover the possibilities of
the human passion for pills and potions. Writer
once had a mate who took daily doses of a popular
patent-medicine just because he had “ got into the
way of taking it ” years before. He has met a
household in which the wife was often beaten by
the husband for taking his medicine, and where
the whole family had a craving for that particular
brew that suggested the opium-habit. Ask Imy
country storekeeper, and this is very like the
testimony you will receive: “Lots of my cus-
tomers go for new patent medicines as soon as they
come out, others stick to the old ones. So far as
my experience goes, if the average bushman has
to choose between his favorite medicine and his
favorite booze, he takes the medicine.” This is
the word of a tradesman in a Gippsland mining
and agricultural centre. The Australian bush
NOT THE HORSE’S FAULT.
TIM : “ It’s divilish poor your horse is lookin’.”
MIKE : “ God knows it’s not his fault—he ates all he gets.”
could do with a strong anti-medicine revival and
an active crusade against pills.

“Barwon”: Re* carriers’ method of ex-
tracting liquor from casks (“ A. A. A.*” B. 22/6/’01).
When writer was in Central Queensland in the
70’s he frequently heard old carriers speak of
“bulling” casks of spirits, especially of rum.
Method was to pour boiling water on the top of
the cask overnight, cover it over, and (so they
said) the water would be converted into spirits
before morning. The heat possibly might draw
the spirits through the wood. Another way was
to “spile” under hoops ; and make good the
quantity extracted with water.
• • * •
“ A.E.S.” : Re carrier’s extraction of grog
from casks by means of heated iron (B. 22/6/’01).
Have seen the gimlet trick frustrated by a cask
inside a cask. The carrier bored the outside cask
to no purpose. Continued operation, penetrating
inside cask (rum). Inside cask shifted and pre-
vented plugging. Result —cost to carrier about
£l5. Consignor and consignee had “ been there ”
before.

“ Walgo ”: Re “ Pel’s ” query (B. 15/6/’01)
as to why birds “tower” when struck in the eye
by gunshot. I fancy that the shot causes the bird
to hold its head up, thereby directing its flight
upwards. Have often seen ducks hit in the eye
by shot, but it has always caused instant death.
Have also seen ducks tower when surprised by the
appearance of a man—without being fired at.

' supposed poison-weed (8.,
22/6/ 01) Have heard old practical hands assert
cattle in these cases are not poisoned at all,
but tbat weak and starving stock, allowed to
depasture suddenly on this weed, gorge themselves
with fatal results ; whereas stock depasturing
habitually m the same country get fat, simply
because, to begin with, after ram they cannot
gorge themselves till the weed grows long enough,
Such disasters mostly if not always, occur
amongst travelling or shifted stock,
* * * *
Jaynol : I know a man who kills snakes
by catching them by the end of the tail, and
“cracking” them as one would a whip. The
head comes off at the first joint, just as a piece of
rotten whipcord will break off with every crack of
a whip. I also know a local resident who recently
had a live tiger-snake sent through the post to
him, done up in a box like wedding-cake.
* * * *
“ Pelican ” : Re “ superstition ” of horses
(“ Wat Tell, ”B. 15/6/’01). Remember once camp-
ing close to the ruins of a “ haunted ” pub. on
Gayndah-road, Q. An employee of mine (native
of the locality) protested, assuring me that no
horse would stop there. We belled and hobbled
the mokes, and as, up to turning-in time, they
seemed contented enough, the prophet was duly
“guyed.” About midnight, however, there was a
regular stampede, and next morning we had a
lively hunt to find the brutes. Condensation of
the legend : Woman. Man rides up, night-time.
Squabble. Bottles fly. Publican runs for gun.
Horseman runs for horse, mounts, clears, full up.
Bang, bang—man and horse dead on road.
Story may be a myth, but the horse stampede was
painfully true.
• * * *
“D.H.R.”: Re the frequently-made as-
sertion that black ducks will not lay in a hollow
tree. I once saw a duck of this kind fly out of the
“spout ” of a gum-tree on the Bar won. Took no
notice at the time, but passed there two days
afterwards, and witnessed the same thing ; where-
upon I borrowed a tomahawk and climbed the
tree. Found a nest with five fresh eggs.

of the lower Bogan. Can any Bulletin reader
corroborate ?
“E.W.8.”: Re prospectors’
luck. Swincers were out nine
months last year near Laverton
(W. A.) for nothing. This year they
had been out five weeks, when-
searching for their camels —they
struck a likely - looking gully.
Brought shaker up and put a run
through—Gold ! Sinking, 2ft. to
4ft. Rush set in; 600 men there
in a week. Nobody outside the
Reward getting wages. Swincers
getting 20oz. to 40oz. daily.

Seen through a window in
Little Latrobe-street, Melbourne —
24 newly-arrived Hindoos squatting
in a room 12 x 12 : in the centre a
white man reading from a book.
Probably the niggers were being
coached in the elements of hawking-
conversation.
A remarkable mirage was seen from the
P. and O. s.s. Australia last Nov. while crossing
the Australian Bight. At the time of the appari-
tion land was invisible, but the mirage—appearing
to seaward —was apparently a complete represen-
tation of the coast on the opposite side of the ship.
The mirage lasted for some hours, and was very
distinct; the scenery altering• just as the real
coastal scenery does when a vessel travels at speed.
The oldest hands on board had never before seen
anything like it.
• * * *
“Eller”: A few years must see our
marsupials extinct—at the present rate of exter-
mination. I know a storekeeper in Stanthorpe
district (Q.) who, during 1898-9, bought 54,326
skins of all kinds, whereas during the ’99-00
season he could only purchase 14,586.

Dear B.— that cruel Victorian boy.
I knew a school lad who, one day, was found in
front of three small crosses, upon which were
nailed, with forelegs outstretched, three living
frogs, evidently a representation of Calvary.
Predicted a bad future for that kid, but-was
possibly wrong, as he is now something in the
parson business. —SPRING.
• » • •
The deplorably dirty Castlemaine (Vic.)
vagrant who had half-crowns hidden in his matted
beard, and 3d. pieces encrusted in the loam on his
skin, and who had to be hydraulically sluiced from
afar off by the gaol people, had (says The Argus),
“tufts of grass growing between his toes.” A
truly effective climax, this. The memory of the
poor beggar (who died as the result of the douche)
may be handed down to posterity with that of the
way-back man wlio ventured on a Turkish bath.
After shampooing him for an hour, the attendants
came upon a very old-fashioned waistcoat.

“ Scotty W.” to “ J.W.C.” : I accept your
challenge, and will find a backer to plank £lO
that you do not clear a holding of less than 640
acres of live rabbits in one week. At the com-
mencement of the 168 hours, there will be de-
livered to you or your man one bag pollard, one
shilling’s worth of poison named by you, sugar
ad lib., and rations and camp for the manipulator.
I shall be present during the whole time, not
necessarily for publication but as a guarantee that
there is no extraneous barrowing, or burrowing, or
borrowing. You shall choose an umpire, I an-
other ; and, if necessary, they will call a referee.
My tenner and yours to be lodged with the
manager of any bank in Narandera. The holding
is practically rabbit-proof, or will be made so.
Water nearly all round; plenty of timber for
firewood and rabbits.

“8.R.”: Why not extend the Bill for
shearers’ accommodation to harvesters and plough-
men ? The big “ cocky ” employs as many men as
the average squatter, for a longer period, and
generally provides no accommodation, unless the
privilege of sleeping in a stripper, or the hay-
stack, or under a few chaff-bags thrown over a
wire-fence is “accommodation.” Surely these
bush-workers are as worthy of consideration as
the “swaggers,” “tomahawkers,” and “loppies”
of N.S.W.
• * * *
“ Prospector ” : When camped on the tops
of the highest mountains in Victoria, I have seen,
at sundown, countless numbers of the huge
“ bogong ” moths arise from the rocks. They fly
away to feed at night, and return at daylight.
The blacks used to make annual summer trips to
these mountains to feast on the moths, and I
have found stone tomahawks of a hard, blue or
green diorite on the tops of Kosciusko, Buffalo,
and Bogong (latter named from the moth).
THE LATE MR. GEORGE COLQUHOUN, N.S.W.
CROWN SOLICITOR.

The Bulletin has received from Gunda-
booka, near Bourke, N.S.W., a carpet-snake skin
9ft. 4in. long, which “ Abdul ” thinks is a record.
But in North Queensland writer has seen one
nearly 22ft. long, and 16-footers are not uncom-
mon. Mr. L. F. Sachs, lately of Sydney, and
now of London, has in his possession the skin of a
carpet-snake (killed by himself) measuring, if we
remember rightly, almost 23ft.
y * * *
Not long ago two youthful lawyers success-
fully appeared for a defendant, who gave, as
security for their costs, a lien on a horse in his
possession, described as a “ thoroughbred.” When
the specified time for payment had arrived, they
decided upon “collecting” the security, and,
armed with the writ, a halter and sundry nobblers
they proceeded to their client’s residence. The
owner pointed out the “thoroughbred” in, t'.e
middle of a 200-acre paddock, and told them to go
and catch their “costs.” Over the attempt they
spent four fruitless hours, and are now certain
that if they could have secured the noble animal
a Melbourne Cup would have been within their
grasp—such “foot” and “bottom” did the
“costs” display in evading capture.

Reported that three black gins of Coraki
(N.S.W.) drew first prize in Tatt.’s special on
Metrop. Stakes. What a corroboree on Bunga-
walbyn Creek !
A curious animal was caught last month at
Thuddungra (N.S.W.). It was about the size of
a rabbit, but had a snout Gin. long, tapering to a
point; ears 4in. long, and almost hairless; and a
12in. tail brushed at tip. A Young paper says
“ it was of the graminivorous order, very active,
and could jump to a considerable height.” De-
scription savors of a cross between a rabbit and
kangaroo-rat.

question: “We’re sendin’ all our best men to

“ Nabo ” : In the construction of a Govt,
tank (started some months back), to furnish a
water-supply for a small N.S.W. township, some
hundreds of sq. yds. were excavated before it was
discovered that the operations were upon private
property, the owner of which had preserved a dead
silence —being naturally a silent man. To avoid
litigation, the partly-made tank was filled in
again. Needless to say the official responsible for
the blunder pursues the even tenor of his way in
the Govt, service.

Three stud merinoes from Cullenbone
(N.S.W.) yielded 12-months’ fleeces of 341 b., 331 b.
and 281 b. A 5-year-old ram at Pine Park,
Burrendong, yielded 391 b. of wool.

“C.D.J.”: We’ve heard a lot about the
goanna’s bone-swallowing capabilities, but I think
the following is new: —Nest-eggs having persis-
tently disappeared, I got half-a-dozen porcelain
“ fakes,” thinking thus to “ euchre ’’ the suspected
“ ’anna’s,” but the china all went within a couple
of days. Soon afterwards I shot one of the
marauders, and found inside him two of the china
eggs, as well as several of the real article.

“C.J.C.”: At pub. in the drought-
stricken west. Two swaggies and “ swell ” at
table. Landlady: “What will you try, sor—
Galah, goat, or porcupine ? ” Swell: “ A little of
each, please ma’m.” Miss Public-house : “ Try a
little lignum sprouts wid it ? ” Galah, by the
way, takes much stewing, but porcupine is_ real
good and fat. Too fat to roast; must be boiled ;
and then tastes like ox-tongue.

Near Blackall Range, S.Q., a timber-
getter got his leg badly smashed at the foot of a
fairly steep range. His one chance of being
picked up was to get on top of the hill along
which ran a timber-track, and although the dis-
tance was almost half-a-mile, rising as much as
25ft. in a chain, he managed it. Most of the
journey was done backwards, the poor devil
prizing himself with his hands, from a. sitting
posture, then leaning forward and lifting the
injured member as close to the body as possible.

Re queer geological formations
(“Pelican” B. 22/6/’01). Near Byalong (Vie.) a
railway-cut exposes, amidst reddish granite, what
looks like the filled-in course of an ancient river.
On the bottom and partly up the sides—which
have a uniform slope—is a layer of large, water-
worn quartz gravel. The rest of the filling is
black soil. In the same district, on low flats, are
similar formations, mostly running in a ciroular
direction. The grass in summer dies first on them,
and thus makes them easily discernible.
* * * •
“ Prospector ” : Not generally known
that the “ bobuck ” mountain-’possum is car-
nivorous. I recently snared a wallaby, and, after
skinning it, threw the carcase into the limbs of a
sassafras tree. Next day I was surprised to find
the greater part missing. Set four whip-snares,
and on following morning had four bobucks
caught. Being still sceptical, I watched that
night and saw quite a mob of the ’possums feast-
ing on the remains. After three nights only the
bones were left.
* * * *
“Prospector”: Re the black-backed
Monaro magpies (B. 22/6/’01). Has “ Gumleaf ”
or any other observer ever seen two ‘ 1 maggies ”
marked alike ? I have seen thousands, but never
two quite the same.
» • * *
“ Belah ” : “ H.M.T.” (B. 8/6/’01) is in
error as to how the kylie is thrown by the W.A.
.blacks. The woolana, as it is termed by the gold-
field natives, can be thrown from either hand, and
it does hit the ground when thrown with hostile
intent. Have seen many a native fight, both
tribal and otherwise, and whenever the. woolana
was used it hit the ground a few yards from the
person aimed at, and then rebounded with a
twang on its upward flight of destruction. If it
misses its mark it describes the usual circles. The
high circle-throwing is mere gallery-play. You
must see the native throwing for his daily food to
understand what woolana-throwing is. Unlike
the spear, the woolana cannot be dodged, and
escape depends on the manipulation of the shield
or woondah.
* * * »
“ G.P.B ” strikes a rich patch :
After footing-it some hundred miles, I struck a job (at
Commonwealth time) a few miles outside Temora. I was
expected to sling the axe at huge trees from daybreak
till an hour before sundown, then get the cows in and
milk them, look after horses, and do a little farming, etc.,
for fun. Wages, 3s. a cord of wood cut and stacked. I
was expected to cut down the tree, saw it into 14in.
lengths and split up fine for the stove. The other work
was exacted from me in return for permission to sling
the axe all day.

Springvale, near Win-
ton, W.Q., lias a spring of
good water, somewhat miner-
alised, in which moderately
dense objects won't sink. A
human plunging in, for instance,
is immediately jerked to the sur-
face again. A strong up-rush is supposed to be
the cause of this.

At a station in Cloncurry (Q.) district
there is a gin 6ft. high and weighing 13st.
N.S.W. Lands Minister Crick declined to
answer a question on the eternal Mercadool case,
“as the case is to go back again to the Courts.”
Seeing the amount of fog in which the lawyers
have involved it, how would it do for Par-
liament to appoint a court of independent business
men, and make their decision final ? The first
step to that is to order a return as to the costs to
date. That would be a many-thousand-pounds-
shock to the people.
The reductions, varying from 20 to 75 per
cent., made by local Land Boards in N.S.W.
Central Division on the capital value of c. p’s and
h.s’s, give a nasty set-back to those officials who,
to curry favor with the head office, imposed
ridiculously high prices on land thrown open for
settlement. Writer knows a case where one of
these officials, now out of the service, put, in his
capacity as district surveyor, a price of £3 per
acre on a block of land unimproved in any way,
and adjoining a highly-improved freehold block,
on which, a few weeks previously, he had refused,
as private money-lender, to advance more than
16s. Bd. per acre.
The accompanying likeness of Miss Stella
Miles Franklin, of Goulburn, N.S.W., gives a
good idea of the author of “My Brilliant (?)
” as she appeared at 18, when the story
was written. Her book has been described as the
first really Australian novel, —Australian through
and through ; but the credit for its lively charm,
given to Australian sunlight, should possibly have
been divided with Irish heredity. Yet Miss
Franklin is “totally, absolutely, completely
Australian entirely.” Her father, John Maurice
Franklin, is the youngest of three Franklin
brothers, formerly of Brindabella station, N.S.W.,
well-known as intrepid riders and expert bushmen.
Miss Franklin was born at Talbingo station, near
Tumut; and has been a bush-girl all her life, and
a scribbler since 13.

King Edward is said to be in poor health.
He is nearly 60, and has lived well up to his years.
Lloyd’s insurance people are charging 10 per cent,
to insure his life for tradesmen until after the
coronation.
A farmer has been arrested, the cable
states, for giving false information to the British,
thus enabling the Boers under Theron to cross
some river or other. But the same Theron, by
the cables of a month back, had been captured, or
killed. Boer commandants are fabled, i.e. cabled,
to have at least nine lives.

A WESTERN PATENT FOR A BAD
LEADER.
When the pack-horse jibs the rope is pulled
tight, and suddenly released. The sapling, re-
bounding, strikes the horse sharply on the rump ,
and he gees up.

No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.

handed in ; and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any State) accompany.

Platt-Betts’ Pacer Interviewed.
Mr. Arthur L. Ryan, who resides at Church-
street, Parramatta, close to the Railway Bridge,
is well known in this State as an athlete, and in
conversation with a reporter he recently gave
some interesting particulars of his life. Mr.
Ryan is twenty-eignt years of age, was born in
Parramatta and educated at the local Grammar
School. He is prominently identified with foot-
ball, cricket and cycling.
“You accompanied Platt-Betts when that
eminent cyclist toured Australia, did you not ? ”
said the reporter.
“Yes,” said Mr. Ryan, “I travelled with
Platt-Betts all over the colonies and was chief
steersman for his pacing machines.”
“Did you have a smash-up ? ” was asked.
“ No,” said Mr. Ryan, “ all went well, although
I must admit that I owed my steadiness of hand
to Dr. Williams’ pink pills for pale people, for
before that I had suffered greatly from indiges-
tion for two or three years, and tried many
patent medicines without avail. I had no
appetite, did not relish my food, and after meals
I suffered from wind, flatulence, heart-burn, and
a heavy, drowsy feeling ; there was often a nasty
taste in my mouth, which was so dry that my
tongue seemed to stick to the roof of it. Pains
in. my chest and stomach afflicted me, and I
could not sleep properly. I used to turn from
side to side for nours before falling into an unre-
freshing sleep, and when I rose in the morning
I still felt tired and weak. I was always des-
pondent, and could take no active interest in
anything. I lost weight and came down to nine
stone. Mr. L. W. Pye, the well-known chemist
of Church-street, Parramatta, recommended Dr.
Williams’ pink pills, and I tried them. After
taking two boxes I felt greatly benefited, and
before long my appetite returned. Now I eat
heartily, relish my food, and am stronger than
ever. I sleep well at night and am com-
pletely free from every symptom of indigestion,
debility or unstrung nerves. Since taking Dr.
Williams’ pink pills I not only went through
the colonies successfully with Platt-Betts, but
have won five handicaps in cycle races, and have
paced for the Dunlop Tyre Co., on the Sydney
Cricket Ground. I can conscientiously re-
commend Dr. Williams’ pink pills as a cure for
indigestion and debility.”
Athletes and others who suffer from unstrung
nerves and debility will fiud that Dr. Williams’
pink pills tone up the nerves, enrich their blood,
and strengthen their constitution in a manner
hitherto unknown to medical science. Owing to
their blood-building and nerve-toning properties
these pills cure rheumatism, neuralgia, sciatica,
bronchitis, consumption, skin diseases, hysteria,
St. Vitus’ dance, heavy colds, effects of influenza
and pneumonia, etc. Sold by chemists and store-
keepers and by the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co.,
Queen’s Place, Sydney two and nine per box,
six boxes fifteen and three, post free.

SYDNEY.
K3T Send to Headquarters for a Genuine Bottle
Otago (M.L.), gold-dredges are now producing
upwards of 2000 oz. per week, and yet stocks won’t
rise. One co., the Monte Ohristo, in three weeks returned
3660 z., and prospects were good as ever. Yet shares
were at latest quoted at 355.; whereas IS months ago a
dredge returning 40oz. a week was considered a good
thing, and shares read .ly found buyers at £3 or £4, A
good yield now produces not holders, but unloaders.
Expenses and liquidations have destroyed confidence ;
the average investor feels that dredging investments are
about as risky as trotting bets.
* * * *
After long years of alternate hope and disap-
pointment, the syndicate which has been boring for
pet oleum at New Plymouth (M. L.), has decided to throw
up the sponge. The oil is there in good quality ; but the
(juantity is small, and the bores seem liable to constant
inrushes of salt water. Another syndicate has secured
boring rights over large inland areas in the same district,
but doesn’t seem in a hurry to start operations. The loss
to the chief New Plymouth syndicator, a prominent M.L.
lawyer, is said to run close on five figures.
* * * *
Mr. J. H. Bryant writes from Derby; Tas. :
“ The alleged fly in the Briseis ointment — i.e., the silting-
up of the main watercourse—is simply a bogey, and that
is all. The watercourse in question is the river Ringa-
rooma, which h is an uninterrupted and rapid course from
the Briseis outlet to the South Pacific ; when the ocean-
tides cease to operate, then, and only then, is it possible
for this watercourse to silt up. In support of my conten-
tion, let me state that recent expert examination of the
river below the Briseis outlet d.scloses that scouring to
the extent of almost 4ft. has taken place during the past
year and that between the Briseis outlet and Moorina
(seven miles down stream) there is 70ft. of fall; at the
latter place, the river is as bare of debris as it was before
mining began on the coast. A few years ago, 4000 miners
were engaged sluicing on the terraces and streams of six
fair-sized rivers and numerous small tributaries that
empty into the Ringarooma. Those workings consider-
ably choked the river; but now, less than 10) Chinese
are engaged fossicking, hence the tendency of the waters
of those tributaries is to slowly, but surely, scour the
Ringarooma. That some low-lying terraces along the
Ringarooma’s banks are silted up must be admitted, but
the grade from the Briseis to the ocean is the same now
as before mining commenced here. So long as the ocean
does not refuse to accept the debris , the Ringarooma
can’t silt.”
• * * »
Mining in the Northern Territory continues a
ghastly disappointment. The failure of the Northern
Territories Co., boomed ridiculously by Horatio Bottom-
ley, and then kicked bj' the same man for the awful ex-
travagance which naturally followed the booming, has
taken the heart out of most of the other English co’s.,
none of which have been making much more than office
exes. When co’s. throw up the sponge they nearly always
get tribute-offers from the local Chinese ; for the rest, the
only people who really look like making a fair thing out
of the mines are one or two Palmerston and Pt. Darwin
merchants, who are going into the business of mine-
running regularly. As for the one-time-threatened gold-
dredging boom, only two leases have been taken, and
neither of them has been worked.
* * * *
“ Sessil ”: Here and there on almost played-out
fields one sometimes stumbles on a good show. Thus on
the Mornish field, near Rockhampton (Q.) —otherwise
deserted, save for a few fossickers--William O’Donoghue
has for 11 years drawn a nice little income from the
Caernarvon Castle at Blackfellow’s Gully. Originally
owned by a co., much over-capitalized, money was spent
on expensive machinery and in uncovering fresh and low-
grade reefs instead of sinking. Finally the co. retired
with experience and a sickly smile, and O’Donoghue
stepped in. The reef is only 2in. to 12in., but the crushings
have varied from 2to 7oz. Owned by a practical miner,
it is worked at minimum cost, and is a good thing—with
the aforesaid low-grade stone as a reserve. At Rosewood,
near by, is the usual collection of old hands living on hope
and their savings. One claim was at work—the Golden
Bar ; but the manager being absent, no information was
procurable. A patchy field always.
* * * *
A measure before the Westralian Parliament
proposes that mines shall be open to inspection by share-
holders on one day in each fortnight—that once in 14
days the owner should be allowed to look at his own pro-
perty. It seems a reasonable idea—what are the rights
of property if an owner isn’t even allowed to look at what
he is supposed to own ? Almost all W. A. mines are bossed
in London. Local shareholders are shut out as rigidly as
possible. All reports are sent to London and carefully
suppressed until the management has bought in, if pros-
pects are good, or sold out if they are bad. And now the
local shareholder has grown weary of the arrangement,
and wants to see his own capital now and then, and find
out how it is getting on.
* • * »
Not often do Govt, officials write so strenuously
as the Govt. Geologist (N.S.W.) and Jaquet, F.G.S., do
in opposing the building by the State of copper and lead-
smelting furnaces at Broken Hill. “Just,” they say,
“ when experience has proved to business men that it is
not profitable to smelt locally, even with silver and lead
at much higher prices than they are now, this request is
made to the Govt. It amounts to an application for a
bonus to work unprofitable mines.” The great difficulty
would, however, be to keep the furnaces in blast for little
men’s shows. But, anyway, if the S.A. Govt., and
more especially the Silverton Tramway Co , will be
liberal in the nutter of railway freights, the startin'* of
the State smelter at I’t. Augusta (S.A.) will to a great
extent serve Barrier prospectors and holders of small
mines.
* * • *
The high price of coai last year may not prove
quite the good thing for N.S.W. and English producers
that it looked. For instance, it set India opening up
her mines, so that she raised 6,095,000 tons against only
2,530,000 tons in ’93. And she begins to open up an ex-
port trade, too, to Java, Sumatra, &c.
* * * *
“ Wad ly ” : The desulphurisation process for
B-'oken Hill ores announced as creating “ intense excite-
ment ” turns out after all to be a colorable imitation of
the Heberlein process (now used by the Sulphide Cor-
poration), which is in itself the application of a chemical
reaction of oxide of lime on sulphide of lead or its asso-
ciated sulphide minerals which was first announced in
1372 by Kivot in Traits de Mbtallurgie, Paris, vol. 11.,
page 42. The difference between the Heocrlein process
and the Carmiehael-Bradford is that in the former they
mix oxide of lime with the ore and then heat it in
furnaces so as to produce sulphate and sulphide of lime
wn,h sulphate of lead, etc., while the latter process
starts right off with sulphate of lime or sulphide of lime
instead of waiting for the furnace to produce it. Both
processes blow air through the hot ore at one
point in the roasti ig operation, to bring about the com-
pletion of desulphurisation, and also to agglomerate the
fine ore. Where sulphate of lime is found native as gyp-
sum, the Carmichael process would be commercially all
right, but as in most parts of Australia gypsum is not
plentiful, and ordinary limestone is both plentiful and
cueap, it looks as if its application is likely to be localised
to districts where gypsum is found, or where sulphate
of lime is a by-product from some other process. It
might be interesting to state that Storer and Marsh
patented, some years ago, the use of lime in roasting
Broken Hill ores. The only thing new about either pro-
cess seems to be the use of air to finish the desulphurisa-
tion.

Lucknow Mines, N.S.W. : Another application
for suspension of labor conditions, based on the ground
that amalgamation arrangements are not yet complete,
but are expected to be within the next two months, and
that for several years the mines employed an average of
60.) men and paid in wages over £5OOO a month. The
application was objected to on the ground that under the
Mining-on-Private-Lands Act regulating this pro-
perty, suspension could only be granted for six
months ; also, that these mines could be let on tribute,
and muscle would take the place of capital. One objector
said he was ready to take the property on tribute,
but was not prepared to say how much money would
be put up. The warden has given a limited exemption.
These mines were originally a freehold, for which £50,009
was paid. Several co’s were floated in England, two of
which —the Wentworth Proprietary and Aladdin’s Lamp—
were for a time fairly successful, each paying some
divs. The others, such as the D’Arcy Wentworth,
the Wentworth Extended, and the Amana, never
had a crushing, much less a div. Some tons of gold
have been taken out of the mines, and some tons
of gold have been sunk in getting it. At the
moment the writer can’t say where the most gold went;
but would like to ask what the effect of the forfeiture
of these mines would be. It seems almost impossible in
N.S.W. to do any big gold-mining without London
capital, and there would be very little hope of getting
any if such a group of mines as these were forfeited
because labour conditions were not complied with. But
there is no excuse for the co. not letting portion of the
mines on tribute if it can be shown that there are
tributors ready. The co. should not be allowed to follow
a dog-in-the-manger policy.

SOME OF THE
Many Notable Features.
AN UNPARALLELED COLLECTION-
The “Library of Famous Literature” is a collection,
unparalleled in extent, of the greater literature of the
world, from the dawn of letters, from Homer to Kipling.
ITS EDITORS—
The Editor is Dr. Richard Garnett, C. 8., equally known
for his fifty years of service in the British Museum and as
one of the'foremost of English scholars. The association of
Professor Brandi, Professor of Literature in the Royal
University, Berlin ; of M. Leon Vallee, Librarian of the
famous Bibliotheque Nationale of Paris ; and of Donald U.
Mitchell, of the United States, not to speak of many
qualified assistants, suffices to stamp the work as ot the
highest authority.
COMPLETE AND NEW—
It is an absolutely new and original work, the first com-
plete collection of the world’s literature published in
Great Britain. Its mere extent is equalled only by great
works of reference like the “ Encyclopedia Britanmca.
WHAT ITS TWENTY VOLUMES CONTAIN—
Over 300 of the finest poems, over 400 of the best
stories, together with the best of travel and adventure,
philosophy and science, art, wit and humor, letters and
journals, religious meditation, criticism and miscellan-
eous essays which may be gathered from all the books
still preserved among men.
OVER 10,000 ROYAL OCTAVO PAGES—
The Library comprises over 10,000 royal octavo pages,
of large, clear type, cast specially for the Library, and
restful to the most sensitive eyes.
DELIGHTFUL TO READ—
The work is complete in twenty handsome volumes,
printed upon a paper made specially for this work, pre-
senting a clear, soft, unglazed surface, grateful to the
eye, and rendering these books so light that they may be
held with the utmost ease.
ILLUSTRATIONS THAT ARE INTERESTING
The Library is richly embellished with nearly 500 full-
page illustrations and colored plates, printed separately
from the text, on heavy enamelled paper. These illustra-
tions are apt and interesting ; often rare and quaint.
RARE COLORED PLATES—
The colored plates, running through every volume,
form one of the most notable features of the Library.
Thev have been gathered from rare sources, and comprise
some of the most exquisite specimens of the antique art
of book illustration.
A GREAT LIBRARY IN ITSELF—
A thousand volumes could not contain all that one may
find in the “ Library of Famous Literature ” not many
thousand such as the average man or woman would buy,
if they set out to purchase a huge library of the world s
literature. All these treasures are here in the most
compact and convenient form, arranged in chronological
order to show the gradual development of the human
intellect and the art of literature.

♦
The “ Library of Famous Literature ”- gathers together
more than a thousand masterpieces from the great writers
of all ages ; it provides an endless store of good reading
the most interesting portions of all that has been written
since civilisation began ; and, arranged in chronological
order, it affords a fascinating view of the world s literature
from the earliest times to our own day. .
In addition to all this, the Library comprises a series of
delightful essays on the literature of different epochs and
different races, written specially for the Library by the
most distinguished of living men of letters. The follow-
ing list will suffice to indicate the character and scope of
these charming studies :
MR. HENRY JAMES—“ The Rise of the Novel.”
DEAN FARRAR—“The Literature of Religious Cri-
ticism.”
FERDINAND BRUNETIERE—“ French Poetry.”
MAURICE MAETERLINCK—“ The Drama Since Shake-
speare.
BRET HARTE—“ The Rise of the Short Story.”
ARMANDO PALACIO VALDES—'“ The Decadence of
Modern Literature.”
SIR WALTER BESANT—“ Novels That Have Made
History.”
PAUL BOURGET —“The Evolutions of Literary Criti-
cism.”
EMILE ZOLA—“ Naturalism and Romanticism.”
DR. EDWARD DOWDEN—“The Elizabethan Era.”
PROF. PASQUALE VILLARI—“ The Renaissance and
Modern Literature.”

the World.”

Science.”

Literature.”
ANDREW LANG—“ Literature in the Nineteenth Cen-
tury.”

Literature.”
THE EDITOR OF THE LIBRARY, DR. RICHARD
GARNETT, C.B.—“ The Use and Value of Autho-
logies.”

POLITICAL
POINTS
Barton, who gave “ One Man One Vote ”
to N.S.W., ought to hold to the ambition of giving
One Man One Color ” to Australia.
Philp s two-babies-two- votes proposal is
claimed by its supporters to be, after all, only the
logical sequence of “manhood” suffrage.
Kitchener having, by his famous procla-
mation, outlawed Botha, Botha naturally responds
by outlawing Kitchener. So both Commanders-
in-Ohief are outlawed now, and things are level.
1 he Protestant Banner (Sydney) puts
down the murder of M‘Kinle.y to a Popish olot
win Ist the equally reliable local R. C. organ finds
its cause in the Godless system of undenomina-
tional education in IJ.S.A.
Freetrade England gives lOd. per lb.
Protection to home-made cigarettes. 'Result':
Whereas a few years ago American brards had
“ monopoly, now England makes
b,000,000,000 annually “on her own.”/.
At the critical division on the Alien Immi-
gration Bill, Barton had (ominous sign !) eight
knights on his side, and only one (M‘Millan)
against him. It is not good for a man’s democracy
to keep the company of K.C.M.G.’s.
Last S.A. elections, while the male voting
power exceeded the female by 15,000, the female
votes cast were in the majority of 21,000. More
than one-half the male electors failed to record
their votes, but only one-ninth of the females.
The straight-out White Australia vote in
the Federal Parliament revealed a thick black
streak in several rabid white Australians’ compo-
-Bltl°n. The most notable case is that of Wilkin-
son (Q.l who was elected as an out-and-out White
man.
Bruce Smith, of N.S.W. whose fossildom
makes even Sargood and Zeal appear like furious
reformers, last week got in his final denunciation
about the White-Australian “hysteria.” B Smith
was left over by the Mound Builders when they
and the mastodon quitted this earth very long*
ago. He is an able man, and would have been a
boss Mound Builder, but there is no demand for
mounds now, and Smith waves his stone-axe man-
fully in a dead cause.

“ Old Hand ” writes :
Who is the liar ? It was stated in N.S.W. Assembly on
20th Aug. that the inmates of the Govt. Asylums on
getting their old age pensions, went on the razzle and
sought re-admission to the institutions. The members
laughed. It can be easily proved that no inmate received
any pension until Ist Sept. Why does the Govt, insist on
throwing cold water on the old age pension scheme by
making such a glaring mis-statement and many similar
ones ?
An analysis of the voting for a Federal
representative for Darling Downs makes, on
paper, the seats of Bell, Macintosh, and the
recent “’vert,” W. Thorn, none too safe. Bell
was without a doubt the Bhilp nominee, and every
effort was made by the party to have him returned!;
yet, on the face of this the constituencies of Car-
narvon, Dalby, Cambooya and Aubigny gave
Groom handsome majorities, as also did Cunning-
ham, the electorate of Kates, the “ candid friend ”
of the Philp ministry.
The Federal House of Representatives
actually voted, in the debate on the Distillation
Bill, to allow the nefarious potato spirit, or any
other kind of spirit, to be used for fortifying
wines. It thought better of it later on, but even
then the awful mistake was only rectified by a
majority of one/ And it wasn’t consideration for
the corroded Australian stomach that moved it.
The House was only shifted by Isaacs’s impas-
sioned appeal about the prospective ruin of the
export trade if it became known that Australian
wine was adulterated with potato liquor.
