JOB AND HIS FRIENDS.
N.S.W. Legislative Council, after not only
passing the Women’s Suffrage Bill, but growing
so cordial in the good cause that it amended the
measure to give her the right to sit in Parliament
also, changed its alleged mind, and rejected the
Bill at the third reading. This is what comes of
a crowd of dull and deaf old men thinking they
have minds when these implements have long
since fizzled out.
Melb. Age has been trying to work a
quiet job in Vic. politics during the past few
months by offering to take over the Hansard
reporting. So far, the scheme has been kept quiet,
and, according to all indications, it is unlikely to
further. In Adelaide the official reporting is
shared by the morning papers, who do the work
with cheap men, and make a profit to their pro-
prietors of about £3OOO or £4OOO yearly.
Isaacs, of Victoria, attempted unsuccess-
fully to amend the Immigration Restriction Bill
so that the Bill itself wouldn’t shut out anybody,
or enforce any educational test, or make un-
pleasant allusion to any nigger or colored person
whatever. He proposed that it should simply
give the Federal Parliament power, by resolution
of both Houses, to exclude at any time whatever
race it considered undesirable. Even the aggres-
sive Jap could hardly object to that idea. There
would be no reflection on him in the Bill ; so far
as the evidence went it might be intended to shut
out American artisans and German professors of
dead languages. But the suggestion came alto-
get her too late, and it was lost in the hurly-burly.
Reported by cable that sundry American
millionaires are moving to secure control of five of
the great English tobacco-businesses, and if the
purchase can be effected this will practically put
the English tobacco-trade in Yankee hands,
where the match-trade has already gone. Also
offers have been made by Americans to buy up
“?dily the Cunard and _White Star shipping co.’s.
I he United States, which is now reaching out for
a mortgage on the globe, is solid evidence of the
absurdity of that Freetrade doctrine that it is an
infallible sign of prosperity when imports exceed
exports. The U.S. has the biggest surplus of
exports m the world ; and the surplus represents
money exported to pay off the remainder of its
external debts and to buy up odd bits of earth in
all directions.
Victoria has fairly good laws to cope with
the plentiful Chow if they are decently adminis-
tered, but it is made evident that the wire fencing
is anything but Chinky-proof if the party in
charge is not inclined to object very strenuously
when he finds a Celestial wriggling through.
Confucians are very insidious “on their own’"’
but with powerful influences operating to work
the oracle on their behalf they can ooze into the
country like mercury through a sugar-bag.
Trenwith lately made a surprising disclosure in
the most casual manner. It has been the practice
he says, to give Chinamen permits to bring their
sons from China, “but,” he adds, with all the
ingenousness of an oil-painted nun, lam afraid
the ; privilege is being used to import cheap labor ”
and he goes on to imply that he will jolly well
stop it one of these days if somebody doesn’t look
out. Think of the shocked Tribune almost
suspecting at this late hour that the Chinaman
may not be quite honorable in his dealings !
Brisbane Chamber of Manufacturers re-
cently passed a resolution disapproving of a
decimal currency “unless adopted by Great
Britain.” How would it be to carry this touching
faith a little farther, and disapprove of borrowing
money for unproductive works, as Britain doesn’t
do it, and decide that sufficient taxation should be
levied to pay off part of the public debt every
year, as Britain does that ? Why will these
people who are so intensely British in coinage and
Jingo matters treat the example of Britain, with
such utter contempt in other directions ?

“The real object aimed at by the piesent
Q. Pastoral. Leases Extension Bill,” says member
Hardacre, in Brisbane Workkr, “is the further
locking-up against grazing-farm settlement of the
best sheepholds of the State. . . West of the
Main Dividing Range are immense downs, where
land for settlement is in eager demand. Here
extensions of leases have previously been secured
and have now from 13 to 20 years to run. And
yet . . the moving advocates . . . are seek-
ing to secure furthur extension of these leases,
which the present Bill proposes to give ! ”
The division on the Watson amendment
to exclude Asiatics from the Commonwealth is so
far the historical one of the Australian Parlia-
ment; 67 members divided (36 to 31), and six
“paired.” The only two votes unaccounted for
were the Speaker’s and that of a Queens-
lander away on business. Cruickshank (N.S.W.)
and three Victorian supporters of the Barton
Govt, voted with the Opposition (Reid’s following
and the Labor party). The Govt, was saved and
the amendment defeated by Bruce Smith, Dugald
Thomson, G. B. Edwards (N.S.W.), and Braddon
(las.), of the Opposition, refusing to follow Reid.
The Library of Famous Literature.
Announcement is made in our columns this week of
the offer in New South Wales of copies of the “ Library of
Famous Literature ” reserved for the Australian States.
This work was compiled under the direction of Dr.
Richard Garnett, C. 8., for many years in charge of the
great library in the British Museum. He has been
assisted in his labors by M. Leon Vallee (Librarian of the
Biblioth6que Nationale), who has not only brought- the
s P eciali sed experience, but has also enriched
the Library ”by the contribution of an admirable study
of French literature. Professor Brandi, who occupies the
Chair of Literature in the Imperial University -of Berlin
has also been associated with Dr. Garnett in the
preparation of the “ Library,” and has written,
for the fourth volume of the work, a study of
The Main Currents of German Literature.” Mr. Donald
G. Mitchell, who is, perhaps, better known under his
familiar pseudonym of “Ik Marvel,” is responsible for
those sections of the work which deal with that Ameri-
can literature which, in a good deal less than a working
century, has added so extensively to our bookshelves
The plan of the work is simplicity itself. The twenty
volumes of the “Library of Famous Literature ” contain
the best parts of each author’s work, not a ragged extract
but a carefully-chosen complete picture, in that author’s”
particular style, enough for half-an-hour’s reading-
enough to give the reader the desired sense of an intel-
lectual change of air. More than a thousand of those
examples are offered in a handsome and eonvenient form
Nor is the result a mere compromise. The best thing aii
author ever wrote is, for the purpose of occasional read-
i better than the whole body of his work, since it
enables the reader to get at the essence of his creation
without losing time over the straw and chaff which form
a part of even the most precious literary growths.
The Work, when issued last year by the Standard, in
London, met with phenomenal success, more than 19 000
sets of 20 volumes each being sold within a few months
As the terms upon which the work is offered here are
even more favourable than those which prevailed in
London including free delivery as far as Sydney, a pro-
portionally large sale is anticipated in Australia The
number of copies available for New South Wales is strictly
limited, however, and intending purchasers should apply
tor particulars at once. The application form, which
appears in another column, should be cut out and posted
to-day.


A Question
Answered.
If the Pianola ena-
bles those who
have absolutely no
musical knowledge
to play the piano,
of what interest is
it to those who are
able to play ?
This is often asked, and when asked in our showrooms, generally results in the
purchase of a Pianola ; but more often the question finds expression only in
thought, and offers no opportunity for an answer.
PADEREWSKI HAS A PIANOLA in his home.
ROSENTHAL HAS ONE.
SAUER HAS ONE.
and only recently has ordered two more for friends in Germany, who are themselves
eminent musical critics. This certainly disposes of the question, although it fails to
give the reason. The reason is equally clear.
Pianola being placed in position to play Upright Piano.
The Pianola increases the
repertory of even these musi-
cians from twenty-five to fifty
compositions which they can
play in concert form, to prac-
tically everything they may
desire to play. It increases
the repertory of every skilled
musician, and gives a reper-
tory to the novice.
The Pianola does not mar
the piano, as it may be moved
up to or away from the key-
board at will.
The Pianola does not mar
the playing, as artistic taste
receives full opportunity for
expression to which the player
can give his undivided atten-
tion.
The Pianola offers relaxa-
tion from the technical and
enjoyment of the musical.
It is true, since the player
controls the expression, the great musician obtains a more artistic result from the
Pianola than the novice, because he is more critical, but in this very point lies the
secret of the Pianola’s fascination.
The player, whoever he may be, gives his own individuality to his rendition.
This causes the musical taste to develop, and it is not long before the veriest novice
finds his own playing preferable to many of the pianistic efforts in the average concert.
Expression-marks appear on the musisas it unrolls, for the guidance of those who
may be unfamiliar with the selection.
In this article there has been little attempt to explain how the Pianola is manipu-
lated, the principal object being to show the desirability of the Pianola for every one
who owns a piano, whether he be skilled in its use or not.
We shall be pleased to mail a complete description of the instrument upon request,
although we urge all who have the opportunity to hear the Pianola for themselves.
Can be bought by instalments if desired. Send for Catalogue C.
HENRY M. BIRGE & CO
Sole Agents for Australasia.
The Pianola is sold at no other
Address in Sydney.

MR. S. A. PALMER.
Dear Sir—l had been a great sufferer from indigestion
for five or six years, with headaches, giddiness, heavy and
oppressed feelings. Added to these about eight months
ago severe pains seized one of my legs from the knee
downwards, and became a source of real agony to me,
destroying my rest at nights, and causing me worry and
trouble during the day. After much pain and anxiety I
called at your Adelaide office about four months ago, and
as the result decided to try Vitadatio, and I took four
bottles. It is now three months since I finished the last
bottle, and I am pleased to say that the excruciating pain
has entirely left my leg. The headaches and oppressed
feelings have disappeared. I am able to enjoy food that
I dared not touch before, and feel very thankful for what
Vitadatio has done for me.
You may mention my name to any enquirer who may
be interested, and who may desire relief such as I have
obtained.
(MRS.) S. WOODHEAD.

The painfully familiar story of the lying
hussy crops up again in ML. Two innocents,
one-time inmates of a children's home, gave a
circumstantial account of having been cruelly
wronged bv the master, since resigned, and the
man was committed for trial. The allegation was
that he had committed the offences, on four
different occasions, in daylight, and m rooms
accessible to 30 inmates and the matron On one
alleged occasion both girls were together, the
liars broke down hopelessly under cross-examina-
tion, and the Grown proceeded no further. Ihe
small girl whose diseased mind suggests getting a
splurge of excitement out of a rape charge never
is proceeded with further. It is its absolute safety
that popularises this form of amusement.

The Blight of Phthisis.
The religion of militant medicine was preached at
a. meeting in Sydney on Monday night. Resolu-
tions, spoken to by prominent physicians, were
agreed upon requesting the State to build a
hospital for advanced cases of phthisis, and the
citizens to join an association to assist in the fight
against this disease by private effort. Phthisis is
the most deadly of all human ills in Australia.
Yearly it claims over 4000 victims—more than
typhoid, scarlet fever, diphtheria and other fevers
combined—yet modern medical science knows it to
be curable, and knows that, with care, it might
be utterly stamped out of Australia just as
the plague was stamped out in N.S.W, If an
earnest effort were made, not in one State alone,
but throughout the whole of Australia to fight
phthisis, in a generation or two it would occupy
the same place in the vital history of Australia as
smallpox does now. But the consumptive patient,
the most pitiable of all, is, at the same time the
most neglected of all. In N.S.W. the State
spends on the patient in a metropolitan hospital
from £65 to £75 a year. The sick man at Little
Bay Hospital costs £6B 11s. a year, and on the
hoodlum m gaol £3O a year is lavished; but
on the consumptive in that awful den, Liverpool
Asylum, is spent only £l5 6s. sd. a year.
He is most in need of generous diet and spacious
living accommodation (in Germany the phthisical
costs a hospital twice as much as the ordinary
patient), and he is starved and kept in an
absolute Black Hole on .£l5 a year, whilst the
garotter battens on £3O a year. Dr. Nash, M. L. C.,
who has collated these figures, states that the
criminal consumptive, who gets from gaol to the
Liverpool hospital, is anxious always to go back
to gaol, for the treatment is better there. The
shame of this fact should haunt Premier See until
a change is made. Dr. Sydney Jones pointed out
that with proper sanitary dwellings for the poor
consumption would be driven out of its very
citadel: but he held out no hope of any early
reform of slum tenements. Surely, though, if the
greed of landlords is so largely responsible for
breeding this poison in the community, there is no
need to calmly knuckle down to them. If the
funny police-court reporter recorded one day that
Silas Ebenezer Purse, a stout gentleman, mottled
with purple, and of pronounced religious convic-
tions, was sentenced to six months hard labor for
letting poisonous, insanitary premises, the tuber-
culous dens would soon disappear. The State
grapples fearlessly with the blights which afflict
trees—with phylloxera, potato-grub, codlin-moth,
orange rust. Human life, in a community where
babies are comparatively infrequent, is valuable
enough to warrant as much care as, say, the potato-
crop.
The latest concession demanded by the
unspeakably impudent and squalid promoters of
the Great Western (Tas.) railway syndicate is
that £7OOO deposited by the original promoters
with the Treasurer as a guarantee of good faith
be returned. It is promised that the money will
be spent in surveys, etc., and if the co. fails to
float during another 12 months the Govt, is told
that it may step into possession of all the plans in
lieu of the £7OOO ! If the concern again fails to
float in London the plans, etc., will probably be
valuable as pipe-lights for the Cabinet, but it will
take a lot of paper to outweigh 7000 golden so vs.
Anyhow, Tas. Parliament decided to part with
the £7OOO.
Clement Wragge, the meteorological wild
cat of Queensland, has no luck. His much-
bragged-of “high-level station” on Kosciusko
never produced anything in particular-
in fact, Wragge only started that frozen
elephant when the high-level station idea
was worn out and about dead. And now
he has struck a patch of ill-luck that is
really memorable. Wragge’s latest craze is
the Stiger vortex gun—said to be beneficial in pre-
venting the fall of hail, though Wragge is using
it to accelerate the fall of rain. And last week,
having carefully explained in his forecast that it
was likely to rain in any case, Wragge publicly
fired his big gun at the clouds in Brisbane (one of
the dampest places in South Queensland), and the
weather remained dry immediately. Whereupon it
was wired that Wragge was quite satisfied and was
going to fire two guns at the clouds next time. If
the old theorist keeps on at it he will probably
at last strike a time when it was going to rain in
any case, and then the spectacle of Wragge as the
Man Who Did It All will be something immense.
Concerning the case of Vic. Private Steele,
lately sentenced to death in S’Africa :
Trouble threatens over the intimation that one Steele,
a member of the Victorian Fifth Contingent, was lately
sentenced to death for “inciting to commit mutiny. 11
The facts, as stated in Melbourne, are that Steele was a
member of the last Vic. contingent, one section of which
was cut up severely at Wilmansrust by the Boers, who
rushed its camp at night while the men were asleep, with
their rifles stacked. Twenty Victorians were killed and
many wounded, and all who did not escape were taken
prisoners. After the affair, one Major Beatson, an Im-
perial officer, characterised the Vic. contingent as
“ white-livered curs,” notwithstanding that the Wilmans-
rust disaster was wholly due to the incompetence of an
Imperial man, subsequently retired. The Victorians, not
relishing the epithet, made audible comments on the
quality of British courage as shown at Nicholson’s Nek
and some other places, and, eventually, Beatson found
it advisable to tell a Vic. officer that he apologised
for what he had said. Then came the order that
the Vies, were to go out under the command of the man
who had characterised them as “ white-livered curs ”
Whereupon Private Steele told his mates to “ chuck down
their rifles, and refuse to go out under Beatson, unless he
apologised personally.” Two of the men took up the
cry ; but, ere anything could happen, word reached head-
quarters, and all three were arrested and court-martialled
for inciting to mutiny. Found guilty, Steele was sen-
tenced to be shot, and the others were sent to gaol for
two years apiece. Subsequently, Steele’s sentence was
commuted by Kitchener to 10 years in a British gaol with
the scum of England. There is already a very bitter
feeling in a wide Australian circle over this matter. There
is no doubt that the free-and-easy Australian does cor-
dially resent the overbearing insolence of the typical
Imperial officer, and crowds of Steele’s compatriots will
insist on the Commonwealth demanding his release. The
incident may yet loom large in politics.

Another wise man has spoken. Sydney
Cooper, the veteran cattle-painter, savs he has
not tasted tea or coffee for over half-a-century,
but although 96 years of age, is enabled to con-
tinue working by moderate potations of “Old
scotch at luncheon, dinner, and bed time In
asmuch as “the best old Scotch” means D.C.L.,
and nothing else, this grand old apostle adds
another to the long list of intellects whose strict
adherence to D.C.L. has won for that brand its
well-known title—the Whisky of the Wise.*
The Macgullykiddies wanted to see what
cat-pancakes would look like, so they started to
put the family-mouser tail foremost through the
mangle, which was a new plaything for the
youngsters, and easy to work. . Macgillycuddy,
hearing frantic yells, rushed into the wash-house
in. time to save most of the animal, though its
tail, as a tail, was ruined for ever. The cat always
paid a hurried visit next door when washing-day
came round after that, and never truly appreciated
what was really a first-class mangle—the Ewbank
Jewel—which Mac. bought for 555. at M‘Lean
Bros, and Bigg, Ltd., Melbourne.*
A small tragedy took place on Woolloo-
mooloo wharf t'other day. One of 63 sledge-dogs,
en route to join the German Antarctic Expedition
proved so savage that a policeman was asked to
destroy him which he did with an iron bar. Hard
luck for a dog coming all the way from lone
Siberia to be brained by a Woolloomooloo bobby.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Lucknow, with which Kelso had two good wins
at the recent A.J.G. Spring meeting, first saw light at
Rylstone. His dam was bred by Hon. Geo. Lee, at Kelso,
and was one of a buggy pair sold by him to a Presbyterian
parson for 60 guineas. After the mare had done good
service for about five years the parson decided to breed
from her, and she was accordingly sent to Widden, where
she spent the remainder of her days. Her first visit was
to Russley, and Lucknow was the result. He also did
harness duty for a time, but, becoming too flighty for the
rev. owner, was sold for £l5. The rest of the mare’s
progeny were sold very cheaply, and will doubtless be
heard of ere long. In other hands Lucknow’s dam would
have proved a little gold-mine.

Jcickeying is being purified in Maoriland.
Black-markers," pub. loafers, and the card fraternity
are being refused licenses all over the land, and the ranks
skimmed of the baser element. Auckland Racing Club
u ?.? one further than that in ordaining that any jock
holding a license and not in regular employ must give a
satisfactory reason for his idleness.

Plebeian origin of sundry M.L. crack nr ads •
Goldspur once ran in a Dunedin tram, and didn’t reveal
his great sprinting powers till late in life. Moifaa was a
mounted-infantry hack who got into the Hawke’s Bay
Hunt Club programme somehow; and Kohunni used to
hump the mails between Martinborough and the coast
and was valued at £7.

Now that Vic. football season is over the rats
begin to squeak, and it is coming out that a number of
high-up clubs have been controlled bv a syndicate of
metalhcians, who simply decided how matches were to
finish according to their books. The downfall of Vic.
football—probably the best outdoor game ever invented
bo h for player and spectator—dates from the time when
a big sport.” stiffened enough players in a leading team
to enable him to win a pot by backing t’other side. Since
then the public has looked with suspicion on football but
was just beginning to get back confidence when this’ ex-
posure came along. There is less excuse for crooked
practices in football than in any other sport, seeing that
the amount of wagering on any match is
nificant as compared with the money held by the ring at
an obscure suburban meeting. That big men who deal in
thousands at Cup-time should trouble about such a thing
at all only shows that some people would sooner flatter
their own smartness by getting ss. “on the cross” than
£1 squarely.

Sydney cricket season opened on Saturday with
the initial round of local matches. Previous night’s rain
favored bowlers, who scored well in most instances. Best
batting was shown at Hampden Park where Paddington
last year s premier club, piled up 337 for 7 wickets, Victor
Trumper contributing 158, which topped individual
scores. Pyes 133, not out, for Central Cumberland
against Glebe ranked next. W. R. Iredale got 91 for
Paddington. R. A. Duff and A. J. Hopkins made 71 and
75 respectively of North Sydney’s 184 against Waverlev ;
and, for Leichhardt-Balmam against Sydney, Diamond’s
score of 53 was highly creditable, as he had four good
bowlers, including Marsh, against him.

first-class matches. Clem Hill, under a new strict enforce-
ment of the electoral system, will have to play for East
Torrens, which has just lost Lyons and Darling Mac-
Laren’s team opens the season on Nov. 9, and N.S.W.
pays a visit in January, in readiness for the third test-
match.
* * * •
MacLaren had, at latest advices, made two bad
blunders in captainship in consecutive matches. Against
Derby he declared his first innings closed at 183 for five
wickets : result, the opponents led by 26 on the innings
Then, after winning the toss, he put Sussex in first;
result, Fry, Ranji, and co. made 457 for seven, and were
able securely to “declare.”
• * * *
N.S.W.-Victoria tennis tourney, on Sydney
Cricket Ground, Friday and Saturday, was hampered by
bleak weather and sodden courts (which favored the
visitors) first day. The finish on Saturday was the closest
on record—N.S.W. winning by only one point. Actual
figures were—N.S.W. : 10 rubbers in singles, 20 pts., and
4in doubles, 12 pts. ; total, 32 pts. Victoria : 8 rubbers
in singles, 16 pts., and 6 in doubles, 15 pts. ; total, 31 pts
Closest previous victory was by 2 sets.
* * * •
The Vic. tennis team was among the strongest
seen in Sydney. Kearney, fresh from big achievements
in Britain, played splendidly on the first day, beating
Gaden, Irving and Rice in the singles. Vic. doubles-
champions (Brooks and Fraser) never lost a set on Satur-
day, but Spence’s play was the most brilliant and force-
ful. His slow, skilful cutting from the left court on the
return of the service drove a boundless ball down on the
mde lines within a yard or so of the net, and utterly
baffled the server. Rice, of N.S.W., is shaping excellently
and will make a stern fight for the championship to-day
(Saturday). J
* * * •
Lesna, who claimed, when in Australia, to be
a champion behind pace ” rather than a racer, seems to
have lost the recent Paris-to-Brest-and-baek event only
by bad judgment. He did the outward half, 372 miles, in
just under 24 hours, being miles ahead of anything else,
but had to give up when 135 miles from home. Winner,
a - fc 9 ok llmin., or over 14 miles an hour
right through. Motor-pacing was not allowed.
* * * *
Another big match between pigeon-slayers
Eales and Gorrick. Pity some of the aggressive
Puritanical energy directed against “Tatt.’s” harm-
less gamble hadn’t been brought to bear on this
barbarous alleged sport.
* * * •
Victorian draughts championship—won lately
by previous holder, F. T. Best—was notable for the clever
play of a Ballarat youngster of 15, G. D. Cairns. The boy
shook-up a lot of the old hands who know the “ book ” of
the game better than they know their alphabet and only '
went down in the final to Best.
• * * *
Telegraphic chess match, N.S.W. v. Q., played
28th Sept., resulted in a hollow win for N.S.W. by 8
games to 2. The Banana State had a very weak team
Champion Jacobsen was one of two Walers beaten, losing
to A.M.P. Secretary Apperley.
• * * *
A. Jewell last week bolted with the West Aus-
tralian 50 miles road championship record on a Massey. *
Pedal-pusher, Bob Mutton, at the Agricultural
Grounds, did an unpaced quarter in 27sec. dead on a Red
Bird—New South Wales record.*
• * * »
Champion sprinter McManus stripped no less
than five times for a handicap at Newcastle t’other day
In the first round he dead-heated with Dowrer, Gilmour
and Pogonowski, and in the run-off finished abreast of
Dowrer, who went under in the third effort. Later the
judge couldn’t separate McManus, Smith and Davis but
a second essay saw the back-marker (McManus) beaten
with much of the steam out of him. McManus and M L
crack McLachlan will contend for a stake and the “ gate ”
at Newcastle to.day (Saturday)—McManus conceding

Fate ! Had M'Kinley been defeated by
Bryan at last election, the assassin’s bullet would
surely have found another billet.
The sordid row about the bill is still alive
in Melbourne. That roomful of gorgeous furni-
ture which was bought because some unauthorised
person waved his hand and said, Bo the tiling
properly! ” has been returned, but £lBO is
demanded for the hire of it ; and the trouble
about the 248 (or thereabouts) bottles of strong
drink which were got in for the alleged refresh-
ment of the duke and duchess hasn’t fully sim-
mered down even now. Apparently in the whole
tribe of “ loyal ” FitzGribbons there isn’t one who
will pay a copper to prevent the duke’s refresh-
ment becoming a laughing-stock.
The retirement of Mr. Mair from Sydney
stipendiary bench should be taken advantage of to
appoint a strong-jawed, fairly young man who
will have no hesitation in dealing out vigorous
“stoush ”to pushites and hoodlums. Mr. Mair is
well-advanced in years and has lately been some-
what troubled with deafness. He had not the
best of luck. Whenever there was a very con-
tentious and notable case before the police-court
with Jack Want’s chin wagging hard on one side,
it was almost certain to strike Mr. Mair; and
these stormy interludes in the peaceful chant of
“ten shillings or the rising” are not appreciated
by magistrates.
Nurse Macready (a daughter of the lately-
deceased Sydney veteran Presbyterian cleric of
that name), recently back from the war, tells
Sydney D.T. things that ought to make every
back-number of that paper blush for the wilful
lies, or woeful ignorance, of its Jingoistic
creators :
Speaking of the Boer, Miss Macready says he has an
unshakeable belief in the justness of his quarrel. He
values the independence of his country above all things,
and in his heart is prepared to fight on until beaten past
recovery. And when he is beaten he will not be con-
quered. That may appear a paradox. Yet Miss Macready
says it represents the spirit of the Boer. And it is be-
cause he values so highly his independence that he deeply
felt the hand the Australians took in the war. He was
prepared to admit that the English soldiers were doing
nothing but their duty as they saw it, but believing that
the Australians were'living under conditions similar to
those which existed in South Africa, the average Boer
looked upon the Australian interference as unjustifiable.
Of the immediate and final termination of hostilities,
Miss Macready is not very hopeful. Her belief is that
Great Britain has a most difficult problem to face in South
Africa. The feeling among Boer men and women alike is
most bitter towards Great Britain, and the restrictions
which military government means is, she adds, beginning
to cause irritation to the South Afrikander. The Boers
themselves will never forget nor forgive the burning of
their farms. The refugee camps are also likely to prove
difficult things to handle. Behind the establishment of
these camps was the idea that, by taking the women away,
the Boers could not so readily obtain food, and would he
robbed of one of the sources of information. What has
really happened is that the Boers, relieved of the imme-
diate necessity of looking after the women and children,
have greater freedom to travel from place to place, and
prosecute the war as opportunity offers. Nor does Nurse
Macready look to the future with feelings of hopefulness.
So devastated is the country, she says, that years will be
required to re-settle it in anything like its former shape,
and the Boer women, even if an apparent ending is made,
will foster in the minds of their children the hatred for
the English which they now have in common with their
husbands.

Obit. : Capt. R. J. Skinner, oldest skipper
in the Newcastle Co.’s service. He had been in
the Australian shipping trade since the 60’s, and
an acknowledgment was made of his thorough
knowledge of the N.S.W. coast when he was
asked to pilot the Ophir with the Duke on board
from the Hawkesbury.

A remarkable youngster died in Perth
Hospital at the end of August—“ Tony ”
Ryan. In the middle of his law-course in Victoria
he jumped off with the early rushes to the West,
battled out as far as Malcolm, made more than
tucker, and took a goldfields’ pub., sold out well,
and returned to Vic. and the law, but, with only
a final exam, to pass, soon rushed back to fight the
Malcolm seat. The pace killed—through some
affection of the brain —at 26.
\e gods ! The Boer war threatens to
return to its old, inexplicable entanglements in
the Tugela River districts. Are we never to hear
the last of the Upper Tugela and the Lower
Tugela, and will they be “forded’’and “flooded”
this coming summer as they were two years ago ?

WHY WE ADVERTISE!
Because Advertising is the legitimate means vouchsafed to enlighten people,
to acquaint each other with their avocations and wants. If a man has a
Speciality, and has devoted years of his life and expended large sums of money in studying that Speciality and in
purchasing the necessary appliances, etc., for treating certain diseases, it would be absurd for him not to announce
the fact, and disseminate the knowledge universally, We have no apology to offer for informing the world through
this advertisement and otherwise of the means of relief we can afford to sufferers who need our help. Therefore,
however opposed to your own preconceived ideas and to those of your medical advisers, our method of treatment
may be, the fact still remains that it is successful, and this is verified by the thousands of testimonials received from
grateful patients. We make no secret of our mode of practice. We only claim to have utilised in a common-sense
way the result of scientific research and long experience. If you listen to the counsel of your sceptical friends and
jealous physician you may omit consulting us, but remember if you allow the disease to run riot in your system, you
are guilty of culpable neglect, and are alone responsible for your future pitiful condition.
Nine Diplomas and Certificates of Qualification and Registration by Government
Medical Boards of New South Wales, Victoria, South Australia, Great Britain
and America Open to Inspection by Dr. Wallace, our Physic an-in-Chief.
Late Consulting Staff, Homcepathic Hospital, Melbourne.
Late Medical Staff, British Army.
—lf you live out of town and cannot visit us, this need not deprive-
you of the benefits of our wonderful treatment, because in the new
system of combining medicine and electricity a complete Electro-Medical home treatment is provided for. Scarcely a
day passes that the news does not reach us of some wonderful home cure of a person living so far away that a per-
sonal visit could not be made. Write a plain statement of your condition, explaining your feelings and symptoms as.
clearly as possible, and from it we will be able to accurately diagnose your case. It only costs you the trouble of;
writing, and twopence postage to get the opinion and ad\ ice of Australia’s Leading Specialists.
DO NOT BE DRUGGED TO DEATH BY QUACKS
When there are so many quacks and impudent pretenders in the field of Medicine, who arrogate to themselves,
the monstrosity of giving' counsel and advice in matters of which they are profoundly ignorant, arid who have
nothing to recommend them but their audacity, sufferers cannot be too careful whom they consult. Although
liberty of choice belongs to all, discrimination is necessary. In consulting THE FREEMAN AND WALLACE
ELECTRO-MEDICAL AND SURGICAL INSTITUTE patients may feel assured that they shall receive the advice
of those fitted by superior skill and education, the result of special study. Our reputation and unparalleled:
success as Specialists entitle us to your confidence.

The Eucrasg Company’s Book
. . ON . .
DRUNKENNESS.
The Eucrasy Company, of Sydney, extracts from whose
book on the disease of drunkenness have been published
by this paper from time to time, have lately issued the
third edition of their pamphlet, explaining the action of
Eucrasy, and pointing out their method of curing those
addicted to the liquor habit.
The Eucrasy cure is one of the oldest, having been
before the public since 1882, at which time sanatoriums
were in successful operation in many cities in America,
and the succees of their remedy is shown by hundreds of
grateful patients all over the world.
A well-known merchant, residing at Newcastle, says :
“ I have much pleasure in testifying to the excellent
qualities of Eucrasy. I felt even after the first dose a
different person, and may say now that lam cured. The
craving has entirely gone, an d I can with full confidence
recommend it to anyone, no matter what hold the liquor
habit may have of them.
“ Thanking you sincerely for your kindness, I am,
yours faithfully,
Any reader of The Bulletin can obtain this book free
by addressing THE EUCRASY CO., 62 Hunter Street,
Sydney.

A Brisbane Appetite.
Nobopy can tell the following story better than
the writer tells it himself.
“In 1891,” he says, “I first experienced the
miseries of acute indigestion. I was obliged to
knock off work, and suffered agonies for two
months. Then I felt slightly better and went
back to my post at the North British Fire Brigade
Station, and stayed there until the great flood in
1893.
“Then I had a terribly severe relapse which
laid me down for ten months, seven weeks of it
being spent in the hospital.
“ Several doctors attended me, but they failed
to effect a cure.
" Yet, at the end of ten months I felt somewhat
better, and being of an energetic disposition, I
started in to work again.
“I managed to keep at it for six months and
then collapsed completely, and resigned my
position in the Brigade.
“On returning home several of my friends
urged me to put my faith in Seigel’s Syrup, and
in that only, saying they had known it to cure
cases as bad as mine. ‘Don’t be down-hearted,’
they said.
“On this I began using Seigel’s Syrup (and
Seigel’s Pills occasionally) and gradually got
better. When I had taken about twelve bottles
in all, I was so much better, I accepted a position
as mate in a ship going to trade in the Islands —
taking a supply of the Syrup with me.
“ I must mention that I kept on using Seigel’s
Syrup until we got to Sydney, and then I was
quite restored to health, and have been perfectly
well ever since.
“My health is now splendid; my appetite is
excellent, and all the bad symptoms I once had
are gone like an ugly dream after morning breaks.
“ My wife uses the medicine for a milder form
of indigestion than mine was, and is as firm a
believer in it as I am.
“I will name a few of the peculiarities of the
aggravating and dangerous disease as it affected
me. There was always a bad taste in my mouth,
my breath was bad, and I used to retch frequently
after eating ; I belched continually, lost flesh, and
had thin, lantern jaws.
“Scores of friends and acquaintances can
testify to the truth of what j have said. It is
certain that Mother Seigel’s Syrup cured me
when all other medicines and forms of treatment
proved to be no good, and I have to thank the
Syrup only for the fact that I am now a strong,
healthy man. I have now been nearly three
years in the employment of the Queensport Meat
Company, but my home is at the address given
below.” A. C. Blackmann, Myrtle Villa, George
Street, Kangaroo Point, Brisbane, Q. September
Oth, 1900.
At Poverty Point.
Mr. Tyrone Power writes from Melb. Princess’s In
justice to Mr. Maurice Barrymore, who is not here himself
to make the. author of the foul remark ‘literary bounder,’
bite every bitter word of it, permit me to speak in hi's
behalf. Far from being a ‘bounder’ of any sort, Mr.
Barrymore is a man respected in America and in England
for his talents as an actor and an author. Your ‘ scribe ’
here accuses Mr. Barrymore of forming his play
‘Nadjezda’ upon the models of Sardou’s ‘Fedora’ and
‘La Tosca.’ Why ? What does he know about the
matter? Had he made inquiries, or sought for infor-
mation, instead of relying upon his ignorance, he would
have learnt differently. As a matter of fact, ‘Nadjezda’
was written long before either ‘ Fedora ’ or ‘ La Tosca ’—
Barrymore having no other inspiration than his own
intelligence ; Mr. Barrymore himself lending the play to
Sarah Bernhardt, with a view to its production in Paris,
and when later Bernhardt produced ‘ Fedora ’ by Sardou’
Mr. Barrymore threw the gauntlet of plagiarism' into Mr.
Sardou’s teeth. The gauntlet has yet to be taken up ”
[To which The Bulletin scribe : Ist. That Mr. Barry-
more’s yarn re Sarah Bernhardt, “ Nadjezda,” and Sardou
looks like mere authoi’s bluff—an advertising dodge.
If he ever did charge Sardou with plagiarism in the long
ago, when Tyrone Power was a youth, then the eminent
French author treated Barrymore as a “bounder” by
ignoring his “ gauntlet.” Probably the “ gauntlet ” story
is a modern fiction. What does Mr. Power know about
the matter, except what he has heard from the older
showman ? 2nd. The Bulletin scribe is a better authority

Regina Nagd was presumably known to Nellie Mitchell
Melba m her Very young days, for her father had a small
farm at Lilvdale (Vic.), close to the station of David
Mitchell. Regina joined the local 0. of E. choir, and
when Bishop Goe came on a tour she was selected to sing
the solos in honor of the visit. Mrs. Goe remarked the
beauty of the young girl’s voice, and took an interest in
her later when, through the kindness of some Lilydale
friends, she came to Melbourne to get tuition from Mine
Steinhauer. Everyone now knows that Nagel’s first
success was her singing of “ Ben Bolt ” behind the scene
in Trilby, but the story of her subsequent Westralian
tour is not so well known. Zeb. Lane asked her and the
company with which she was travelling to visit the Great
Boulder mine, and way down in the bowels of the earth
the organ-toned contralto was persuaded to give ’em the
Lost Chord.” Naturally, the little crowd thought thev
had never heard anything half so beautiful, in a mine dr
out of it. The managers of other mines on the Boulder
Mill got her to visit their shows and sing, and the amiable
young woman did her throat considerable harm bv
obliging them. J
There s a horrible fascination for the public in the
dinner of steak and ’taters which Bland Holt furnishes
for the Baker household in his latest drama. Bland
ostentatiously peels the bond-fide vegetables and pops
them into a pot over the stage fire what time the steak is
getting cooked. A few minutes later the meat is dished
the taters also are turned out of the steaming pot, and
the company start a savage attack on their respective
helpings just ere the curtain falls. The actual consump-
tion of steak is so palpable that the audience are filled
with misgivings re the potatoes. Sympathetic matrons
protest against Bland’s inhumanity. lie gets credit for
ordering the co. to devour ’taters which were not more
than lOsec. “on the boil.” Even strong men totter forth
to the refreshment-bar exclaiming, “ Godelpem if thev
swallow those spuds ! ” J

Concerning newspaper thieves :—
Dear Bulletin, — Re the recent gaoling of a man
for derision of court in the stealing of Charles
Arnold’s “ What Happened to Jones,” and merely
altering the title. Can’t anything be done to impu-
dent thief-papers, like London Photo Bits, in which
(issue of 12/1/’01) I recently came upon an original
Bulletin story, printed l/ll/’OO, under title “ How-de-
do ? ” The London thieves had merely re-named the yarn
“Seventeen Years After,” and altered the locale from
Melbourne to London. The impudence of the thief-
editor is astounding, anyhow. I tried to sell a story to
a M.L. leading paper, but had it returned because “ they
could get things so much better from English magazines
for nothing.” I then went away, translated De Maupas-
sant’s “ La Rivi&re ” into English, brought it to the editor
as mine, and begged him to read it and compare it with
some of the English work. He read it, and was “ sorry to
say it was ‘ not up.’ ” I came down gradually from £1 to
2s. 6d.—and then told him the thing was a masterpiece
of the prince of short-story writers. But he only smiled
serenely and went on with his work.—P.A.
Premier Philp was recently asked by a
member of Q. Assembly, if he (the Premier) were
supplied by the questioner with the names of
civilservants employing colored aliens in Brisbane,
in contravention of the Pacific Island Laborers’
Act of ’92, would he prosecute. The Courteous
I riend of the Fatted Syndicate immediately
replied : “ The lion, member can lay the informa-
tion himself. If he does so he will be entitled to
half the penalty inflicted, the same as any other
informer ! ” The law with regard to the employ-
ment of Pacific Islanders is broken every day in
Queensland with absolute impunity—so long as
the offender is a Somebody.

Invalids
AND
THE AGED.


Nervousness and General Debility.
Suffered enough to break
anv Constitution.
Another Thorough Restoration.
The Case of
MRS. FRANCES STARR
(BY a special.)
Our representative called upon Mrs.
Frances Starr, of 206 Victoria Street North,
Darlinghurst, for the purpose of ascertaining
the particulars concerning her recovery from
a distressing, attack of nervousness and
general debility, and upon explaining the
nature of his errand was cordially invited to
take a seat.
“ To what cause do you assign the com-
mencement of your suffering? ” enquired the
reporter.
“Well, I must tell you that some years
ago I contracted an internal complaint which
caused me to undergo medical treatment,
and the pain I endured at that time was
enough to break down any woman’s con-
stitution for life. When the doctors had
done all they could for me I was too weak
to do anything, and as I have always been
used to doing plenty of work you may guess
how low-spirited I felt. My nerves were
quite shattered, and the simplest thing
would cause me to tremble most violently.
It is impossible for me to describe the head-
aches I used to have, and they probably
seemed all the more painful because I could
scarcely ever get to sleep, and whenever I
did manage to snatch an hour’s doze it was
of such a restless description that I did not
feel any benefit from it. I would wake up
with a start, and my nerves would shake all
over my body. A feeling of abject depression
was always upon me, for I commenced to
despair of ever getting well and strong
again. When in these despondent moods I
was frequently seized with hysterics, and
would sit and cry for hours. My face was
thin and as white as a ghost’s, and my lips
were dry and bloodless. Whenever I at-
tempted to walk about a sensation of giddi-
ness would come upon me, and I had great
difficulty in saving myself from falling. To
make matters worse, I was everlastingly
catching colds—through being so weak, I
suppose —and so I had to wrap myself in
flannels ; but that did not prevent me catch-
ing thorn all the same. My constitution,
was that weak and exhausted, and there
appeared so little hope of recovery, that I
lost interest in life.”
“ Your nerves must have been greatly
unstrung?”
“ That is no name for it. They were in a *
state of collapse; for the slightest noise of
an unusual kind would cause a feeling of
terror to run through me, and I did not like 5
being left by myself. The sound of children’s
voices was a source of annoyance to me, and
I was nearly always in an irritable mood.
My greatest trouble, I believe, was an entire
absence of appetite, because I knew that if
I could persuade myself to eat a little there
was yet a chance of becoming stronger.
But it rarely occurred that I could touch
any food, and, as a consequence, I became as
thin as a skeleton.”
“Were you receiving medical advice all*
this time ? ”
“Oh, yes; but it did not improve my
condition in the least. I was thoroughly
prostrated for weeks, and unable to attend
to my business, although I took all the
medicines and pills that the doctors pre-
scribed—all to no purpose. Nothing they
ordered mo did the slightest good. I was
becoming weaker and more nervous every
day through my inability to eat and sleep,
and would spend my time picturing a miser-
able future for myself. The physic I used
to take according to my doctor’s instruc-
tions had but one effect—that of making me
feel sick, and increasing the misery of my
feelings after each dose. My condition was
about as hopeless as it is possible to imagine,
and I felt quite sure that I would never be
able to go about my work like I used to in
the days of my health and strength, which
appeared to have vanished for ever. Friends
would call and sympathise with me, and
advise me not to take any more of my
doctor’s medicines, but to try something
else. I did not follow their recommendations
for a long time, as I thought the medic al
man who was attending me ought to know
the best remedies for my case. But at last
my patience would hold out no longer, and
I began taking mixtures that I saw adver-
tised, and gave them a fair trial; but they
did me more harm than good. My hopes
were altogether shattered. There seemed
to be nothing for it now but to drag out an
existence of misery, as everything I bad
taken failed to cure i re. But lam thankful
to say that a lady living opposite, who had
a delicate daughter, happened to haar of my
condition and paid me a visit. The good
soul told me that her daughter had been a
similar sufferer to myself, and the same
medicine that had cured her would cure me.
‘lf you only take Clements Tonic I’m sure
it will restore your health.’ m3' friend said.
I naturally felt very doubtful about it, after
all the stuff I Rad taken before. I though fc
I was incurable. But, at all events, I bought
a bottle of Clements Tonic, and by the time
it was finished my health had greatl \ r im •
proved. My nerves were much steadier,
and the sleep that came to me, and which I
needed so much, relieved the dreadful head-
aches from which I used to suffer so terribly.
My appetite was at once regained, and my
friends were delighted with my steady pro-
gress. I took five bottles altogether, and
during that time I grew stronger and
brighter every day. The nervousness dis-
appeared, and I speedily became as healthy
and happy as you see me now. I never
experience headaches, and my meals are
always a pleasure to me. I can go through
a good day’s work without feeling any ill
effects, and am so grateful to Clements
Tonic for my complete recovery that you
can publish my case in any form you like.”

The Great Smoke
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Here I am in London.
Is it really I,
Walking down High Holborn,
Unconcernedly ?
Circumscribing Fleet-street,
Wandering through the Strand.
Here I am in London.
“So I understand.
Here I am in London,
Someone just like me.
Not myself, of course not—
That could never be.
I shall never wander
Down a London street;
I am in the gums’ land
Where the curlews greet.
Who is this in London
Then, who wears my face,
Dons my clothes, and settles
In my little place ?
Who is this, for ever
Torn with joy and fear ?
Like me, but that my “ me”
Never could be here.
Who is this still figure
Sitting in St. Paul’s,
Gazing at the angels,
Drinking in the walls ?
Bending in the Abbey
To some golden name,
Kneeling to the pavement,
Kissing without shame ?
Ah, the Abbey’s Abbey,
And St. Paul’s St. Paul’s,
Though the halls of music
Be not Music Halls.
Yes, and London’s London,
Very big and spry,
But I cannot fathom
Who it is am I.
Some day it may happen
I may journey here —
Some day, in a distance
That will never near.
In the meantime, tell me.
Who I am to be :
Best pretend, since London
Seems to think I’m Me. ■
By the Indian Ocean,
Merry old Ceylon,
Mecca in the distance,
Suez coming on.
By the wicked Said lights
Flickering in the dawn,
By the white Crete Ranges
Where the gods were born.
On past pink Palermo,
Through the Golden Straits
purple twilight,
To the blazing gates
Of Stromboli’s island,
Flaming through its smok
Past the Rock and Fortress
Where a brave heart broke.
Five long weeks of water -
Time to think it out,
Time to know your Ego,
Find what you’re about.
And yet never, never
Can I think I’m 1,
London’s clean abstracted
My identity.
ENG.
LOUISE MACK.
Broughton, M.L.A. for King-division of
Sydney is the first legislator to indicate anv
regard for the Lord Howe Islanders. The island
is technically a portion of the King electorate, but
Jf.M? re alit y , so far as Parliament is concerned, a
disfranchised land. G. H. Reid, who nominally
represented it for nearly 20 years, never gave it a
moment s attention. Broughton purposes visiting
the island at an early date.
According to the latest available census
returns, there were in the cotton, woollen and
other textile factories of Britain 86,499 children
ot ten years and over, working half-time ; 88 696
Kin* coq , betv Y ee o 13 and 18 working full time;
610,608 females from 13 years upwards working full
time (ten hours per day), and 298,828 men—every
A l i oV ®rH 8 K U f lg . considered a man. Total
1,084,631 The half-time child of ten may begin
work at 6 a.m. and work five hours a dav Thi«
is Freetrade. Yet how beautiful it is'to hear
George Reid orating about the employment of
females in protectionist countries !
The threat of the Brisbane wharf authori-
ties to employ returned cohentingenters, should the
wharf-hands go on strike, recalls that during the
great ’BB maritime strike sis. Takapuna left Wel-
lington (M.L.) wharf with a stokehold crew of
coilege-boys. But by the time the vessel reachi d
the Heads her gallant stokers were all sea-sick
and it was with difficulty that she returned to her
berth.
Hicks, M.L.A. (Westralia), the gentleman
who says that he habitually attended nigger-
floggings, and who wants W.A. Parliament to
appoint a committee to find a verdict that the
treatment of the aborigines is all that can be
desired:—
With regard to the squatters .... they were law-
abiding and courageous, and he would ask 'if, amongst
men of that sort, one would expect to find cruel practices?
This kind of logic travels in a circle. First of all
(without waiting for any evidence), the squatter
isn’t the kind of man who would commit cruelties.
Then, as he isn’t the kind of man who would com-
mit cruelties, it is naturally absurd to suppose
that he would commit cruelties. Therefore, what’s
the good of saying any more about it ? Hicks also
“ quoted figures to show that black labor wpuld
actually cost twice as much as white labor”—
which is doubtless the reason why the squatter
uses it. The Bulletin could also quote figures to
show that Hicks’s uncle would have eight legs,
but it isn’t worth wasting space.

Concerning His Honor.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]

The firelight was playing strange tricks with two
faoes—one a woman’s, the other a man’s. They
sat motioiiiess, each following a train of thought

The Woman, a 9 always, Was the first to break

" I have a proposition to make to you. It in-

“You know, of course, that the no-confidence

Australian Spring.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The dewy freshness of the dawn, to breathing
blandness turning;
The clear translucent hours of noon, the sun so
softly burning;
The faint, suffusing sunset-bloom, than damsel’s
blush more tender;
The gentler shining of the stars,—all mark Spring’s
virgin splendor.
Some trees are speckled witli small buds, some
eagerly are greening,
Their rueful leisure left when they found out life’s
mighty meaning ;
The insect tribes are just astir, birds to and fro
are winging,
Or fluting snatches of the songs they’ll soon be
sweetly singing.
But, oh ! this shower of melody, this clear strain
floating o’er us,
The gush and cry of utter joy—love’s care-
abandoned chorus!
The subtle sirens sang to death the souls their
spells could capture,
But yon lark sings Spring’s bridal song to lift us
to life’s rapture !
The orchards, lulled by lazy bees and shallow
streamlets flowing,
Seem drifted o’er with snow warm-tinged in sun-
set’s rosy glowing ;
And, like some splendid passages in an unvaried
story,
The wattles in the bush burn here and there in
yellow glory.
N.S.W.
ARTHUR A. D. BAYLDON.
A Cornstalk Baby-Farm.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We read Zola’s latest, “Fruitfulness,” which
describes how the French factory-girl gives her
baby into the care of the motherly woman, who
leaves it, when-pay is in arrears, in some place.con-
venient for the hens to pick out its eyes and the
cow to give it its final quietus ; and then took train
for the Ashfield (Sydney) Cottage Home. This
refuge for foundlings is prettily situated in a net-
work of garden and field. There is a great variety
of babies of all ages up to three or four, and of all
shades, excluding absolute black. They treat the
casual woman - visitor quite as a relation,
and give her live snails, reels, pieces of doll,
grasshoppers, and other donations in a friendly
and engaging spirit. The elder children, besides
other toys, have a big toy-boat, large enough to
admit four or five, to sail a stormy sea on the side
verandah, and they also possess a garden to which
they attend during the summer. The matron
tells that their specialty is sunflowers.
How different are these bright-eyed youngsters
from the usual State-children of school age 1 An
institution for very young children, open thus to
the inspection of any passer-by, could never hide
cruelty for long. The voluntary grasp of a small
hand on the matron’s dress speaks for itself, for
no infant can pretend affection or confidence. But
the smile of the fourteen-year-old girl may hide,
and often does, immeasurable hatred.
Some of the children are fortunate enough to
have their own mothers in the establishment
waiting till positions as domestic servants are
obtained for them. Not infrequently the man
responsible for Jane’s entry into the ranks of un-
married mothers offers to take her back on legal
terms, and then Jane departs cheerfully, taking a
large, blue-eyed baby as her contribution towards
the new menage. Sometimes another man takes
Jane and accepts the infant in a philosophical
spirit. Australia, generally speaking, has a sen-
sible way of looking at Jane i. *' trouble,” and
nobody seems to wonder w aether sue is less like
the Beautiful Snow than other girls.
There are some mothers who ought to be here
and are not. One such, some years ago, stood by a
quiet pool in the neighbourhood and looked at it
till its stillness got into her very soul, and she took
her baby to its peace with her. It was her second
illegal child, and the rules of the establishment,
in this land that is crying out for population, are
against the admission of a woman who has twice
“burdened” the State with what it seriously
needs.
Sometimes baby’s grandma plays a disagreeable
part in the. drama that concerns the arrival of the
unauthorised infant. One such old dame, in a fit
of annoyance at her daughter’s ringless finger,
“planted” baby in a shed while she and its
mother were travelling through the district.
Having grimly kept her secret for years, grandma
died keeping it still, and it was only after years
of trouble that baby was identified at this Ashfield
Home, and restored to her ma and also to a brand-
new father. A half-caste Chinese girl of two
years is the result of the sale of a fourteen-year-
old girl by her mother to a Chinaman. It is an
unexpectedly attractive result, considering .the
parentage, but that doesn’t put the vendor in a
more pleasing light.
A few of the mothers outside the Home pay a
little towards baby’s support—never more than
five shillings a week. Otherwise the burden falls
on the State and the voluntary charity of the
public. Most of the children, however, are found-
lings. One small atom, with an Abraham Lincoln
smile, was picked up on the road, and another,
thoughtfully ticketed “Oliver,” was coaxed back
into life (after an all-night sitting under a hedge),
by hot water bottles on the incubating principle.
He looked ninety years of age when he came, but
has now got down to fifty-five.
A setting of crimson frocked youngsters on
emerald lawns makes a good effect on a landscape,
and it is a pity there is not room at Ashfield to
accommodate more than the sixty small occupants
who already reside there, for every little asset in
an empty continent requires banking in as careful
a way as possible, if the State is to be kept from
insolvency in human beings.

the custom of ladies being kissed by the King on

An old lady named Sullivan died the other
day atKingower (Vic.) at the reputed age of 109.
But, as Snazelle says, these fine old veterans are
such fine old dubiosities.
“Tweed": a Photograph.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Among my cherished photos, —among the true-eyed
comrades of the back-country, the kind-faced
women of the exile days, the good horses valued
not the least of all, race-groups and mining-groups
and polo-groups, a hundred casual snapshots of the
little world down-under—there is one of a prick-
eared Kelpie dog, that is very dear to me.
Tweed was something more than dog and a
little less than human. Most of us have owned a
faithful dog at one time or another, and valued
him ; but when he is also the pride of our voca-
tion and the mainstay of our income, the obliga-
tion is tenfold. And the drovers say that a good
dog is worth two men and a boy. I watch
1 weed’s photo., and dream and dream.
* • •
The warm glow of niid-day steals through the
thin-leaved gums by the creek, the blanket-bundles
and the big pack-saddle with its torn lining are
tossed upon the ground in negligent disorder, the
heap of gray ashes marks where the billy has
boiled ; and the drovers lounge upon their rolled
blankets and blow their smoke-rings to the blue.
On the great bank above them two thousand
wethers lie close in the warm dust or stand with
lowered heads trying to dodge the million flies ; in
the open glade the bay horse and the chestnut colt
that carries the pack stand head to tail in the sun,
flick-flicking the flies from one another and
stamping in the gray dust till the hobble-chains
ring. Down at the creek the big brown horse,
with a saddle on, drinks long and noisily, and then
trails away to the others with his foot through the
muddy bridle —rousing a lazy cheek-on-elbow,
oath from his master.
Away out on the top of the bank the hungry
wethers, lured by the disappearance of the sun
behind a cloud, string slowly into the scrub with
drooping heads and lpw calls to one another.
“ Tweed /” A pair, of. quick ears leap to atten-
tion and a black tail wags expectantly. “ Look
out for ’em ! ”
A rush in the dead leaves and he is gone, wide
to the right, two sharp barks away ahead of the
wanderers, and the mob comes rushing down hot-
footed to the camp; then Tweed comes limping
back, carrying one forefoot, and saving a hind one
whenever possible. “ Come here, old man ! ”
Tweed drops his tail and crouches up ; lie knows
that he came back far. too close to his sheep, and a
kelpie has a conscience, but—his feet were sore,
and the sharp stickshurt. And his master knows,
and makes allowances. He rolls Tweed over on
his back and examines the poor, blistered little
feet, raw and red with long padding up and down
on burning ground, and a little lump thickens in
his throat as he thinks of the pluck and devotion
that keeps the game little beggar going on the
hottest days, in spite of creeping age. For the
old dog is failing, there is no doubt about it. The
muzzle that lies so confidingly in his master’s
hand is pathetically gray, and there is a stiffness
in the near shoulder and a visible breakdown in
the near fore-leg.
“I won’t bring the old fellow out any more,”
says his master, meditatively; ‘ ‘ poor old chap,
he has earned a long spell, but he is so game one
forgets his age!” (Again that swelling in.the
throat.) “ D n those sheep, they’re off again !
Send Lady ! ”
WULL H. OGILVIE.
One of the most curious papers in Aus-
tralasia is the Canterbury Times (Christchurch,
M.L.). It is a liberal and progressive paper (in
M.L.), and speaks encouragingly of the democratic
policy which lifted that State out of the old-time
slough of despond. And it keeps an incurably and
hideously Tory correspondent on this side, who
curses every progressive idea, so that by its
showing everything that is good in Maoriland is
unspeakably bad in Australia. This gives the
paper a curiously piebald appearance, but the
editor doesn’t seem to mind.
Thus a returned S’African warrior :
Too much has been made of the undisciplined troops in
this war. People have grown to scoff at discipline and
training, and to hold up the Boers as examples of fighting
men. But if the Boers had been well generalled and dis-
ciplined, the British would have been glad enough to
patch up any sort of a peace a year ago. The Boer
generals should have rushed into Natal and Cape Colony
a month earlier, when three-fourths of the local popula-
tion, who are always ready to jump with the top dog,
would have been with them. And, as for discipline, the
Boers repeatedly held positions which were absolutely
impregnable, yet, after firing a few shots, they abandoned
them and rode off; whereas, had they been disciplined
soldiers, capably generalled, they would have held these
positions until the British had brought an overwhelming
force to carry the places with the bayonet at immense
cost. Then would have been the time for the Boers to
take horse —leaving English dead ten times more than
their own. The Australian civilian regiments were good
enough for the style of fighting provided by the Boers,
and the British Tommy Atkins had all his self-reliance
disciplined out of him. The men who had just the proper
amount of discipline to have coped with either sort of
fighting were those of the first Australian contingents,
made up from the local militia and volunteer forces.
Cut from an English paper :
There is at Hartlepool a peculiar industry carried on
nearly all the year, but more especially during the
months of spring, in which a number of women, varying
from 480 to 700, are employed. The industry is caused
by the importation of eggs into the Hartlepools, and the
work consists of sorting, pickling, washing, and breaking
of the eggs. The sorters separate the good eggs from
the bad and the large from the small, and receive
in wages from Is. 2d. to Is. 6d. per day. Picklers
receive anything from Is. 4d. to 2s. per day, those who
wash the eggs Is. Bd. per day, and the “breakers,” Is. 2d.
and Is. 4d. per day. Their hours are from 8 till 6 o’clock,
with an hour’s break for dinner. When the pressure of
work is very great, however, they have to work all night
as well. Many of the women appear to be widows, some
of them with families to maintain. The work is extreme-
ly disagreeable, and the conditions very unsatisfactory.
Some of the women are lifting sieves full of eggs in and
out of water all day long, which means that their feet
and clothing soon become saturated, in which condition
they have to work for the rest of the day. The
“ breakers” have to separate the yolks of eggs from the
whites, the latter being put into a cask—it is said, for
sale. During this progress the girls become covered with
a filthy slime. They work in what are termed “ dark
cells” —why, is not very clear. The odour from the bad
egg is simply awful, amounting to a foul stench, which,
of course, the women are compelled to inhale during
working hours. The “cells’’are badly ventilated, and
the sanitary conveniences inadequate. In one warehouse,
where from 300 to 400 women are employed in the rush
of the season, there are nominally three sanitary con-
veniences, but practically one convenience only is
available for over 300 women. There is no adequate
provision made for enabling the women to take their
food in decency and comfort. They have to go about
the docks and sit where they can, and get their dinner
in the best way they can, no matter what the weather
may be. There is, it is true, some provision made for
the warming of their dinners. One solitary old woman
is supposed to cook for 15 men, and to warm the cans
and dinners of over 300 girls ! It can be imagined how
such an arrangement works. The women are inevitably
of a low type, and, of course, the tendency of their work
is to drag them further and further down. Apparently
the “ cells ” in which they work are neither “ factories
nor workshops” within the meaning of the Factory
Acts, which ignore egg-workers.
